Vice President JD Vance Delivers Remarks at the Critical Minerals Ministerial

The White House(2.9M) ·41:37 ·42.9K views · 2026-02-04
> "as much as data centers and technology and all these incredible things that we're all working on matter, fundamentally, you still have an economy that runs on real things.
TL;DR The Trump administration is convening global partners to address critical mineral supply chain vulnerabilities, proposing a preferential trade zone with enforced price floors to stabilize markets, encourage investment, and diversify supply away from current geopolitical...

TL;DR The Trump administration is convening global partners to address critical mineral supply chain vulnerabilities, proposing a preferential trade zone with enforced price floors to stabilize markets, encourage investment, and diversify supply away from current geopolitical concentrations.

Current State of Critical Mineral Supply Chains

  • Market Failure & Concentration: The international market for critical minerals is failing, leading to brittle, exceptionally concentrated supply chains. This results in depressed asset and commodity prices driven by external forces, making consistent investment nearly impossible.
      • "Dumping" & Price Collapse: Projects (e.g., lithium, gallium mines) are frequently announced, only to have foreign supply flood the market overnight, collapsing prices and causing investors to withdraw. This leads to project abandonment across advanced and developing economies.
      • Investment Barriers: Capital cannot reach known deposits due to investor lack of confidence in market stability, despite efforts to streamline regulatory environments (e.g., in the US). This punishes strategic investment, diversification, and long-term planning.
  • Vulnerability & Dependence: Nations have become dependent on arrangements they did not choose or cannot control, facing vulnerability in access to materials vital for national defense, energy infrastructure, advanced manufacturing, and emerging technologies (e.g., AI).
      • Historical Neglect: The US, like many advanced economies, historically outsourced mining and manufacturing, believing it was less glamorous, leading to a loss of economic security and reliance on external supply chains.
      • Impact on National Security: Critical minerals are indispensable to national defense, powering missile defense systems and other vital infrastructure.

Trump Administration's Strategic Response

  • Re-industrialization Mandate: The administration aims to re-industrialize the United States, placing American workers at the center of economic policy.
      • Addressing Deficiencies: Unlike previous administrations, the Trump administration has actively identified critical deficiencies in global supply chains related to rare earth and critical minerals.
      • Dozens of Deals: In just one year, President Trump has struck numerous deals with domestic and foreign producers to ensure durable end-to-end supply chains for the US and its allies.
  • Mobilizing Public Financing: The US has mobilized public financing tools at an unprecedented scale.
      • $100 Billion Lending Authority: Provided to the Office of Strategic Capital for critical minerals.
      • Direct Stakes: Overturned old orthodoxies by taking direct stakes in high-value mining and processing companies.
      • New Smelters: Announced two primary smelters in the last month, the first since 1980, with one already fully funded.
  • Stockpiling & Reserves:
      • $2 Billion for National Defense Stockpile: Largest investment since the Cold War.
      • Project Vault: America's first domestic critical minerals stockpile, a $12 billion vehicle under the Export-Import Bank, for civilian economy protection.
  • Rebuilding Mining Ecosystem:
      • Permitting Reform: Reduced average mine development timelines from 29 years; more than 50 mining projects added to the federal permitting priority list; environmental impact statements now completed in less than a month.
      • Workforce Development: Acknowledging a shortage of mining engineers, the administration is prioritizing mining as a national development industry.

Proposed Preferential Trade Zone

  • Mechanism: The Trump administration proposes a concrete mechanism to stabilize the global critical minerals market: a preferential trade zone protected from external disruptions through enforceable price floors.
      • Reference Prices: Establish fair market value reference prices for critical minerals at each stage of production.
      • Adjustable Tariffs: For members, these reference prices will operate as a floor, maintained through adjustable tariffs to uphold pricing integrity.
      • Eliminating Undercutting: Aims to prevent foreign supply from flooding markets with cheap critical minerals, which undercuts domestic manufacturers and allows subsequent price manipulation.
  • Benefits for Members:
      • Consistent Prices: Guaranteed stable prices within the zone, regardless of global market fluctuations.
      • Diverse Production & Stable Investment: Fosters diverse production centers, stable investment conditions, and supply chains immune to external disruptions.
      • Secure Access: Provides a necessary foundation for private financing and secure access to critical mineral supplies during emergencies.
      • Economic Incentives: Creates incentives that reward investment and building in member countries, elevating miners, refiners, investors, and producers.
  • Collective Action: While the US market is large enough to create its own zone, the effort will be stronger and more competitive if built collaboratively. Many nations represented have already signed on, and others are encouraged to finalize agreements.
  • Agreement on Trade and Critical Minerals: Ambassador Greer (Jacob/Jameson), the US Trade Representative, will present further specifics about the agreement, focusing on detailed, substantive negotiations.

International Cooperation and Global Impact

  • Multilateral Effort: This initiative is an international, multilateral effort, aiming to secure a diverse and enduring global supply of critical minerals at affordable prices for all nations.
  • Shared Strategic Objective: To align trade policy, development finance, and diplomatic engagement towards diversifying global supply in the critical minerals market, strengthening partner countries in the process.
      • Agreements & Initiatives: Over the past year, the US has signed agreements, launched joint initiatives, and committed extensive resources with numerous governments across various regions and stages of the value chain.
      • Forge Initiative: Japan strongly supports the upcoming launch of "Forge" (Forum on Resource Geostrategic Engagement) as a key venue for supply chain diversification and policy coordination.
  • Lessons from History: Recalling the 1970s Washington Energy Conference which led to the International Energy Agency, today's ministerial aims to establish a similar framework for critical minerals, creating alternative supply sources and protecting all stages of production from non-market disruption.
  • Everyone Has a Role: Countries can contribute regardless of their mineral deposits; those without minerals can help refine them. Collective buying power of consumer nations can build a more resilient market.

Notable Quotes

"as much as data centers and technology and all these incredible things that we're all working on matter, fundamentally, you still have an economy that runs on real things. And there is no realer thing than oil. And I would add to that, there's no realer thing than critical minerals." — Vice President JD Vance "We were the we we were at the mercy of whoever controlled supply chains for these minerals. So my hope is that we are gathered here today as the first but important step to rectifying this mistake to bring together our collective talent for innovation when our advantage over rivals where our advantage over rivals has only grown and to apply it to bringing back manufacturing and reopening mines here in the United States but also in all the partner nations represented here today." — Secretary of State Marcos Rubio "Diversity as opposed to concentration is what makes us resilient. This has to be one of the our major guiding principles... No single country can overcome fragility of critical mineral supply and overd dependence on specific countries on its own." — State Minister Hiroyuki Horie of Japan "Our leadership have made mining a priority sector for our country again. We have a federal permitting priority list that has practically never been used for mining projects before. It's like concier service for federal permitting." — David Cippley, Senior Director for Global Supply Chains, National Security Council
Your Excellencies, distinguished guests, the Secretary of State of the United States, good morning. Welcome to Washington. We are glad you are here. This is a very important ministerial on a topic that I think we have been talking about quite a bit over the last year, year and a half, of great importance to all of our nations. We are very honored that all of you would join us here today. We have a great agenda and a lot of work ahead. This is an issue of incredible importance to this administration and it is one of the top priorities of this administration. I think it is a top priority for the world: how to diversify our supply chains, how to diversify our access to critical minerals to ensure that they are secure and safe. We will have more to say about that later on today. We wanted to begin today by demonstrating to you the high level of engagement and interest on the part of this administration on this topic by hearing from the Vice President, who himself has taken a personal role in being a leader on this front. And so, with no further ado, I would like to introduce my good friend and a very excellent person who is doing an incredible job in the role he is playing in this administration, the Vice President of the United States, JD Vance. Thank you. Good morning, everybody, and thank you all for being here. We are honored by the presence of so many world leaders, and it speaks to the importance of what Secretary Rubio and the team are working on to fix this critical minerals issue. I just wanted to let you know that we are honored by your presence and certainly grateful for you coming to Washington for this very important conversation. I know there are many important discussions to be had over the rest of the day, so I want to be relatively brief. First, let me say thanks to our incredible Secretary of State for putting this group together. Marcos is doing an incredible job. When he asked me to come and speak today, I said, "Man, this is your building. Why don't you come and give a speech?" And he said, "Because I've got five jobs and you've only got one." So I figured I might as well do Marco a solid, since he is also the archivist and the official White House florist and has a number of other jobs in addition to Secretary of State. So I will kick us off, and then he is going to take the reins from here. Let me say a few things about what we are doing and why this matters. I remember after our very wildly successful military operation in Venezuela, the President of the United States was talking with me and Marco and some of the other senior members of the cabinet. He was talking about how important it was that we ensured that the global economy had access to oil and gas many years in the future. And that, of course, is one of the reasons why we are interested in Venezuela: because it has such a critical importance in this all-important resource called oil. And as much as we talk about the modern economy, the digital economy, the high-tech economy, the President said something that was very, very important and I think should inform a lot of how we think about future growth, which is that as much as data centers and technology and all these incredible things that we are all working on matter, fundamentally, you still have an economy that runs on real things. And there is no realer thing than oil. I would add to that, there is no realer thing than critical minerals. I think a lot of us have learned the hard way in some ways over the last year how much our economies depend on these critical minerals. Every man and woman in this room understands what we are confronting together today. I say together, and that is very much something that is at the heart of Secretary Rubio's initiative and, as he said, at the heart of what we are trying to do all across the administration: recognize that this is something where our alliances and our friendships can really help one another. We are all on the same team. We are all rowing in the same direction. I believe that in this room, we have close to two-thirds of the world's GDP represented. So we have the capacity to make ourselves more independent, more self-reliant, and that is what we should be doing. Now, we know that today the international market for critical minerals is failing. It is failing to create domestic markets or dignified jobs for our labor forces. And it is failing to keep our nation safe. Supply chains remain brittle and exceptionally concentrated. Asset and commodity prices are persistently depressed, driven downward by forces beyond any individual country's control. How many times cumulatively have one of us or many of us in this room heard some variation of the story that I am about to tell? A lithium mine, a gallium recovery center, or similar project, is announced, sometimes with years in planning and financing nearly in place. Then overnight, foreign supply floods the market. Prices collapse and investors pull out. The project stalls and eventually dies on the vine. We have all seen it firsthand in all of our countries. The result is a global market where consistent investment is nearly impossible, and it will stay that way so long as prices are erratic and unpredictable. That is one of the things that we want to work on with this initiative. Let us make the prices more predictable and less erratic so that we can support the domestic supply chains and the investment that makes those supply chains possible. Across Europe and North America, there are dozens of mining and processing initiatives that have been suspended or completely abandoned because sustained price weakness makes financing impossible. Time after time, advanced economies with deep capital markets are finding that projects cannot clear costs. The same story plays out elsewhere around the world. In resource-rich developing economies, only a tiny fraction of global mining investment, including from nations, many of them in this room, is reaching the point of actual projects. We plan, we think about it, we even invest a little bit of money, we do the regulatory work, but so many of them die on the vine. Everyone knows that these deposits exist. We have mapped a whole lot of them, and we know they are ready to be mined and processed, but capital cannot reach them so long as investors lack confidence that those markets are going to remain stable for long enough to justify the long-term commitment. That is the problem that we confront. And even here in the United States, even with all the incredible things that President Trump and the entire administration has done to eliminate permits, to open up the regulatory environment, we have private financing that we have secured. But even in some cases, in the United States, some of these projects are struggling to attract investors. We see telltale signs of a market distorted beyond recognition. One that punishes strategic investment, one that punishes diversification, and one that punishes long-term planning. This is crazy. We should have the kind of global markets, the kind of domestic markets, that reward long-term planning, that reward strategic thinking. And that is exactly what we are trying to do. I think every single one of us represented in this room has become dependent on arrangements we did not choose, arrangements that we cannot control. We all face the same vulnerability: access to the things that protect our people and sustain our way of life. Everything from missile defense systems to energy infrastructure to advanced manufacturing to emerging technologies. The fundamental supply chains that support these industries sometimes can vanish in the blink of an eye without any control or influence from many of the countries in this room. We in the Trump administration believe it is the business of the government, of the people's government elected just over a year ago, to confront such problems, and we want to confront them together. I think that you are all here, at least I hope you are all here, because you all feel the exact same way. Our administration came into office last January, barely a year ago, with a clear mandate to re-industrialize the United States of America and return American workers to the center of our economic policy. And that is what we are doing. I want to tell you a little story about one of the very first meetings I had with our Secretary of Commerce and the Secretary of the Treasury, two great people, two great business leaders. I remember saying, "Look, we have our views of the Biden administration. We are not particularly fans, as you may have guessed. Setting that to the side, surely the Biden administration put together a list of the critical industries, the critical technologies, the critical manufacturing facilities that are supported by these rare earth minerals, these critical minerals. We may think it is bad work. We may think the people who put it together were not particularly wise, but surely that gives us some foundation to build on." And I was shocked when our Secretary of Commerce and Secretary of the Treasury came back to me and said, "Actually, we have never done this. The last four years, the B administration never sat down and tried to understand what the critical deficiencies in world supply chains are." That is crazy. It is insane that we ever got to this point. But in just one year, the President of the United States has struck dozens of deals to fix that problem. Domestic and foreign producers are both included in this. We are trying to ensure durable end-to-end supply chains for our country, but also for many of our friends. We have mobilized public financing tools at an unprecedented scale, providing up to $100 billion in lending authority for critical minerals to the Office of Strategic Capital. Marco is now a venture capitalist, too. He has got many jobs. We have overturned old orthodoxies and taken direct stakes in high-value mining and processing companies. The United States had not built a primary smelter in this country since 1980, before I was born, four years before I was born. In the last month alone, we have announced two, one of which is already fully funded. And on Monday, the Trump administration announced Project Vault, America's first ever domestic critical minerals stockpile. With these moves, we intend to build an ironclad network of new industrial supply chains to span the entire nation. We are going to create good-paying jobs, skilled jobs for the American labor force. Those people, those great workers, are going to be securing a new era of prosperity for American families and I believe for many people all over the world. But we seek to make sure our friends and our allies are part of this and that you all are covered as well. Our goal here, and the reason we are doing this conference, is to align trade policy, development, finance, and diplomatic engagement towards a shared strategic objective. That objective is very simple: diversifying global supply and the critical minerals market while strengthening the partner countries who help all of us in this shared effort. Over the past year, our administration has signed agreements. We have launched many joint initiatives and we have committed extensive resources with many of you in this room, with many governments, and our work extends across disparate regions and all stages of the value chain. We have convened this ministerial in an effort to accelerate that shared work. So this morning, the Trump administration is proposing a concrete mechanism to return the global critical minerals market to a healthier, more competitive state: a preferential trade zone for critical minerals protected from external disruptions through enforceable price floors. We will establish reference prices for critical minerals at each stage of production, pricing that reflects real-world fair market value. And for members of the preferential zone, these reference prices will operate as a floor maintained through adjustable tariffs to uphold pricing integrity. We want to eliminate that problem of people flooding into our markets with cheap critical minerals to undercut our domestic manufacturers because we know that as soon as they have undercut our domestic manufacturers, they leave the market, and the people who undercut them then jack up the price to a completely unfair level. We are going to fix that problem together. We want members to form a trading bloc among allies and partners, one that guarantees American access to American industrial might while also expanding production across the entire zone. The benefits will be immediate and durable, regardless of how much material flows into the global market. Prices within the preferential trade zone will remain consistent over time. Our goal within that zone is to create diverse centers of production, stable investment conditions, and supply chains that are immune to the kind of external disruptions that we have already talked about. For those of you who join, we offer you a necessary foundation for private financing and secure access to the critical mineral supplies your nation would require in an emergency or some other contingency. By regulating imports to preserve free and fair competition within the preferential trading zone, we will elevate our nation's miners and refiners, our investors, and our producers alike. We are all on the same team, and we need to create the economic incentives that reward people for investing and building in our countries. Membership will be vital for developing economies seeking to expand mining capacity, and it will be just as important for the advanced economies that rely on these materials to sustain their advanced industries, to sustain their growth and security. I want to be clear with every person in this room: while we believe America's market is large enough to create its own critical minerals trading zone, this entire effort will be stronger and far more competitive if we build it together. So, I am pleased that so many of you here today have already signed on to this plan. Some of you have not. For those not yet there, we hope that today's discussions will encourage you to finalize those agreements as quickly as possible. I know you are going to be hearing from a lot of others in our administration, including our great Secretary Rubio, the greatest Secretary of State the United States has ever had, and other senior administrators in our team. I know that Ambassador Greer, our great trade representative, will present further specifics about what he will be negotiating, what we are calling the agreement on trade and critical minerals. Knowing Jameson, I know it will be a very productive session and I know it will be a very detailed session. As all of you who know Jameson well, he is a guy who gets into the details because he knows that is where the substance of an agreement can really be made. I want to close, but before I do, let me just reiterate. I think all of us are friends. We have all spent many years describing this problem. Before I was ever in politics, I would hear politicians and global leaders talk about this problem. For too many of those years, we talked about it, but we refused to actually solve it. The framework before us is a change. It is a practical and enforceable approach to solving the problem, not just complaining about it. Many of you have helped us design it, and we are, of course, constructing it together in real time. So to those of you still on the fence, I say let us move. Let us move together. Before you is a chance to lead, to secure your nation's ability to build, to produce, to defend itself. What is before all of us is an opportunity at self-reliance, that we never have to rely on anybody else except for each other for the critical minerals necessary to sustain our industries and to sustain growth. So I propose that we come together and we shape a market worthy of the great men and women of our countries who depend on that market. Again, I want to thank you all for being here. I am honored by your presence. The President sends his very best regards, and I look forward to everything that we are going to accomplish together. God bless you all and have fun today. Thank you, Mr. Vice President. As you saw, I have to put my own sign here, too. It is another job I have. I am in charge of the signage. Thank you all for joining us. I can tell you how honored we truly are that all of you would make the trip to be a part of this on such an important topic. I want to thank the Vice President for coming this morning. I thought it was important you hear from him because it is a priority for this administration of the highest order, as it is, I know, a priority for many of our partners. Our hope is to use both this forum and our position here today to serve as a convenor on what really is an international problem, situation, or issue that needs multilateral international solutions. I do not need to explain to anybody here that critical minerals are vital to the devices that we use every single day. They power our infrastructure, our industry, and our national defense, which is something that is not talked about enough, but it is a key part of that. So, our goal is to have a global market that is secure, a global supply that is enduring and is available to everyone, every nation, at an affordable price. That is a top priority for this administration. I want to talk briefly about our policies here domestically, primarily to serve as an example or a reminder of what a priority we give this topic. President Trump, from day one in office, has made clear that economic security is national security. So, for example, following in March, he issued an executive order to accelerate permitting reform to increase domestic mining and to build reliable supply chains both at home and abroad. We feel all of you, our friends and partners, stand to benefit from the secure supply chains we want to be a big part of building. In October alone, the United States secured over $10 billion in critical mineral agreements across five countries. Our friends and partners stand. In December, for example, we convened stakeholders from around the world at the PAC Silica Summit. Thank you, Jacob, for doing that. It is launching a partnership dedicated to building a resilient silicon supply chain capable of powering a new era of global prosperity. PAC Silica and artificial intelligence may be the future, but the future will never come to pass. None of these things are going to be possible unless we rebuild the factories and reopen the mines upon which these technologies depend. I want to use a case in point from our own history to highlight this. The United States used to produce its own critical minerals and derivative products like rare earth magnets. Back in 1949, miners in Mountain Pass, California, discovered one of the world's richest mineral deposits. By 1952, the United States was mining rare earths there. That discovery sparked a revolution. American scientists and engineers, alongside innovators from many of the countries that are here today, rushed to discover new applications for these minerals. With these new technologies ushered in the jet age, we ushered in the space age. We ushered in the computer age. And then we became blinded, blinded by the potential of the technologies those metals enabled. But we neglected their importance. Mining is less glamorous than building computers. It is less glamorous than building cars or airplanes. But building computers and cars and airplanes is less glamorous than designing them. As we embraced what was new and glamorous, we outsourced what seemed old and unfashionable. We allowed, for example, Mountain Pass, and with it most of America's critical mineral industry, to wither and to die so that we could focus on manufacturing. Then we outsourced the manufacturing. I know this is a story I am telling, but it is a story many of the advanced economies represented here today understand well. We outsourced the manufacturing so we could focus on designing these goods. And then one day we woke up and we realized we had outsourced our economic security and our very future. We were at the mercy of whoever controlled supply chains for these minerals. My hope is that we are gathered here today as the first but important step to rectifying this mistake, to bring together our collective talent for innovation where our advantage over rivals has only grown, and to apply it to bringing back manufacturing and reopening mines here in the United States, but also in all the partner nations represented here today. I think it is interesting that about 50 years ago, actually in this very room, ministers from around the world joined then Secretary of State Henry Kissinger for the Washington Energy Conference. That meeting took place during a moment where global energy supplies were concentrated, where markets were distorted, and access to a single critical resource, at that time of course being oil, had become a tool of political pressure. That is the world 50 years ago. That meeting led directly to the creation of the International Energy Agency to coordinate among like-minded partners and secure access to energy commodities. And so today, we return 50 years later in the hopes of advancing a bilateral critical minerals framework agreement to the same end on that front. These frameworks are designed to create alternative sources of supply and to protect every single stage of production, from mining to refining and processing to manufacturing, protecting them from non-market disruption. They will provide platforms for coordinating policies that ensure all of our citizens can enjoy abundant and affordable access to these critical minerals. We stand ready and intend to work with each country here today to find a specialized role that you can play. If you do not have minerals, you can help refine them. The countries gathered here include the largest consumers of critical mineral products, who together collectively have the buying power to build a more resilient and diverse global market. Everyone here has a role to play, and that is why we are so grateful for you coming and being a part of this gathering that I hope will lead to not just more gatherings, but action. The United States stands ready alongside each of you as we enter this new age of shared prosperity and security. I want to end by saying that while we intend to play a key role and an important role, and to use the size and influence of our markets to drive this, this is an international multilateral effort and only works. You can only have diversity and choice in these markets if it is broadly adopted across multiple countries. So this initiative here today may be one that started with us, but this is not solely an American initiative. This must be an international global initiative with like-minded countries who all have one thing in common: we want to see a diverse supply of critical minerals and secure and resilient supply chains across the world so all of our economies can prosper without ever having these things be susceptible to being used as leverage against us, or any other disruption that could come to the market that would undermine our collective economic security. Thank you all for being a part of this today. We appreciate it very much. Let me now turn as we will begin our program here. Minister Hy, I think you are going to start off. Thank you very much for being here today. Thank you all for being a part of this today. Thank you very much, Mr. Secretary, Your Excellencies, and distinguished guests. I am Hy, State Minister for Foreign Affairs of Japan. It is my great honor to address you on behalf of the government of Japan regarding one of the most pressing and crucial issues we collectively face today: critical mineral supply chains. May I begin with thanking our host? I would like to express my sincere appreciation for the decisive leadership of the United States in convening today's critical minerals ministerial meeting. I first would like to echo the remarks by Vice President Vance and Secretary Rubio. Japan shares a deep sense of concern and urgency about the risk of disruptions to critical mineral supply chains. Sitting at the very upstream end of supply chains and being used across a wide range of industries, critical minerals and their stable supply are indispensable to the sustainable development of the global economy. That is precisely why Japan, under Prime Minister Takah Ichi's leadership, has redoubled its efforts to diversify sources of supply for critical minerals. Recently, Japan designated critical minerals as one of the priority areas under the new initiative of promoting bold strategic investment to enhance Japan's resilience and achieve robust economic growth. Amid rising geopolitical risks, Japan will accelerate efforts to secure upstream sources. At the same time, we will take flexible measures downstream, such as diversifying procurement sources in cooperation with like-minded partners who are here today. In fact, in recent years, we have already witnessed firsthand supply chain disruptions. Even in recent months, this challenge has further deteriorated. Against such backdrop, we have come to understand that we are in the same boat, as any supply chain disruption would bring significant impact on the global economy. We should work together to address this challenge. To prevent such supply chain disruptions, the key is to diversify supply sources, including the refining and processing of geographically concentrated critical minerals. To do so, it requires a collective action. We must seize this opportunity to advance cooperation among like-minded countries and partners like us. With that in mind, I am looking forward to the launch of FORGE, the Forum on Resource Geostrategic Engagement, in the next session. Japan strongly believes that FORGE will become an important venue and a vehicle for us to focus on supply chain diversification and ensure policy coordination. Japan stands ready to actively contribute to discussions to further deepen collaboration with partners and to ensure the effective implementation of this initiative. So, how we should move forward from here on the supply side? Diversification is essential. Diversity, as opposed to concentration, is what makes us resilient. This has to be one of our major guiding principles. In this context, I would like to take a moment to briefly talk about Japan's efforts. To date, Japan has been pursuing various projects in cooperation with the United States as well as with stakeholders across the globe. In this regard, diversification of supply sources and enhancement of supply chain resilience has been Japan's top priority for many years, and we have taken policy measures accordingly. Back in 2022, Japan enacted the first ever economic security promotion act and created new programs with a focus on critical minerals. Just as importantly, Japan has been implementing public-private support measures, including expanding Juggbeck's investment framework, increasing allowable investment ratios, and allocating a total budget of $3.5 billion US. Around this table, there are many partners with enormous potential for the production and refining of rare earth elements, as well as other significant critical mineral sources: Angola, Argentina, Australia, Bolivia, Brazil, Canada, the Democratic Republic of the Congo, France. The list goes on and on. Japan is determined to seek robust partnership with you all. As geopolitical risks continue to rise, we will redouble our efforts to secure alternative supply sources and to build more resilient, reliable, and secure global supply chains. Diversity only comes from a collective action. No single country can overcome the fragility of critical mineral supply and over-dependence on specific countries on its own. With the launch of FORGE, now it is time for action. On the demand side, it is imperative that we avoid the situation in which critical minerals and their derivatives, produced through non-market-based policies or practices, crowd out products with distorted price competitiveness and unfairly monopolize the global market. Japan will engage in discussions with like-minded countries, including the United States, the European Union, and others present here, in a positive and constructive manner, and will work to build an effective mechanism with industrial engagement. In this regard, Japan highly appreciates the US for its decisive leadership. Distinguished colleagues, as we gather today, we are clear-eyed about what our mission is and what our challenges are. Together, we can build on our advantages and make our critical mineral supply chains more resilient. Japan has unwavering determination to work with you as your trusted partner on every aspect: demand and supply, mining and refining, upstream and downstream. Japan is ready to enhance coordination and collaboration with you all. Together, let us make a meaningful difference. In closing, I thank you for your kind attention. I very much look forward to the discussion today. Thank you so much. Thank you, Secretary Rubio, for your truly historic leadership on this critical issue, and thank you, State Minister Hy and Senior Director Copley, for kicking off our ministerial. We are currently serving in the most historic administration in over a century, one that recognizes that a supply chain responsive to national security realities is a prerequisite to long-term economic growth. The decisions we make today about who we partner with are multi-decade decisions. We believe our partners will capture economic growth and value at all layers of the supply chain stack. The AI revolution we are living through is leading to record historic demand for everything from copper to cobalt, nickel, zinc, aluminum, silicon, and virtually every element on the periodic table. We are also seeing surging demand for products downstream of the supply chain, including manufactured products like smartphones, chips, telecom infrastructure, industrial robots, machinery, and much more. My friends, the pie is rapidly expanding, and the United States accounts for 30% of global consumption. We are taking steps to narrow the gap between what we produce and what we consume. But in order to merely keep pace with global demand, in the US and abroad, we need to surge production capacity at home as well as in partner countries. David sits at the very center of this mission. His work is foundational to the strategic resilience of our country. He is tasked with a monumental challenge of derisking our critical mineral dependencies, ensuring that the United States and our allies remain unassailable in the face of systemic competition. David brings a level of rigor and foresight that is essential for our department's mission and for the country at large. Please join me in welcoming David Copley. Thank you, Jacob, for that. Thank you all for being here today. Thank you to the Vice President for his remarks and for his leadership. Thank you, Secretary Rubio, for hosting. And thank you to President Trump for his visionary and forward-leaning leadership on the critical minerals issue. The President understands the need to secure the foundational elements of the modern economy, and he has prioritized this issue as a matter of national security and economic prosperity. I am going to speak with you all today to provide an overview about what we are doing in the United States to fix the critical minerals problem. We know this is an issue that will require a global solution and teamwork. Of the 57 minerals on our critical minerals list, over a dozen are not even mined in the United States. So this is truly a global challenge. In the United States for a number of decades, we have neglected our mining sector and frankly been happy to outsource mining activities to others around the world. We have had some of the worst mine development timelines in the world. In the United States, 29 years to build an average mine because our permitting framework and our litigation framework have been so onerous. Mining in the United States has been perceived as a dirty, old-world industry. We have not had enough young people choosing mining as a career. We only graduate something like 200 to 250 mining engineers annually. Not good for a country of 340 million people facing a critical problem. But I am proud to say that under President Trump's leadership, we in the United States have done a complete 180 and again made mining a priority industry for our national development. Because as all of you hopefully understand, minerals are the elemental building blocks of everything we need for our manufacturing sector as we re-industrialize our country. What are we doing in the United States to fix the problem? Broadly, four key initiatives. We are investing in mining projects. We are stockpiling minerals. We are going to protect our mining companies. And we are rebuilding our mining ecosystem. To start, we are investing heavily in mining-specific projects like recent deals with MP Materials, Lithium Americas, Korea's Inc., Modern, and many, many others. I believe over the next few years, the United States government, between debt and equity, will deploy hundreds of billions in capital into the mining sector to get projects going. We are doing things the American government has never done before, like equity investing, so we can get deals to pencil quickly and move forward. We have created public-private minerals investment funds like our critical minerals consortium with Orion Resources and another partnership with TechMet. Investment is an area of great potential for collaboration with your governments as we look to jointly develop mining projects with partners around the world. Second, we have begun stockpiling minerals. President Trump and Vice President Vance convinced Congress to give us $2 billion over the summer to stockpile minerals in our national defense stockpile. That is the largest investment we have made in our defense stockpile since the Cold War ended. And just two days ago, the President announced Project Vault, the creation of a national strategic critical minerals reserve, a $12 billion vehicle under our Export-Import Bank. This is the first time in the history of the United States we have ever stockpiled minerals for our civilian economy to protect our commercial industry and insulate them from the painful effects of supply shocks. Third, we are going to protect our mining companies. We know the mining industry has a problem with commodity pricing caused by strategic dumping and overproduction. Returns in the mining sector have not been good enough over the last 30 years, at least partially due to this problem. This is a key part of what we want to talk to you all about today. The current state of affairs, in which commodity prices are kept low for geostrategic reasons through overproduction, is damaging to minerals producing nations by reducing the value of your assets and your tax receipts, and it is equally damaging to consuming nations by harming your national and economic security. The current system does not make sense for any of us. But with coordinated global action as described by the Vice President, we can fix this problem in a way that will be better for both producers and consumers of minerals. Lastly, we are fixing our mining ecosystem in the United States. I mentioned earlier in my remarks that S&P reported 29-year timelines to develop an average mine in the United States. This is completely unacceptable to President Trump and Vice President Vance and will not continue under their watch. Our leadership has made mining a priority sector for our country again. We have a federal permitting priority list that has practically never been used for mining projects before. It is like concierge service for federal permitting. In just the last few months, we have added more than 50 projects to this list. We have torn apart and rewritten our federal regulations and we can now get full environmental impact statements done, the most burdensome part of our permitting process, in less than a month. Our goal is that the United States again becomes a very attractive place for the mining sector because we intend to mine here and we intend to process minerals here as well. Four key initiatives: we are investing, we are stockpiling, we are going to protect our mining companies, and we are fixing our mining ecosystem, because this industry is so important to our national development, as I know it is to your countries as well. Most importantly, under President Trump's leadership, we are no longer standing around admiring the problem. We are not spending our time writing 200-page book reports about how important critical minerals are. We have a plan and we are focused on project execution, getting deals done, getting companies the permits, stockpiling minerals, and hopefully moving forward with all of you, our international partners, to protect our mining companies and to rebuild global mining in a fair and balanced way. We very much appreciate your time and the distances you travel to be with us here in Washington. We look forward to the discussion, and most importantly, we look forward to working with all of you to make the global mining industry great again. Thank you. Thank you, Secretary Rubio, for your truly historic leadership on this critical issue, and thank you, State Minister Hy and Senior Director Copley, for kicking off our ministerial. I would like to ask the press to depart the room before we begin plenary session one. Thank you.

Tesla and Waymo execs testify before Senate panel on the future of self-driving cars — 2/4/26

CNBC Television(3.3M) ·132:42 ·33.0K views · 2026-02-04
The Senate hearing underscored a bipartisan consensus for a federal framework for autonomous vehicles (AVs) to enhance safety, promote American innovation, and secure jobs, while addressing critical concerns around federal oversight, data privacy, li...
TL;DR The Senate hearing underscored a bipartisan consensus for a federal framework for autonomous vehicles (AVs) to enhance safety, promote American innovation, and secure jobs, while addressing critical concerns around federal oversight, data privacy, liability, and ensuring...

TL;DR

The Senate hearing underscored a bipartisan consensus for a federal framework for autonomous vehicles (AVs) to enhance safety, promote American innovation, and secure jobs, while addressing critical concerns around federal oversight, data privacy, liability, and ensuring equitable access and workforce protection.

The Urgent Need for a Federal AV Framework

  • Current State: Autonomous vehicles are already providing mobility, improving safety, and offering potential to save lives and reduce traffic, but operate within a fragmented landscape of state laws.
  • Dangers of Inaction: Congress's failure to establish a clear federal framework is deemed unsafe, risking an undermined safety and innovation ecosystem and weakening American competitiveness against global rivals like China.
  • Industry Call to Action: Representatives from Tesla, Waymo, and the Autonomous Vehicle Industry Association (AVIA) strongly advocate for national standards to provide consistency, certainty, and to enable the National Highway Traffic Safety Administration (NHTSA) to become a global standard-setter for AV development.
  • Proposed Components (AVIA CEO Jeff Farrah):
      • Safety Case & Competencies: Require the Department of Transportation (DOT) to mandate AV manufacturers develop a safety case and abide by a series of driving competencies.
      • National Data Repository: Establish a national AV safety data repository for NHTSA to collect and share vital incident information with state regulatory partners.
      • Modernize Standards: Evolve existing federal motor vehicle safety standards (FMVSS) that were designed for human-driven vehicles to accommodate next-generation AV designs, including those with enhanced accessibility.

Autonomous Vehicles: Safety Promise vs. Real-World Challenges

  • Life-Saving Potential: AVs hold significant promise to reduce the nearly 40,000 annual US road fatalities by removing human error caused by impaired, distracted, or fatigued driving.
      • Waymo Data: Dr. Mauricio Peña, Waymo's Chief Safety Officer, stated their technology is 10 times less likely to be involved in serious injury crashes and 12 times less likely to be involved in pedestrian injury crashes compared to human drivers in their operating areas.
      • Tesla Perspective: Lars Moravy, Tesla's VP of Vehicle Engineering, highlighted that AV systems do not suffer from human limitations (e.g., sleeping, blinking, tiredness), offering the "next big jump" in reducing accident rates.
  • Oversight Concerns & Incidents:
      • NHTSA Under-resourcing: Senator Maria Cantwell criticized the "risk of letting companies beta test on our roads with no guard rails," citing NHTSA reports linking Tesla's Autopilot to fatal crashes and the agency's severe under-resourcing, which leads to fewer investigations and less enforcement.
      • Waymo Incident Responses: Dr. Peña explained Waymo's methodical process for evaluating incidents, such as those involving school buses in Austin or a child in Santa Monica, noting software updates, continuous learning, and real-time mitigation to improve performance.
  • Sensor Redundancy & Operational Design Domains (ODDs):
      • Waymo Approach: Waymo utilizes a multi-sensor suite (LiDAR, radar, cameras) with continuous, real-time calibration and self-correction, and restricts its fully autonomous vehicles to defined ODDs with technological safeguards.
      • Tesla Approach: Tesla's "Full Self-Driving supervised" (an L2 system) operates on a generalized solution across all public roads, a choice that Senator Ed Markey called "outrageous" given its involvement in dozens of deaths, and in contrast to Waymo's and other automakers' practice of restricting to safe, pre-mapped ODDs. Tesla maintains redundancy through its nine independently wired cameras.
  • Liability: Lars Moravy confirmed that Tesla would accept liability for accidents caused by a software error in its autonomous driving system. Dr. Peña, for Waymo, deferred on questions regarding binding arbitration clauses for users.

Economic Impact, Jobs, and Global Leadership

  • Job Creation: The AV industry is projected to create new, high-skilled American jobs across various sectors, including engineering, software development, manufacturing, safety, and fleet management.
      • Tesla Investment: Tesla has invested over $2 billion in a purpose-built AV line in Texas, projected to create up to 5,000 local jobs directly, with a potential 10x multiplier in the supply base.
      • Workforce Coexistence: The AVIA suggests a hybrid future for industries like trucking, where autonomous trucks coexist with human drivers performing different functions, creating new opportunities rather than outright replacement.
  • US vs. China Competition: A critical race for global AV leadership is underway, with China aggressively investing in its own national champions. Failure to establish a robust federal framework risks the US ceding its technological and manufacturing dominance, allowing Chinese companies to set global standards and potentially leverage AVs as "roving sensors" for strategic advantage.
  • US Manufacturing & Supply Chains:
      • Tesla's Commitment: Tesla emphasizes its commitment to US manufacturing, with over 95% of its parts sourced from North America and all US-sold vehicles manufactured domestically.
      • Waymo's Sourcing: Waymo designs and builds its autonomous driving system in the US, but installs it onto various vehicle platforms, including potential future use of cars from a Chinese-owned company (Geely subsidiary), sparking concerns about undermining US interests.

Data Privacy, Community Empowerment, and Accessibility

  • Data Collection & Privacy: Both Tesla and Waymo detailed strong internal policies, including opt-in data collection, aggregation, and anonymization, to protect user privacy. Data is primarily collected to improve the autonomous driving systems.
      • Law Enforcement: Companies stated they would only comply with valid legal orders for data, retaining the right to object to overly broad requests. Concerns were raised regarding the vast amount of data AVs can collect and the industry's readiness for future privacy legislation and data breach scenarios.
  • Local Government Role: Professor Bryant Walker Smith highlighted the essential expertise of local governments and first responders who often manage AV incidents. He cautioned against federal preemption that could disempower communities and create litigation rather than certainty, advocating for empowering local authorities with resources.
  • Remote Assistance Oversight: Waymo employs human remote assistance operators (dubbed "live agents") who provide guidance to AVs in complex scenarios. Senator Markey expressed strong disapproval that some of these critical safety-related operators are located overseas, specifically in the Philippines, citing potential cybersecurity risks and the outsourcing of American jobs.
  • Accessibility for People with Disabilities: While AVs offer immense potential for independent mobility for individuals with disabilities, current offerings from major AV developers are limited.
      • Tesla's Gap: Tesla currently offers no factory-produced wheelchair-accessible models.
      • Waymo's Progress: Waymo offers wheelchair-accessible options, but they are not yet fully autonomous, as the company seeks an OEM partner that can meet its safety requirements for an accessible, fully autonomous vehicle.

Notable Quotes

"Without federal oversight, we risk a fragmented patchwork of state laws that could undermine safety, innovation, and American competitiveness." — *Senator Ted Cruz* "We have seen the risk of letting companies beta test on our roads with no guard rails. In 2024, a report from Nitsa linked Tesla's autopilot to hundreds of crashes, including at least 13 fatal crashes and many more injuries." — *Senator Maria Cantwell* "AVs present an opportunity to significantly improve safety on the road and enhance accessibility to transportation for all Americans. AVs also provide an opportunity for the US to create jobs and reassert its dominance in advanced manufacturing for the 21st century." — *Lars Moravy, Vice President of Vehicle Engineering at Tesla* "Autonomous vehicles are not just another application of AI. They are the exemplar of what AI becomes when it moves from the digital to the physical world. What federal policy makers decide to do or not do on AVs will send a clear message about America's commitment to AI leadership." — *Jeff Farrah, CEO of Autonomous Vehicle Industry Association* "No one likes a backseat driver, but a transatlantic backseat driver is downright dangerous in our country." — *Senator Ed Markey*
Good morning. The Senate Committee on Commerce, Science, and Transportation will come to order. America is at a crossroads in transportation policy. Autonomous vehicles, or AVs for short, are no longer theoretical. Like it or not, they are here and they will be central to the future of roadways. AVs are now providing mobility to the disabled, improving auto safety, and advancing technologies that have the potential to save millions of wasted hours in traffic, and even more importantly, to save tens of thousands of American lives. Yet, Congress has failed to establish a clear federal framework to govern AV deployment. That inaction is no longer neutral. It is unsafe. Without federal oversight, we risk a fragmented patchwork of state laws that could undermine safety, innovation, and American competitiveness. As we consider a surface transportation reauthorization bill, it is imperative that Congress act now to create a national standard for AVs. Even as some states seemingly wish to put up metaphorical roadblocks to AVs, other states are smartly getting into the fast lane. My home state of Texas understands that clear rules enable growth, investment, and safety. In Texas, AVs are moving freight on some highways and operating as taxis in cities like Austin. But AVs can't just stop at Texas's border. We should be clear about the resistance to federal action. Some insurance interests and advocacy groups argue against national standards, often under the banner of safety, while opposing reforms that would modernize our system. The vast majority of automobile accidents and crashes are from human error. But lower accident rates challenge business models built on high premiums driven by preventable collisions. The reality is this: the overwhelming causes of roadway fatalities today are drunk driving and distracted driving. Autonomous technologies never drive drunk. Autonomous technologies don't text while driving. They don't change the radio station. They don't drop their sunglasses and look for them while driving down the freeway. Expanding AV deployment offers real measurable opportunities to reduce these deadly behaviors and to improve safety on our highways. If we want to save lives and avoid tragedy for almost 40,000 families each year, we don't need lawmakers saddling automakers with expensive junk mandates that make little to no real difference. Instead, we should follow the data, follow the evidence, which increasingly shows advanced AVs reduce crashes and prevent serious injuries. We need a consistent federal framework to ensure uniform safety standards, liability clarity, and consumer confidence. Uncertainty benefits no one, not drivers, not manufacturers, and not state and local officials responsible for public safety. Some, but not all, insurers are responding to the data. Companies like Lemonade have lower premiums for vehicles using full self-driving mode, reflecting growing confidence and growing evidence that these systems reduce risk. The numbers confirm what many already see: technology designed to reduce human error makes roads safer. Let's also acknowledge that the technological progress from AVs won't endanger jobs; it will ultimately create them. AVs require American engineers, software developers, safety technicians, mechanics, manufacturing workers, and infrastructure specialists. AV deployment can support new high-skilled jobs built in the United States by American workers. Moreover, AVs can make people more productive and traffic more bearable. If Congress fails to act, we're not going to stop innovation; we'll simply push it elsewhere. China is moving aggressively to deploy autonomous transportation at scale. The technologies at stake were developed in the United States. They can be built by American workers, and they should be governed by American safety standards. A patchwork approach puts that leadership at risk. Finally, let me be clear about one other thing. The American consumer will decide what they choose to drive. No one is, and no one should be, mandating AVs. This isn't like Biden's de facto mandate killing the gas-powered car. But government inaction should not deny consumers access to choose safer options. A federal framework for autonomous vehicles is not about picking winners. It's about setting clear rules, improving safety, creating American jobs, and ensuring that states like Texas can continue to lead. Surface reauthorization is the moment for Congress to act. I'll now turn to Ranking Member Cantwell for her remarks. Thank you, Mr. Chairman. I certainly want to get the surface transportation bill done, and I've mentioned it as my number one priority since you took over the committee. So I hope we will get to that. I don't know whether this kind of legislation is the appropriate place for that. But I do know that nearly 40,000 people die on the roads each year. Thankfully, we have seen how innovation in vehicle technology can make human drivers safer on the roads. For instance, automatic emergency braking technology has been shown to reduce injuries from rear collisions by 60%. These technologies are saving lives today. Fully autonomous vehicles offer the potential to reduce crashes on roads, but we have seen the risk of letting companies beta test on our roads with no guardrails. In 2024, a report from NHTSA linked Tesla's Autopilot to hundreds of crashes, including at least 13 fatal crashes and many more injuries. Safety advocates have linked 65 fatalities to Tesla's automated technologies. These tragedies have occurred in my state. In April 2024, Jeffrey Nissen from Stanwood, Washington, was killed when Tesla's Autopilot system failed to recognize his stopped motorcycle. Tesla was allowed to market their technology, which they knew needed human supervision, as Autopilot because there were no federal guardrails. In fact, it was the state of California, not the federal government, that forced Tesla to change its marketing or lose the ability to sell in that state. I do believe that federal agencies have a role. What's happened so far is the Trump administration tried to gut NHTSA, the federal agency responsible for ensuring the safety of vehicles. He basically, in the efforts of Dodge, lost 25% of their employees. I would say at this point it's trying to figure out what are the latest and greatest technologies and the people that understand them and can do appropriate oversight, not basically gut the agency. At one point last year the office of automation just had four people. Four people. I don't even know if they knew about automation, but only four people. Fewer resources mean less enforcement. NHTSA launched 41% fewer recall investigations last year than in 2024. NHTSA recalls protect consumers. For instance, over 67 million Takata airbag inflators have been recalled in the US after NHTSA confirmed that 28 people were killed when defective airbags exploded. Chrysler recalled 2.7 million vehicles after 51 people died in fires from gas tank ruptures and rear-end collisions. I have a suspicion right now that a lot of repair dealers are installing faulty airbags from Chinese manufacturers that are failing to protect consumers. Where is NHTSA in protecting and finding out what is happening with these faulty airbags? Are we going to continue to let people die in the United States? Without strong federal oversight, it is no wonder states are seeking to fill the void. You will hear today from witnesses that believe that the best way for the federal government to keep people safe is through a safety case for autonomous vehicles. Companies may try to reassure us that the safety case is a living, breathing document, but I have my own experience having seen this played out in the aviation sector. All I can tell is strong oversight is needed, because the best engineers working on the best safety are going to deliver the best product and the best economies for us in addition to the best safety. So we cannot just rely on a checklist. I agree, Mr. Chairman, we need a new approach, and I'm happy to work with you on that approach. Industry proposals seek to force autonomous vehicles into the existing framework of the Motor Vehicle Safety Act. This is the 60th anniversary of that law. The Federal Motor Vehicle Safety Standard has prevented over 18 million crashes. However, the federal motor safety standards were designed to regulate bumpers and car doors, seat belts, and a variety of things that they're not on top of today. The law was passed 20 years before the first Windows computer was ever sold. So, it's time for us to get a NHTSA that understands technology and knows what to do with it. This revolutionary technology needs a new approach to safety that provides for flexible guardrails for beta testing and a clear path to safe commercial deployment. It needs to have an educated, as I just mentioned, strong safety oversight from officials and the resources to make it the gold standard, just like we need in aviation. I found interesting the House debate on this similar hearing in which California Teamsters basically came out and called for a Waymo ban. A lot of anxiety existed, really, Mr. Chairman. Underneath was the fact that many people were concerned about a law somehow going to allow very large trucks to exist on our highways in automated vehicles. That seems to be the real crux of the issue. So, just like everything else, the devil is in the details. The devil is in the details of how we get here. I noticed that after that hearing, Teamsters, safety advocates, and even insurers found fault with a preemptive strategy that was unclear. I think we're going to hear from Professor Smith about that. But I think two lines in your statement are: "people dying today not because we are careful about automated driving, but rather because we are careless about road safety generally," and the notion that, "I have read many versions of potential preemptive language. In every case, the preemptive effect and even the preemptive intent of that language has been unclear to me." We're dealing with this in AI, and I do think we have to talk about the overlay of AI and AV and how that's going to work. If we're going to have a preemptive strategy, then it has to be a real law, and it has to have real teeth. We have to understand exactly what we're doing. But please understand, many Americans, including Teamsters, are very anxious about how this plays out for them, how this plays out for very large trucks, and how we move forward on advancing both the safety regime that we need here and to continue to be leaders. Thank you, Mr. Chairman. Thank you. I'd now like to introduce our witnesses for today. Our first witness is Lars Moravi, the Vice President of Vehicle Engineering at Tesla. Mr. Moravi oversees the design, testing, and engineering at Tesla and brings more than a decade of hands-on experience, or more precisely, hands-off experience with automotive technology and vehicle safety. I will also note that I understand it's Mr. Moravi's birthday, so happy birthday. And to celebrate, we decided to hold a hearing. Our second witness is Mauricio Pena, the Chief Safety Officer for Waymo. In this role, he leads Waymo's comprehensive safety efforts and brings decades of experience designing, testing, and deploying complex safety-critical systems across the aerospace and autonomous vehicle sectors. He is an Associate Professor of Law in the Joseph F. AVs present an opportunity to significantly improve safety on the road and enhance accessibility to transportation for all Americans.
