Many of the biggest goals companies have set will take serious upskilling. But improving individual skills or team skills isn't simple or straightforward. We don't always know what we need to improve, and we might have an inflated or an outdated idea of what we do well. Keon Katana Fouché understands this problem well. He is the CEO of Worqer, a skills intelligence platform, and their technology measures skills at scale, helping teams understand how ready they are for things like an AI era. Keon has a front-row seat to what leaders and teams need to shore up. He talked to me about the blind spots he sees, the roles leaders will need to play as tech shifts quickly, and the skilling framework he uses in hiring and within his own company. He also shared what prompt engineering can teach leaders about delegation, and why he sees a future where one day it's unethical for humans to judge other human skills. He'll talk about all this, but first, he'll give leaders a grade on how well they're bridging the skills gap. They're bad. I would give them a 2 out of 10. There's a reason behind it: people, including leaders, are extremely unaware of their own skill sets. People have a tendency to overestimate their skills by a significant margin or underestimate them. In our data, we show that 89% of people who take an adaptive assessment on Worqer, after self-rating themselves, are off by 20% or more on their final score. That's where the 2 out of 10 comes from, because only about 10 to 20% of people get it right. So, why is it a problem? When you're a leader and you are pretending to have skills you don't actually have, your behavior cascades down your organization. We find that organizations with such leaders are highly overconfident, which then creates issues with a lot of unknown unknowns. You don't know what you don't know. There's a lot more opportunity to adopt AI and to display different mindsets or behaviors that you're not doing, and that is generally hurting the company or the organization as a whole. Their staff might also individually not have a correct assessment of their own skills. So if you're trying to develop someone and they disagree with you on what they might need to fix or not fix, you've got the blind leading the blind there. That's correct, the staff is also widely off. The one caveat for a leader is that the leader has a lot of influence. An overconfident leader will lead to an overconfident organization, and an underconfident leader will lead to an underconfident organization in the same way. What makes this even more difficult to fix? There's a mismatch of awareness, but what else makes bridging the gap so complicated? What makes it hard to bridge the gap is that skills are changing fast. You might have certain skills and realize a year later that you're already behind by a significant margin. 40 years ago, the half-life of a skill was over a decade, meaning you could have the same skill for over 10 years and you'd be safe to continue to use it. Today, it's a different story. Skills go outdated within a few years—four years on average, and 2.5 years for digital skills. Imagine how short the half-life for AI skills is today. Tell us a little bit about Worqer and the problem it's solving. Worqer's purpose is to build the best measurement of human potential that we've ever built. Measuring skills is a very complex problem; it needs to be done fairly and validly. We really need a system that can evaluate us in a precise way and give us insight so that we increase our self-awareness, and most importantly, we understand the actions to take in order to change. What do I need to learn? What mentor will potentially help me? What projects are best for me? What team will I fit into? All of these require a good measurement or a good understanding of people's skills and potential. Is there an example of a change that Worqer has helped an organization make, a before and after where you were able to give them this assessment and then they were able to take that forward and have an impact? Absolutely. I'll mention a few. A lot of organizations have bought AI software; they've spent a lot of money on it. As McKinsey used to say, with every $1 invested in technology, you need a dollar invested in people, and they then went over it and changed it to for every $1 in technology, you need $3 in people. I think the truth is even more, because today we're seeing a lot of our clients struggle to get adoption on their AI tools. They ask us to come in, to run our AI readiness assessments that are customized for the organization, and to understand: where do we sit in general? Who are our champions? Who are the people that are going to amplify adoption of AI around themselves, and who are the detractors, or who are the people that don't have the skills? Absolutely, we need to help and handhold them in order to adopt these tools. We are able to answer this question and provide a very guided or personalized roadmap for people to get to the level of skills where they are effectively adopting these AI tools. That's very common. In other areas, companies really want to make talent decisions with fairness and more meritocratically. They often call these efforts "skills-based organizations." Think about an organization that moves people into the right projects, into the right teams, and gives the right opportunities to people who are deserving of it. We are often the backbone of this, meaning we evaluate people, we give them feedback, we allow them to have badges, we allow them to become mentors, we accelerate their projects. All of that starts with a good skill measurement. Typically, there has always been a gap, even without the influx of AI, between what talent wants, what employers are looking for, and also what the market actually needs. We know from our surveys that when you ask leaders if they're ready for the future, they will tend to be way more optimistic than when you ask their workforce, where the workforce will usually say, "Our leaders keep talking about it, but we are not being supported to the level we need." So there's definitely a gap between leadership and their employees on these topics of AI or future capabilities. How do we bridge that? I think when it comes to an assessment, understanding where we stand is very important, not only at the level of the workforce, but also the leadership. The best behaviors I've seen are when a leader actually shares their skills profile and says, "Look, we're all in this together. I don't have every skill. In fact, I'm lacking skills in critical thinking and problem-solving, and I need to work on my resilience." They show that to their workforce in order to get buy-in, create psychological safety, and make it clear that it's a priority for everyone, not just the workers. When I have these interviews and people say, "You've got to be humble and you've got to really show people that you're improving and you're working on these things, just like everybody else." But in the everyday, that's hard. It's really hard. There are a lot of reasons why somebody might want to be a little bit more close to the vest or might be hesitant to be vulnerable like that. What is needed to convince leaders, even if they think they are being vulnerable, to take that extra step and say, "Look, here's where I am, here's my actual development opportunities." How can we make sure that leaders especially feel comfortable saying they've got stuff to work on? First, we have to make sure that the measurement is fair and accurate so that there's trust and agreement that if we're showing a certain score in a certain area, it's meaningful. Second, humility is part of a leadership skill, and I think you need to be able to be humble when you need it and inspiring at the same time when you need it. I also want to make it clear that skills are only one component of performance. Not having certain skills is not correlated with, or equates to, being a poor performer. Some of the best performers in organizations have critical skills gaps in many areas. Often, when we look at metrics we care about within an organization, we care more about how fast people are growing their skills than their actual skills. At the end of a quarter, we might look at your learning velocity: where did you start versus where you are today? That information over time is way more valuable than where the person is today, projecting their learning velocity a year from now. Here is a worker or a leader that is adaptable, who is going to be way more skilled a year from now than they are today. Do you think that the skills conversation sometimes goes a little sideways, where people think, "Well, I just get this skill, just like I get a merit badge, and then I'm done?" I think skills in the last decade have been misrepresented and misused. They've been very generic. The first thing I recommend is making sure that whatever skill taxonomy or ontology you are looking at is directly connected to the business outcomes. A company starts with what they care about, goes one level down to what skills are needed, and then measures those skills. Based on the results of those measurements, they determine the course of action to take in order to close the gaps. If you follow this process, the skills that are presented and measured are the skills you care about, and there will be outcomes tied to them. Develop this skill, and you'll get rewarded. I also always recommend multiplying the carrots for the workforce, meaning answering the question, "What's in it for me? Why should I develop that skill?" You should develop that skill because you'll get rewarded for it. Some companies reward in cash. Other companies reward with a brown-bag lunch with the VP. Others create other types of incentives, like becoming a project leader or a mentor to other people. Those are great career developmental opportunities that the workforce is hungry for. You mentioned this taxonomy and identifying what's needed. How do you apply that same structure in skill development at Worqer? All our workforce is certified with our own AI assessments. Even beyond AI, each function within the organization has a set of custom assessments that the employees take. They know where they stand today; they know their target score by the end of the year, and they're able to work through it at their pace. In our interview process, we leverage skill assessments. We also ask a lot of questions that I've started asking more often than before, such as, "What's your learning strategy? How do you keep up with progress?" What I'm looking for is someone who's going to join us today but grow very drastically with the company in the coming years. I sometimes ask people to teach me something they learned over the last three weeks, making it easy for me, because it tells me that they've learned something and, on top of that, they've learned it well enough that they can teach it. Given the assessments that you have, what's a skill that you are looking to improve on? I'm working on a variety of skills. Delegation is a skill that I'm working on, understanding who to go to for what, and the way I communicate when I delegate a certain task. I've also worked a lot on leadership skills in general, including creating trust within leadership teams. I think trust is often one of the most important aspects in order to build a high-performance team. Do people trust each other? Are people able to focus on their own tasks and trust that someone else is going to take care of the rest? All of it comes together to create a high-performance environment. That's been something that I've worked on extensively recently. And it's something that is a moving target for all leaders. When it comes to building trust, what's something that you are learning to do or putting into your repertoire that maybe wouldn't have occurred to you before you found it at Worqer? What's something that you do now that is really helpful in building trust? The most helpful thing might be that I think before speaking, in the early days of Worqer, I used to be very real-time, meaning I would constantly be messaging and speaking with people. It has benefits because people know what's on your mind pretty much at any point in time. But over the course of the last few years, as the company grew, I realized that what I say has a different weight to certain people. I might have a thought that took me just 20 seconds, and I thought it was interesting but not super important, and I just mention it. Suddenly, because of the weight it had on people, they might be distracted for hours or days thinking about this problem that I posed in front of them, even if they had more important things to do. So I've started to be more thoughtful about what I share and how I say it, in a way that makes it clear: is this an important and urgent problem for me? Is it unimportant but urgent, or is it neither of those? And how did your assessments surface opportunity to develop more on trust? For example, when I took our effective communication assessment, I had a gap in understanding the Eisenhower matrix, which talks about something being important and urgent versus urgent but not important versus important but not urgent. That framework, I learned it through an assessment. I didn't know I didn't know it. And what was your reaction to that? My reaction was, "It's a great framework! I wish someone had taught me that framework earlier, and that I didn't have to learn it so late in my career." You also mentioned delegation, which is something every leader has to constantly work on, especially as needs and priorities change. What is a way that you have changed how you are either approaching delegation or how you actually accomplish it? Part of what changed is now when you delegate a task, you need to think: is this a task you want to delegate to an AI agent, or is it a task that you delegate to a human? Often, the answer might be a human. Other times, it might be a system that you're aware of and know is going to get the work done. We're still in the early days of agents being able to do enterprise-grade tasks end-to-end, but I anticipate that it's going to happen sooner or later. When it comes to delegation, I think the words that you use matter a lot. When you delegate a task, you want to make it very clear what you're delegating, when you need it by, as well as the reason or the motivator behind it in your head. A few years ago, I didn't really think about those things. I would just give a task and realize that I didn't define it well or explain the rationale to the person, and as a consequence, it went sideways. Today, I do it better. It's also a skill that's used extensively in prompt engineering. Maybe not the time you need it by, but when you configure an agent to perform a task, you want to be very clear with what you need it to do. Otherwise, it's just not going to do it the way you want. You mentioned that some of the skills in prompt engineering are very useful in delegating to humans or to AI. Some of those skills are useful in both situations. In your opinion, will managing AI agents help people be better at managing in general? Prompt engineering is a trend. A lot of people think about it as a role or a job, and I think that's the wrong way to think about it. Prompting is a skill that any person needs to have. Just like you need to know how to search on Google, you need to know how to prompt and how to get the AI to help you complete a task. Prompting is going to change over the next few years, and you're not going to need the craftiness you need today to get the AI to do certain things. I was at a dinner with a friend last week where we played this game where you take a picture of your friend and ask the AI to guess their spirit animal. He tried, took a picture of me, and couldn't get it to work. I took his phone, took a picture of him, and I knew how to prompt the AI so that it would do it. The AI would originally refuse to do it, just to not hurt the person. But it turns out that if you know prompting well, you can get around it. Those problems are still the case in a lot of job tasks. People who know how to prompt and are crafty are going to get the job done better. I think a year or two from now, the models are just going to be so good at understanding your intent that it's not going to make that big of a difference if you can be crafty or not, unless you're a builder of AI systems where you need that level of craft. To your question about whether the skills of a manager of humans are the same as the skills of a manager of agents, I think they're very similar. However, human management has a much deeper emotional component, where you need to care about how your message resonates with the person, not only in the content of the message but in the form. In fact, we joke that you should not thank Chat GPT or AI, because it uses unnecessary tokens and more energy and more compute. You should do it with a human, though, because it is not useless. It tells them that you're grateful, and that might have an impact on the way they complete the task or the way they interpret your feeling about their work. We can all get better at refining what we want from somebody. Here are the parameters: can you have it by this date? I want it to be like this and that. Would you recommend people practice with an AI to practice refining what they want? Absolutely. Just practicing to be precise with your tasks, and reuse those skills with humans as well, just adding a little bit of emotional connection to it. Some people treat AI as a search engine; they just search for information and get a reply. Others use AI as a set of tasks they want to complete. Finally, some use AI as a complete operating system, as my friend Omar Bao says. That's what we should all aspire to do. I try to practice that. Within Worqer, almost every information that gets to me somehow passes through AI, and any information that goes out from me also passes through AI. AI is getting better at more difficult tasks. What you might want to try is to give AI a task that you think a human could do in 30 seconds, and then try to think of a task in your daily life that might take an hour and see if AI can do it. As you go through that, you're going to understand the reasoning capabilities and how AI needs more time to complete certain tasks and is currently more prone to errors. But tomorrow, AI is going to get better at it. Practicing will give you a feel for what AI is capable of, and once you have that feeling, you'll know better when to use AI and when not to. I want to talk a little bit about mentorship. You have a special tool for that, which we will get into in a second, but I want to talk generally. You had a very special mentor at Coursera. Can you tell us about that? Why was that so shaping for you? I was lucky in my life to have a few great mentors that shaped how I think and the things I aim to achieve. One of them was Andrew Ng, my adviser at Stanford University. Professor Ng is the founder of Coursera, as well as one of the leading founders of Google Brain. I learned a few things from Andrew. He has a really high bar when it comes to content development. With him, I had the chance, through lecturing at Stanford or teaching online, to teach millions of people around the world AI skills. We spent 80% of our time deciding what to teach and only 20% teaching it, because we knew it was so important not to distract people with methods they might not need, but to focus on the essentials. I remember the first article or paper that I wrote and gave to Andrew; he looked at the first 30 characters out of 17 pages. He gave it back to me and said, "You need to work again on it." Every day I would come back and put that stack of paper in front of him, and he might read a little more and mark what he liked and what he didn't like until we got calibrated. I felt I had a better sense of what his bar is for content development, and over time it became my bar, where I was able to understand what a high-quality article or research paper looks like, and that has served me all my life. Starting an AI company requires specific knowledge or specific skills in terms of how to structure your team, and what is going to work and not going to work. I learned a lot of these skills with Andrew as well. Is there a way that you structured Worqer that you would never have done had you not had this experience with Andrew? For example, one of our values at Worqer is learning, a growth mindset, and lifelong learning, and that comes from Andrew's lifelong learning mindsets. We truly look for whether they have this growth mindset or this insatiable curiosity that you want to have when you're starting a startup in an uncertain environment and are ready to pivot before you can find what your true purpose is and scale it. In your mind, what are the characteristics that really effective mentors always share? Effective mentors are able to do three things well. First, assessment. If the mentor can't assess your skill, understand your skills and your psychological profile, they're not going to be able to help you very well or customize their guidance. Second, they understand what you're capable of, your potential, and they're able to help you dream bigger. You might be dreaming of something, and you could be dreaming of that thing times two. A good mentor helps you identify that. Then, once the mentor understands the starting point and the ambitious yet achievable goal, the mentor is able to close the gap. Good mentors have a lot of experience having mentored other people on how to connect the dots between those two points. My point of view is that we're never going to be able to scale human mentorship, and that's why I think AI helps a ton. AI, configured correctly, is a better assessor of skills than any human ever. In fact, I think that in a few years from now, it will be unethical for a company to ask a human to judge another human's skill, whether it's external for an interview or internal for a performance management process. AI will improve. AI is currently learning from many interviews and many internal company assessments on when it is fair and when it is not. At Worqer, we have expert PhDs in skills measurements who are teaching the AI how to measure and correcting it when it makes a mistake. Soon enough, it's going to be very clear that the AI is less biased than humans. Right now, you have companies that have thousands of interviewers who are completely uncalibrated from each other. Even if the HR team tries very hard to calibrate them, you might see an interviewer who likes people who sound like them or look like them or think like them. All those things are things that AI is less prone to. Bias, I would say, is a human bug. Worqer has an AI tool, an AI mentor called Sage. Can you tell us a little bit about Sage? Sage is the AI mentor of Worqer, specialized in assessing skills. Sage is an agent that can show up in any enterprise workflow, whether to assess someone for their career development, their eligibility for a project, or simply to give them feedback. Sage is really good at assessing people. It's really fair, and it's getting better by the day. Worqer is trying hard with our research to build a universal agent that can measure any skill with best practices. And what problem does this help solve? Immediately, it saves a lot of time and money for people and organizations. The cost of a bad hire is extremely high, both for the individual who was hired and then fired, but also for the organization who could have hired a better fit. That measurement should have been right. There's a lot of untapped potential within organizations. We hear every day from employees who are saying, "I should have had that project. I just didn't know the VP personally, and this person got that project. I had no way to show my skills and to raise my hand and say, 'I want to take care of it, and I'm going to do it well.'" That's significant untapped potential. Then there's the billions of hours of humans interviewing other humans or humans judging other human skills. Time is wasted, it's unfair, biased—a lot of problems to solve there. Finally, retention and attrition. Most people leave because of their manager, and often you realize the manager has never been trained at evaluating people's skills. Very few managers actually know how to evaluate people's skills well. Without understanding the skills of their people, managers are assigning the wrong tasks and coaching them the wrong way. As a consequence, a portion of the population that you did not want to leave ends up leaving. One of the problems we solve in the longer term is a more meritocratic society. I truly believe in a skills-based society where work and rewards are based on who deserves it and who's capable of it, and we're able to support people to get access to those opportunities. That future of a more meritocratic society is only possible if we have a universal measurement engine that everybody trusts, that is scientific and research-backed, and we know it's better than human judgment. Once we have that system, I expect a lot of things are going to change in the way we assess people, we evaluate them, we promote them, we reward them, and it's going to be for the better overall because people are going to get placed in the right thing that they're good at, or going to be coached to achieve the things that they're not able to do yet. How would that coaching fit in? How will mentors need to work differently? Human mentors will need to work on their emotional intelligence. I've seen it in different ways. Teachers, for example. We've trained so many teachers around the world. The job of a teacher is extremely complicated. It requires you to be a facilitator, a motivator, and a mentor. It also requires you to have subject matter expertise in math, physics, chemistry, and so forth. It's a lot to juggle. You have to teach, but you also have to look at which student is disengaged or which student is ahead and adapt yourself to them. That role will change. Subject matter expertise on a variety of topics can be delegated to AI. The real role of a teacher or a mentor is to be the facilitator, the motivator, the person who's going to cheer you up when you need it and hold the bar high when you need it. That's a very human skill, and I think we'll need a lot more of that in society—less of the person who just knows the curriculum by heart and is teaching it on a whiteboard. For managers in the enterprise, you can imagine that they're going to be able to manage more people. Today, the ratio of a manager to employees is perhaps 1 to 5, maybe 1 to 7, and then it gets complicated. In the future, you might have a manager that relies on AI agents that are specialized for measuring people's skills, allocating the team to the right tasks, and helping them with a variety of management tasks. As a consequence, one manager might be able to manage 100 people. I want to shift gears a little bit and talk about you. I read that you sometimes play computer war games. I was interested in that. Tell me a little bit about that and how it has shaped how you look at strategy and long-term thinking. I love board games in general, and my favorite one is a variation of Risk, the board game where you have to conquer countries, strategize, and make alliances. I've played that all my life with my friends, and we somehow got really good at it, and hate each other for it a lot of times. The game I like is called Warzone. People think of Warzone as Call of Duty Warzone; it's not that. It's an indie developer who alone created a game called Warzone. It's an app you can download on your phone, and it's a Risk board game on steroids. It's a turn-by-turn game, requires some thinking, and you have more time to think and establish your strategy. Additionally, I often play with friends who are not where I am. I'm in San Francisco; my childhood friends are all in my childhood town in the suburb of Paris. It allows us to stay in touch and play together, even if we're thousands of kilometers apart, because it's a turn-by-turn game. They can wait on me tonight to play, and they're in the morning, and whenever they want they play, and then we compare our results. It taught me thinking steps ahead. To be good at a strategy game, you need a strategy. You need to know what you're doing right now, what your Plan A is. If Plan A doesn't work, what is Plan B, and what will that tell you about the third step you need to take? This adaptability and strategy can be learned through board games and social games, and that's part of why I enjoy it, other than the fact that it's just a good time away from work, if you will. You founded Worqer in 2019, and a lot has happened. Was there a particular turning point in the last six years that was really shaping for you? I was always passionate about human capital as a whole. When I graduated from Stanford, I wanted to work in education, human capital, and talent. But the problem within human capital is so vast. At some point, I really felt strongly about the problem of measurements. I said, "If there's one problem I want to solve within that whole human capital ecosystem, it is to build the best measurement tool that you can." It will contribute vastly to the ecosystem. It will help what used to be our competitors—education companies, large companies developing talent, certification boards trying to make their certification better. Measuring skills really well, in a flexible manner, as a universal capability, helps the ecosystem a ton. I also think that in the early days of the company, because of how competitive I was, I was not prone to sharing much or was cagey about partnering with people in the same space. But over time, I realized that we're all working on something meaningful for the world, and our space happens to be full of mission-driven people. Now I'm very comfortable sharing, and in fact, I've seen that as an opportunity to partner closely with many impactful players in our market. Is there one problem that you think leaders should be focused on for the next five years, and how should they be thinking about that? Their people. That's number one. People are the biggest technology problem of the next decade. Leaders should check in on their people frequently. Leaders should inspire their people, who are going through so much uncertainty. If you think your people are doing well, they're probably not. Even if they are doing well, it's better that you, as a leader, think they're not and check on them to confirm that they are doing well. Because we're asking workers to do more with less. They're worried that AI is going to take their job. There's a lot of uncertainty ahead that then flows back to their families and their living situations. As a leader, the best thing you can do is to check on them, bring clarity, and be transparent about the business outlook ahead of you so that they can prepare for it. Is there a piece of advice that you've always been grateful for? I like the one-way door, two-way door decision framework that I believe comes from Jeff Bezos at Amazon, and Andrew Ng taught me that framework. Decisions can be categorized into one-way door or two-way door decisions. Some decisions are one-way doors, meaning once you make them, there's no going back, or going back is really hard. On the other hand, there are two-way door decisions where it's quite simple to go back and forth and change your decision later. If it's a two-way decision, trust your gut and go for it. If it's a one-way decision, think more deeply, make the decision, and move forward. That framework has helped me a lot at work when I'm stressing about a decision. Asking myself, "Is it a one-way door or a two-way door?" gives me a framework. It allows me to calm down and make a more sound decision at a reasonable speed. That was Keon Katana Fouché. Thanks so much to him and thanks so much to you for listening. For more podcasts, including my colleagues' program, Radio Davos, go to weforum.org/podcasts. This episode of Meet the Leader was produced and presented by me, Jerry Oansen, and Taz Kellaher as editor, Edward Bailey as studio engineer in New York, and Gareth Nolan driving studio production. That's it for now. I'm Linda Lina from the World Economic Forum. Welcome to 2026.

Frontier Models & AI | Sam Altman, CEO & Co-Founder, OpenAI

Cisco(417.0K) ·24:11 · 2026-02-04
> "Codex has been my biggest update on AI in a while.
TL;DR Sam Altman, CEO of OpenAI, emphasizes AI's rapid creation of economic value, particularly with Codex, predicting a subjective "10x improvement" in model capability this year. He highlights the need for enterprises to quickly adopt "AI coworkers" and addresses critical...

TL;DR Sam Altman, CEO of OpenAI, emphasizes AI's rapid creation of economic value, particularly with Codex, predicting a subjective "10x improvement" in model capability this year. He highlights the need for enterprises to quickly adopt "AI coworkers" and addresses critical infrastructure, new security paradigms, and evolving business models for AI's pervasive future.

AI's Transformative Potential & Immediate Impact

  • Codex as a "ChatGPT Moment": Cisco, as the first design partner, will have 100% of its AI Defense product code written by Codex within weeks.
      • Sam Altman describes this as a "clear glimpse at the future of knowledge work" that will generate "unbelievable economic value extremely quickly."
  • Vision for AI Agents: AI agents capable of generalized computer and browser use are "here to stay," enabling "incredible stuff."
      • Upper Limit: The ultimate vision is full AI companies where coding models build complex software and interact with the real world.
      • New Social Interactions: Future social networks could feature numerous AI agents interacting on behalf of people, fostering new ideas and collaborations.

Addressing Key Constraints & Adoption Challenges

  • Non-Obvious Constraints: Beyond energy and hardware, critical non-obvious hurdles include:
      • Security & Data Access: A new paradigm is needed to balance AI utility with security and data access, as current solutions are insufficient.
      • Software Architecture: Existing software is not optimized for simultaneous human and AI agent use, necessitating a rewrite to support AI (e.g., agents needing separate user accounts or primary AI interaction).
      • Always-On Computing: Current hardware, permissioning systems, and legal frameworks are not designed for AI to continuously monitor activities (e.g., listening to meetings, learning, then deleting recordings).
  • Capability Overhang & Slow Absorption: The gap between AI's rapidly advancing capabilities and organizational adoption is unprecedented.
      • Diffusion Rate: Despite rapid user growth for tools like ChatGPT, the overall absorption of AI's full potential by enterprises feels "surprisingly slow."
      • Advice for Enterprises: CIOs and CISOs must prioritize setting up organizations to quickly integrate new AI tools, proactively addressing data access and security challenges.
          • Competitive Disadvantage: Companies not prepared to rapidly adopt "AI coworkers" will be at a "huge disadvantage."
      • AI as a Teammate: Shifting the perception of AI from a transactional tool to a "collaborator" or "teammate" is vital for maximizing its impact.

Scaling Infrastructure and Evolving Business Models

  • Massive Infrastructure Demand: AI models will become more capable and cost-efficient per task, but overall usage will accelerate dramatically.
      • Underestimation of Capacity: Even with projected multi-trillion-dollar investments, the long-term infrastructure and power needs may be underestimated.
      • Energy Analogy: AI demand is akin to electricity demand—infinite at low cost, but contingent on price, speed, and quality. The world will require "a ton of tokens."
  • OpenAI's Business Model Evolution:
      • Core Products: ChatGPT and the API business are strong, with Codex showing significant growth. Future plans include consumer devices and robotics.
      • Subscription Success: OpenAI observed a surprising willingness from "many, many tens of millions of consumers" to pay subscription fees, alongside robust business demand for enterprise solutions.
      • Future Models:
          • Advertising: A potential model for mass-scale consumer businesses, requiring careful implementation.
          • AI Cloud Subscription: Businesses seek partnerships for security, platform access, running various agents, and extensive API access.
          • Scientific Discovery Investment: OpenAI may act as an investor in large-scale scientific breakthroughs requiring immense capital, partnering with companies for royalties.

Strategic Imperatives and Future Vision

  • US Leadership in Open Source: Sam Altman expresses concern about the US not leading in open-source AI, particularly as other nations develop their own capabilities.
      • Control and Privacy: He foresees increasing demand for locally running, private models, especially as AI agents become "always on" and deeply integrated into personal lives.
      • Frontier vs. Open Source: While leadership in frontier models (accessed via APIs) is paramount, open-source leadership is also crucial for user control and broad adoption.
  • Exponential Model Improvement: AI models are projected to become "very, very good this year."
      • Projected Gain: Subjectively, Sam Altman anticipates a "10x improvement" in problem-solving capability by the end of the year.
  • Visionary Future: Altman imagines a future with billions of humanoid robots, unprecedented economic growth, continuous scientific discovery, and von Neumann probes.
  • Macro Trends:
      • Tailwind: The rapid and significant improvement in AI models expected this year.
      • Headwind: Potential global destabilization and mega supply chain disruptions.

Notable Quotes

"Codex has been my biggest update on AI in a while. The, the app that we launched yesterday, it just push things over the edge for me of where it's. I'm like, all right, this is going to create an unbelievable amount of economic value extremely quickly."

— Sam Altman, CEO & Co-Founder, OpenAI "I think this is like the first time I felt another chat GPT moment for it. Here is a clear, clear glimpse at the future of knowledge work and how enterprises and individual people are going to use AI to work in a completely different way."

— Sam Altman, CEO & Co-Founder, OpenAI "I think the companies that are not set up to be able to adopt, let's call them AI coworkers very quickly, will be at a huge disadvantage, and it is going to take a lot of work and some risk to be able to adopt those."

>

— Sam Altman, CEO & Co-Founder, OpenAI "The models are just going to get very, very good this year. And that will that is a tailwind that I think we can build incredible things with."

>

— Sam Altman, CEO & Founder, OpenAI
We are the first design partner for Codex. In the past few months, we've had an exponential curve. AI defense was a product we launched last year. In about two or three weeks, 100% of the code in AI defense will be written with Codex. That's unbelievable. Codex has been my biggest update on AI in a while. The app we launched yesterday just pushed things over the edge for me. This is going to create an unbelievable amount of economic value extremely quickly. This is going to change how all of OpenAI works and how other companies work. The models really hit some threshold, and I think now the interface and harness have caught up. This is the first time I've felt another ChatGPT moment. Here is a clear glimpse at the future of knowledge work and how enterprises and individual people are going to use AI to work in a completely different way. What's the upper limit on this? The upper limit, I think, is full AI companies. There's probably no upper limit beyond that. The current one I can think about is full AI companies. That seems very powerful—this idea that a coding model can create a full, complex piece of software but also interact with the rest of the real world to build a company around it. It's a very big deal. This notion of what's happening right now with Notebook and Cloud, is it just a passing fad, or do you think there's something we should take away from that? No, it is definitely not a passing fad. Well, Notebook maybe, I don't know, but Open Cloud is not. This idea that code is really powerful, but code plus generalized computer use is even much more powerful, is here to stay. When I initially installed Codex, I said I was never going to give it full control of my computer without checking what it was doing, and that lasted about two hours because it was so useful. Then I got persuaded by people that I really shouldn't have it running like that, so now I have two laptops until I figure out how this is all going to work. But giving an AI agent full use of your computer and your web browser with all your sessions leads to incredible stuff, and that seems here to stay. The idea that we can take a Codex-style workflow and expand it to all knowledge work, so that whatever you're doing, Codex or some version of that can be using your computer and using the web for you, editing documents and whatever other kind of work you need, feels like a genuine transformation in how knowledge work will happen. Open Core did an incredible job of bringing many ideas together to make that feel usable and real, and that seems certain to be part of our future. More Book is cool, and it points to something that will be real, and maybe it will be a book. But there will be new kinds of social interaction where you have many agents in a space interacting with each other on behalf of people, leading to all sorts of new things. You can imagine a totally new kind of social network where everybody makes an agent or many agents and puts them in there, and the agents are talking and doing stuff, finding people information, and collaborating with other people's agents to come up with new ideas. I'm sure there will be an interesting future of social that may look something like that, very different than today, whether it's an old book or not, I'm uncertain. If you think about the constraints we are facing today, besides the obvious ones like infrastructure, compute, and power, are there any non-obvious constraints that are actually holding us back? People always say in the short term, you overestimate the impact of these technologies, and longer term, it's grossly underestimated. What are the non-obvious constraints that you see right now, where you think, "Man, I wish I had a magic wand if I could change that?" I think the obvious constraints are still the biggest ones: energy, manufacturing enough hardware, all that kind of stuff. The non-obvious ones that are most top of mind for me are, one, how are we going to balance the security and data access versus the utility of all of these models? I don't think anyone has a great answer to this yet. It feels to me like there is a new kind of security or data access paradigm that needs to be invented for this. Another is how are we going to rewrite all software to be equally usable by humans and AIs? There are a bunch of weird quirks right now trying to do that with the way software works. Does that change the architecture of the software itself, where you're going to optimize it for agents more so than humans? And so it fundamentally changes how you build software? Yes, and there are big examples of that, and then there are dumb examples of that. For example, I would love my agent to be able to use Slack on my behalf because I hate drowning in Slack, and I think it's this chaotic mess for me, but it's important. But the way it works right now is my agent can use the Slack web interface and go read all my threads and do something for me, but then it has marked a bunch of stuff as read in the process of doing that, and it's broken my workflows. I don't like that. That's just a silly example of how a lot of software is not quite meant for an AI and a person to be using it together. Maybe we'll want AIs to have different sorts of user accounts in some ways, using the same kinds of things. Maybe a lot of software will get rewritten so that it's primarily or largely used by AI but also still works for people using it the old-fashioned way. Another kind of non-obvious block is how one of the most powerful things about AI is you can do this always-on computing where you could have an AI listening to your meeting or watching your meeting, watching what you're doing on your computer, and then just add a lot of value and do stuff for you. Existing computer hardware is not really meant for that. Our permissioning system and how we think about what an AI gets to see and do stuff with, and what it gets to keep, is not really meant for that. Our legal system doesn't really support that. You'd like to be able to record a meeting and learn something from it and delete the recording. So I think there's a lot of just usability things like that. You talk about this notion of one of the things that I find is a huge dichotomy: there's so much advancement happening in science and all the different areas. Then, on the other hand, the kind of challenges that organizations struggle with just to get the basics up and running. There's a capabilities overhang. This is a new concept; when Microsoft Word came out, people used 2% of its capability back then. So, when you start thinking about this, how do we make sure that we actually increase the absorption rate? What advice would you give CIOs and CISOs on increasing the absorption rate within their organization for AI? The capability overhang feels to me like the biggest it has ever felt. I used to say a few months ago, maybe I would have said it feels bigger than any time except right before ChatGPT launched. It now feels even bigger now, even though people are using that for a lot of stuff. The fact that I can make small but increasingly big scientific discoveries, the fact that I can write full pieces of software, the fact that soon I can do more generalized knowledge work—those are all huge, huge things. We have always said that we're going to automate research, use that to be able to automate the economy, use that to be able to deliver incredible new value to people, and share all these benefits in this whole new technological world. We always saw that as a sort of abstract, sometime-in-the-future thing, and yet now AI is doing research and AI is able to do huge amounts of economic work. This, as you said, has happened many times in the past, but living through it definitely feels like this is not quite how it seems like it should go, that the diffusion, the absorption is so slow. Is it slower than you thought it would be? Yes, but I think I just didn't think about it that hard. In retrospect, and looking at the history, it shouldn't be surprising because it's fast in some ways, relative to other things. ChatGPT grew crazily quickly relative to any other piece of software before. Codex is growing extremely quickly. I expect the general purpose computer use knowledge work to grow quickly. Yet looking at what's capable, looking at what's possible, it does feel surprisingly slow. In terms of advice, figuring out how to set up enterprises such that they can quickly absorb these new tools and not have a year or multiple years of how we're going to think about this sort of data access and security questions that are currently blocking people, even at the level today of adopting things like Codex—that feels very important. I don't want to make too dramatic of a prediction, but I think the companies that are not set up to be able to adopt, let's call them AI coworkers, very quickly, will be at a huge disadvantage, and it is going to take a lot of work and some risk to be able to adopt those. One of the big insights we had when we were using Codex was for the first two or three months, we kept thinking it was going to be this kind of amazing tool. Then there was this light bulb that went off with actually one of your forward-deployed engineers. He said, "You folks are thinking about this wrong because you have to think about it like a teammate rather than a tool." I don't think people have fully grasped that when it comes to this, because it's still a very transactional tool in most people's minds. The Codex app is the first time to me it has truly felt like interacting with a teammate. I think one of the lessons there is, even if you have amazing technology, which the models got—I think Five-Two was the model that got super good—even once you get there, there's still so much value in how you package it, how you have users interact with it, how easy you can make it. But this, it really does to me now feel in a way that talking to ChatGPT has always clearly felt like a tool, that I am working with a collaborator now. That seems very much like the shape of things to come. Let's talk about infrastructure. You've actually spent an enormous amount of time, even on the power side of the house. Talk to us about what happens with, in general, the constraints that we have, our own power constraints, and infrastructure. How are you thinking about it? You're clearly putting enough money behind it, I guess. All the evidence we see right now is that AI models will continue to get much more capable, and also that they will get much less expensive and use many fewer resources per task. All the history you've seen of every time that happens is people want to use them much, much more. So we are planning for a world where AI usage grows at an accelerated pace each year. We think people are underestimating the capacity that's going to be needed, even now after everything that's being built out. Right now, they're saying $5 trillion will be spent in this over the course of the year. Maybe if that really gets spent quickly, that will be enough. I think it's also possible that along the way we have some supply gluts temporarily. But over a period of decades, it seems certain to me that the world is going to need a lot more tokens, even though we'll make each token way more efficient. You all have a device in your pocket running super powerful models off of a battery, but there will still be this drive for more and more. I think the world has come to realize this, and capitalism is doing its thing, and supply chains are reconfiguring, policy is changing, and we are going to build an incredible amount of infrastructure. Now, is it enough? People always talk about the total market demand for AI. It feels to me something like the market demand for electricity or energy. You can't talk about that as a general thing. You can say how much demand there would be at different price levels. In this case, you can say at different price levels for different quality, like how smart it is or how fast it is, or things like that. But if we continue to make AI really capable and really cheap, there will be a ton of demand at some price. If it's more expensive, there will be less demand. But I would like the world to just get to use a ton of it. We're in this capability right now, as we mentioned, where people are like, "Oh, you can use it for chatter." Maybe some people understand you can use it for code. I think this will just be how we do stuff, how companies run, how scientific discovery happens, how we use most software personally in our lives, and making a lot of it, if we can have it at a reasonable price, seems like a very good bet. Do you worry about the fact that the US has not had as much of a lead on the open-source side? I do worry about that. I think we should do more. So what's stopping you from doing it? Focus and time. But I think we need to solve it somehow. Just lay this out in your simulator for a second: if we don't have a substantial open-source presence, and China does, versus if we do, how does the world shape differently? To be clear, I think it's most important that we lead on frontier models, and I expect those to be accessed via APIs and other products. So it would be okay, but not great if we didn't also have the open-source lead. People want their own models. People want control of their own models. People want to run models locally, especially if you think about a world where you have a model that is going to see your whole life, you're going to have a new kind of device that's sort of always-on and kind of keeping track of everything. It adds huge value to you. I at least would really like that running on inference I control. So I think people are going to need this. Again, if we get behind, obviously it's not the end of the world, but I would really like us to lead there. And I think there will be increasing demand for locally running private models. If you were to fast-forward back and start thinking about—because one of the things that has been, I don't even know how you folks have done it within the compression of time that you've done it in, because there must have been moments where you decided that we're going to go full stack, and we're going to now start building our own inference chips, and we're going to start making sure that we have data center build-outs that are happening. It has to have been a conscious decision when you started actually going beyond the core that you had initially started with. Talk about the business model that you have today, and how does that evolve and change over time? I mean, the obvious ones are, of course, advertising is a huge unlock when that happens. But talk about the business model in general. Are you happy with the conversion rates of free to paid? Are you happy with the take rate that people are using this system with? What would you like to see more of? We have kind of two large products, and now we have some others that are coming up. We have ChatGPT and our API business. Now we have Codex, which looks like it's going to get very big, and a few other things. In the future, we'll have consumer devices and robots and all sorts of other parts of this too. People do seem very willing to pay for AI as a subscription service. Not everybody, but a lot of people are, more than we thought would. Businesses, of course, are willing to pay for things like ChatGPT Enterprise. But even consumers, you've reconditioned the consumers quite a bit. We have, I don't know the exact number off the top of my head, but many, many tens of millions of consumers paying a subscription fee. That was a surprise to me, to be honest, a happy one. So that, I think, can go further. As we add things like Codex, people are willing to pay much, much more. Advertising for mass-scale consumer businesses does seem like a good model, although I think we'll have to be very careful in how we do that. Then businesses increasingly want something like an AI cloud subscription where they're like, "I want to partner with an AI company. I want you to handle security and context linking and access. I want to be able to run lots of agents on it. I want a general purpose platform there. I want some agents from you, some agents from other people, maybe want to run other people's models. I want a ChatGPT Enterprise license. I want a ton of API access," things like that. So I think there will be a model there as well. If you were to think of, you've had an interesting model that you've talked about, which is participating in the upside on scientific discovery as well. If you think about the most mature ones, clearly, subscriptions are very mature. Clearly advertising is very mature. At this point in time, it's quite clear that you're going to participate in both of those. And then you'll have other ones that keep coming out. To be very clear, we don't want to take a share of people that are just paying us for the API and making discoveries. That's great. There's, I can imagine though, that if there is a future where we can spend billions of dollars on inference and cure an important disease, we may explore partnerships there where we pay for that cost in partnership with the drug company and then kind of get some royalty on it. This is not something we're doing now, but I think the frontier of scientific discovery with AI will require so much capital that maybe we think of ourselves as an investor in some of those cases. It is our imagination of the things that can be done. Do you find yourself struggling with how far exponentially you can think about what's going on as well? You're a pretty big thinker, but it seems like there are new ideas emerging all the time. Where do you think the imagination shortage is right now with the way these technologies are evolving? I can imagine billions of humanoid robots building more data centers and mining for material and building more power plants and all of that. I can imagine the economy growing at an unprecedented rate. There are all sorts of incredible new services and scientific discoveries happening. I can imagine the Von Neumann probes launching. And then beyond that, I have no idea. That seems like a good start. Macro headwinds, tailwinds. What do you feel like are things that you really worry about capitalizing on that are tailwinds, because you have a window within which if you don't, then you can actually lose that tailwind? And then what are the headwinds that you worry about? The tailwind is the models. We already have this capability overhang, and the models are going to get so much better quickly. We've been trying to figure out how we communicate about what we think is happening that does not seem like hype or crazy, but the models are just going to get very, very good this year. And that will—that is a tailwind that I think we can build incredible things with. Headwind? Probably some sort of global destabilization, mega supply chain disruptions, something like that. On the tailwind side of the models getting infinitely better, do you think in 2026 we see a 10x improvement, 100x improvement, or 5x improvement? What do you mean by a 10x improvement, like things that you can, problems you can solve that you were—? I would bet it subjectively feels something like 10x by the end of this year. I don't know how to put an exact metric on that, but that feels reasonable. Any question that I didn't ask that I should have asked? That was a lot. I know. I feel like we covered a lot of ground. You're going to come back again. Come back here again? Yes, I would love to. Invite me. Well, Sam Altman, thank you. Thanks so much.

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent testifies before House committee on the U.S. economy — 2/4/26

CNBC Television(3.3M) ·202:12 ·35.6K views · 2026-02-04
Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent testified before the House Financial Services Committee, highlighting the Trump administration's focus on economic growth, tailored regulation, and financial security to restore stability, while facing intense scrutin...
TL;DR Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent testified before the House Financial Services Committee, highlighting the Trump administration's focus on economic growth, tailored regulation, and financial security to restore stability, while facing intense scrutiny from Democratic...

TL;DR

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent testified before the House Financial Services Committee, highlighting the Trump administration's focus on economic growth, tailored regulation, and financial security to restore stability, while facing intense scrutiny from Democratic members over alleged deregulation, rising inflation, and potential conflicts of interest related to the President's business dealings.

Economic Growth & Regulatory Modernization: The Administration's Vision

Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent, as Chair of the Financial Stability Oversight Council (FSOC), outlined the administration's core economic philosophy and approach to financial regulation.

  • Economic Growth as Foundation: Secretary Bessent stated that "economic growth is essential for financial stability," leading to lower debt burdens, rising living standards, and a stable financial system.
  • Critique of "Regulation by Reflex": Secretary Bessent criticized past regulatory approaches, stating that under the previous administration, regulators "preoccupied themselves with reputation risk, climate related financial risk, and other risk with no clear nexus to safety and soundness," which he argued led to major bank failures in 2023.
  • Right-Sizing Regulation:
      • Goal: To foster an environment where financial institutions can thrive and contribute to economic stability, moving away from "zero-risk" financial systems that lead to "economic stagnation."
      • Community Banks: Committee Republicans and Secretary Bessent advocate for revitalizing the commercial banking system, particularly community banks, through legislation like the Main Street Capital Access Act and the Community Bank Regulatory Tailoring Act.
          • Impact: Strong, well-capitalized community banks are seen as essential for local lending, especially for agriculture, small real estate loans, and small businesses, addressing the issue of community banks becoming "too small to succeed."
  • FSOC's Priorities: The FSOC's 2025 annual report prioritizes economic growth and security with a focus on four policy areas:
      • Treasury Markets: Ensuring the U.S. Treasury market remains deep and liquid.
      • Cybersecurity: Protecting the financial system from sophisticated cyber attacks through information sharing and monitoring.
      • Regulatory Modernization: Supporting efforts to modernize supervisory and regulatory frameworks, reducing unnecessary burdens, especially for small and community banks.
      • Artificial Intelligence (AI): Prioritizing responsible AI to strengthen financial stability, while monitoring emerging risks.
  • "Trump Accounts": A new initiative providing children born between 2025-2028 with a $1,000 investment from the U.S. Treasury, aimed at promoting financial literacy and broader participation in equity markets.

Democratic Opposition: Deregulation, Inflation & Conflicts

Democratic members expressed deep concerns about the administration's policies, warning of risks to financial stability, consumer protection, and the integrity of regulatory institutions.

  • Consumer Confidence & Trade Wars: Rep. Maxine Waters stated that "consumer confidence plummet[ed] to a 12-year low" in the first year of the Trump administration, attributing it to "chaos and cruelty" and trade wars driving up prices of goods.
  • Deregulation of Wall Street:
      • FSOC's Role: Accused FSOC of being used to "deregulate Wall Street" instead of addressing threats to the economy.
      • CFPB & Enforcement: Criticized the administration for repealing rules curbing credit card late fees, shuttering the Consumer Financial Protection Bureau (CFPB), and dismissing enforcement actions against predatory firms.
  • Federal Reserve Independence Under Threat:
      • Allegation: Rep. Waters and others alleged that the Justice Department is conducting a "baseless criminal investigation into Jerome Powell to bully the Federal Reserve so they serve Trump's agenda."
      • Treasury Secretary's View: Secretary Bessent stated he believes in the Federal Reserve's independence but also accountability, attributing inflation partly to the Fed and noting the President's right to comment on interest rates.
  • Housing Affordability Crisis:
      • Democratic View: Rep. Waters argued the crisis worsened due to tariffs on housing production goods (lumber, steel, appliances) and the administration's immigration policies.
      • Trump Administration View: Secretary Bessent cited a Wharton study suggesting mass immigration has driven up housing costs, particularly rents for working Americans, through increased demand.
  • Alleged Conflicts of Interest:
      • World Liberty Financial (Trump's Crypto Company): Rep. Gregory Meeks raised concerns about a foreign-linked investment firm tied to the UAE government buying "about half" of Trump's crypto company while the President was conducting foreign policy with the country, calling it a "national security concern."
      • Market Manipulation: Rep. Al Green questioned the President's "Truth Social" post ("It's a great time to buy DJT") hours before pausing tariffs, leading to a significant increase in his company's stock value, suggesting potential market manipulation.

Key Policy Debates & Implementation Details

The hearing also covered detailed discussions on various policies and their implementation, highlighting areas of contention and planned actions.

  • Tariffs and Inflation:
      • Secretary Bessent: Maintained that tariffs "do not cause inflation" citing San Francisco Federal Reserve data, and are a tool to renegotiate trade agreements and promote U.S. economic resilience. Noted tariffs on goods like coffee and bananas were reduced to lower prices.
      • Democrats: Argued that tariffs directly lead to unaffordability for consumers and businesses, citing the impact on housing materials and everyday goods, and pointed to Secretary Bessent's own past statements as a hedge fund manager describing tariffs as inflationary.
  • Digital Assets and Stablecoins:
      • Genius Act: Secretary Bessent affirmed implementation of the Genius Act to regulate digital assets and stablecoins, aiming to make the U.S. a "national champion" and potentially finance the government.
      • CBDCs: Secretary Bessent stated the administration is "absolutely not" developing a U.S. Central Bank Digital Currency (CBDC), aligning with President Trump's executive order.
  • Bank Secrecy Act (BSA) Modernization:
      • Issue: Outdated BSA reporting thresholds (e.g., $10,000 for cash transactions since 1972) lead to excessive reports on law-abiding citizens.
      • Treasury's Plan: Secretary Bessent confirmed Treasury is studying modernizing rules and increasing thresholds to focus on "real illicit finance risk," using tools like geographic targeting orders for high-risk areas.
  • Community Development Financial Institutions (CDFIs):
      • Concern: Rep. Joyce Beatty raised concerns about unallocated FY25 program funds for CDFIs, delaying critical affordable housing projects.
      • Treasury's Response: Secretary Bessent stated Treasury is working to implement programs and will disburse funds upon apportionment, focusing on "community revitalization, not unrelated and ill-advised social policies."
  • AI in Financial Services:
      • Promise vs. Risk: Secretary Bessent acknowledged AI's potential for service improvement and fraud fighting, but also highlighted vendor concentration and the risk of malign actors, emphasizing the need for regulation to keep pace with technology.
      • Consumer Protection: Democrats pressed on consumer protection concerns, such as AI's role in bias and discrimination in credit decisions and lack of "explanability," which Secretary Bessent did not view as an FSOC priority for financial stability.

Social & Economic Equity Issues

Discussions touched upon the impact of policies on various social and economic groups, highlighting disparities and differing priorities.

  • Black Unemployment and Entrepreneurship: Rep. Nikema Williams (Georgia) expressed concern about high unemployment rates for black workers and the dismantling of the Minority Business Development Agency.
      • Secretary Bessent's Response: Secretary Bessent agreed to analyze black unemployment rates but did not view discriminatory lending as a direct "financial stability concern" for FSOC, though he acknowledged it as a concern, and offered to work on tailoring bank rules to increase lending power in minority communities.
  • Housing for Working Families: Rep. Ayanna Pressley questioned why essential baby products, required by law, are not exempt from tariffs, unlike other items like asbestos, causing financial distress for working families. Secretary Bessent deflected the question to the USTR, but committed his advocacy for the exemption.
  • Fraud and Waste: Secretary Bessent highlighted efforts to combat fraud, particularly in government benefits, citing Minnesota as an example of substantial waste and abuse.

Notable Quotes

"Economic growth is essential for financial stability." — Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent "Trump promised a so-called golden age. But apparently, he only meant one for his family and billionaire friends." — Rep. Maxine Waters "Regulation by reflex has led to a regulatory myopia that has undermined safety and soundness... The result predictably was the second, third, and fourth largest bank failures in US history in 2023." — Treasury Secretary Scott Bessent "I believe that any other president making this kind of move is almost a hustle... what you're doing is giving him a license to move markets at will, make announcements, and reap benefits." — Rep. Al Green
The committee on financial services will come to order. Without objection, the chair is recognized to declare a recess of the committee at any time. Today's hearing is titled the annual report of the Financial Stability Oversight Council. Without objection, all members will have five legislative days within which to submit extraneous materials to the chair for inclusion in the record. I now recognize myself for a 4-minute opening statement. Good morning, Treasury Secretary Bessant. Welcome. It's an honor to have you with us today to provide testimony as the chair of the Financial Stability Oversight Council. Today, we will examine recent actions taken by the FSOC and provide members the opportunity to address any questions or concerns regarding FSOC's 2025 annual report. As the 2025 annual report makes clear, economic growth is essential for financial stability. In a growing economy with rising incomes, debt burdens fall, American standards of living rise, and the financial system remains stable. It's encouraging to have financial regulators and policymakers who understand this fundamental reality and aim to foster a regulatory environment that considers how both new and existing regulations alter and impact economic growth. Committee Republicans share the commitment to promoting economic growth through the elimination of unnecessary regulatory burdens, a hallmark of the Trump administration. Since assuming the presidency just over a year ago, President Trump has built on this critical work that he achieved in his first administration to make further progress in rolling back overly burdensome regulations that stifle innovation in our economic growth. These efforts go beyond reducing just regulatory red tape; they're about fostering an environment where financial institutions can thrive and contribute to the stability and growth of our economy. I'd be remiss if I didn't remind everyone in the room today of the doom and gloom economic predictions that we heard across the aisle as the Trump administration was sworn in. In late 2024, Moody's analytics anticipated that a Republican controlled government would bring 3.5% inflation, 5% unemployment, a recession, and a budget deficit of 6% of GDP. Since then, Democrats have repeatedly and wrongfully accused Republicans of "tanking the economy." With most of the official data in the books, these warnings missed the mark by a mile. Inflation came in at nearly 1 percentage point lower than that forecast. The unemployment rate never increased beyond 4.5%. No recession materialized, and GDP is on track to have three consecutive quarters above 3% growth. Meanwhile, the budget deficit is on track to fall to some 5.4% of the GDP. These numbers speak for themselves. Under President Trump's leadership and Treasury Secretary Bessant's steady hand, the economy is back on track, reversing the damage of the previous administration. Throughout the 119th Congress, committee Republicans remain steadfast in our goal of reinvigorating the commercial banking system and making life more affordable for our American families. That commitment is why I introduced the Main Street Capital Access Act with Subcommittee of Financial Institutions Chair Andy Barr last month. Our community banking package is designed to revitalize local bank formation. Right-size regulations that are intended for far larger and more complex institutions ensure that community lenders can focus on serving our families, small businesses, and our local economies. This bill also aligns with the administration's efforts to right-size regulation and reflects the committee Republicans' dedication to ensuring that financial operations focus on safety and soundness with a tailored approach to supervisory complexity. Similarly, our bipartisan Housing for the 21st Century Act addresses housing affordability head-on by reducing the burdens that have made homes and apartment construction untenable. Improving affordability requires directly expanding the housing supply. Paired with Main Street Capital Access, Americans will benefit from more homes and more affordable ways to finance them. We're grateful for your time today, Mr. Secretary. We look forward to the discussion, and I yield back the balance of my time. I now recognize the ranking member of the committee, Mrs. Waters of California, for a four-minute opening statement. Thank you very much, Mr. Chairman. Last week, Donald Trump marked his first year in office with consumer confidence plummeting to a 12-year low. That is not a coincidence. Trump promised a so-called golden age, but apparently, he only meant one for his family and billionaire friends. Instead of helping families, Trump's trade wars are driving up the price of goods like groceries, clothing, and furniture. Small businesses and farmers feel it too, and we all know exactly who is to blame. Instead of lowering costs, Donald Trump has doubled down on chaos and cruelty while handing more power and more freedom to Wall Street. For example, instead of directing the Financial Stability Oversight Council to address threats to our economy, it appears they're using it to do the exact opposite and deregulate Wall Street.
