Today we welcome the testimony of the Honorable Scott Bessant, Secretary of the Department of Treasury and chair of the Financial Stability Oversight Council. Secretary Bessant, we thank you for taking time to join us today. You will be recognized for five minutes to give an oral presentation of your written testimony and, without objection, your testimony will be made part of the record. Mr. Secretary, you are recognized for five minutes. Chairman Hill, Ranking Member Waters, and members of the committee, thank you for inviting me to discuss the Financial Stability Oversight Council's 2025 annual report. This report is a culmination of extensive collaboration among FSOC members. I'd like to thank them for their hard work and dedication in advancing the President's bold vision for a better America. Since day one, President Trump has focused on building parallel prosperity, an era of economic expansion where Wall Street and Main Street can grow together. To that end, Treasury has tirelessly pursued pro-growth policies to unlock the potential available to all Americans when they are free to save, invest, build businesses, and drive their own economic destinies. The Financial Stability Oversight Council plays an important role in delivering on this agenda. Too often in the past, we have seen regulation by reflex. Rather than preempting crises, regulators have frequently reacted to them after the fact. They've played the role of a hazmat cleanup team instead of preventing dangerous spillovers in the first place. Regulation by reflex has led to a regulatory myopia that has undermined safety and soundness. Under President Biden, the bank regulators preoccupied themselves with reputation risk, climate-related financial risk, and other risks with no clear nexus to safety and soundness. At the same time, they centered supervision on management and other governance matters that distracted examiners and bank risk managers from the real risks to safety and soundness. The result, predictably, was the second, third, and fourth largest bank failures in US history in 2023. Besides undermining safety and soundness, regulation by reflex has driven excessive regulation that can lead to economic stagnation, and economic stagnation is itself a grave threat to financial stability. In calibrating regulations, federal agencies must avoid the temptation to create a zero-risk financial system, which would result in what others have called the "stability of the graveyard." FSOC should aim to identify vulnerabilities that could lead to systemic crises and encourage the private sector to mitigate those risks before recommending additional regulation. FSOC should also work with its members to support efforts to avoid or pare back existing regulation that stifles pro-growth lending, capital formation, and innovation. And the best way to achieve these goals is by centering economic growth and economic security at the heart of FSOC's agenda. Promoting economic growth and economic security is essential to ensuring financial stability. Economic growth strengthens household, business, and financial institution balance sheets, creating capital buffers that reduce the risk of defaults and financial stress. Economic security reinforces domestic production capacity, raising living standards while reducing vulnerability to external shocks and supply chain disruptions. FSOC's annual report prioritizes economic growth and economic security accordingly with a specific focus on four policy areas: Treasury markets, cybersecurity, regulatory modernization, and AI. First, the council is ensuring that the US Treasury market remains the deepest and most liquid in the world. The council is supporting efforts by member agencies to strengthen this market against future shocks, including through the Interagency Working Group on Treasury Market Surveillance and the Market Resilience Working Group. Ongoing monitoring and targeted reforms by individual agencies remain essential to financial stability. Second, the council is taking action to protect our financial system from increasingly sophisticated cyberattacks. Nation-state actors and criminal groups continue to target our financial institutions and infrastructure. To address this risk, the council is supporting expanded information sharing, joint monitoring, and scenario-based exercises. And it is emphasizing the need for regulated firms to manage cyber risk tied to third-party service providers. Third, the council is committed to supporting efforts to modernize supervisory and regulatory frameworks for banks and credit unions. Going forward, regulation and supervision should address material risks, enhance transparency, and reduce unnecessary burdens, particularly for small and community banks. Fourth, the council is prioritizing responsible AI to strengthen financial stability. The council is working with public and private partners, including international counterparts, to enhance system resilience while closely monitoring emerging risks. I will close by highlighting our progress in enhancing the utility of our annual report. In this year's report, FSOC shifted away from its past approach where nearly every major market and financial sector was described as a financial stability vulnerability by introducing a new structure centered on fostering economic growth and security. We're tuning out the noise to concentrate on the issues that matter most for US financial stability. With this overview, I look forward to taking your questions. Secretary Bessant, the Bank Secrecy Act, or BSA, reporting modernization is very long overdue and is required by the Anti-Money Laundering Act of 2020, otherwise known as AMLA. Increasing these outdated BSA reporting thresholds is, in my opinion, a critical component of this modernization effort. The Currency Transaction Report requires banks to report cash transactions that exceed $10,000. That threshold has remained the same since 1972. This low-value threshold has in recent years led financial institutions to file over 20 million reports on their customers annually. As I brought up during our recent hearing, $10,000 in 1972 was enough to buy two brand new Corvettes, just to give an illustration of how inflation has affected these CTR filings. The vast majority of these reports are filed on law-abiding citizens conducting innocent transactions. I agree with something that you've stated: that banks need to focus their BSA programs on real illicit finance risk, and that focus on higher-value activities would also better serve our law enforcement and national security objectives. Yes, Congressman, we are studying this carefully. We look forward to working with you on it. 3D & AI | Dr. Fei-Fei Li, CEO & Co-Founder, World Labs

Cisco(417.0K) ·22:19 · 2026-02-04
Dr.
TL;DR Dr. Fei-Fei Li, CEO & Co-Founder of World Labs, positions spatial intelligence as the next frontier of AI, essential for machines to understand and interact with the 3D physical world, and introduces "Marble," World Labs' state-of-the-art generative 3D world model with...

TL;DR

Dr. Fei-Fei Li, CEO & Co-Founder of World Labs, positions spatial intelligence as the next frontier of AI, essential for machines to understand and interact with the 3D physical world, and introduces "Marble," World Labs' state-of-the-art generative 3D world model with diverse applications.

Spatial Intelligence: The Next Frontier of AI

  • Foundational Intelligence: Dr. Li argues that spatial intelligence, the ability to understand, reason, interact with, and navigate the real 3D/4D physical world, is as foundational as language intelligence.
      • Evolutionary Perspective: Perception (seeing, touching) kicked off the development of nervous systems over half a billion years ago, far predating language, which is a very new form of intelligence (~half a million years).
      • Linchpin Technology: Spatial intelligence is the key technology enabling this next generation of AI.

World Labs and the "Marble" Model

  • Company Focus: World Labs, co-founded by Dr. Li two years ago, focuses solely on spatial intelligence.
  • Introducing Marble: Marble is the name of World Labs' first-generation spatial intelligence model, also casually referred to as a "world model."
      • Core Functionality: Marble takes multimodal inputs (sentences, pictures, videos, simple 3D inputs) and transforms them into fully navigable, interactable 3D worlds.
      • Key Attributes: These generated worlds are permanently consistent and possess geometric structure, supporting actions like robotic simulations or game development.
      • Current Status: Released about two months ago, Marble is currently the state-of-the-art 3D generative world model, still in its early "first-generation" phase.

Diverse Applications of Spatial AI

  • Immediate Use Cases (Not 5 Years Away): World Labs is already engaging with users and customers leveraging Marble.
      • Creative Industries: Game development, virtual production settings for VFX users.
      • Robotics & Simulation: Used as training environments for robots by partners like Nvidia, startups, and academic labs.
      • Design: Architects and designers are using it for interior and other forms of design.
  • Surprising Applications:
      • Clinical Research: Psychiatric and mental health researchers are using Marble to create personalized, immersive environments for studying interventions (e.g., specific triggers for OCD patients).
      • Well-being & Fitness: Personalizing yoga or other fitness training environments.
  • Horizontal Enterprise Technology: Spatial intelligence is a broad technology with potential across numerous sectors.
      • Future Potential: Healthcare, educational products, field services, financial services, agriculture, manufacturing, inspection, warehouse management, and urban planning.

Challenges and the Future of World Models

  • Computational Intensity:
      • World Labs' explicit 3D/4D models are currently a few orders of magnitude smaller than the largest language models (e.g., GPT-5 at ~10^26 flops).
      • This field is much newer, still in early stages of model architecture development, but rapid progress is expected over the next few years.
  • Data Scarcity and Strategy:
      • Contrast with LLMs: Language models benefit from vast amounts of "clean" internet-scale text data.
      • Hybrid Data Approach: World models face a "messier" world of pixels and voxels, requiring a hybrid strategy that combines:
          • Internet-scale text, images, and videos.
          • Simulated data.
          • Real-world captured data (e.g., from self-driving car development).
      • Advancement Speed: Despite data challenges, factors like increased talent, powerful compute, a maturing ecosystem, and the flywheel effect of synthetic data (models generating data for simulations) suggest rapid advancement similar to language models.
  • Generalized Robotics:
      • A "Very Hard Problem": While a clear "North Star," the journey to generalized robots is long and complex.
      • Car vs. Robot: Self-driving cars (2D, avoid touching) took two decades for major milestones. Robots are 3D, interact with a 3D world, and their goal is to *touch things without breaking them*, making it a much higher-dimensional problem.
      • Key Challenges: Dexterity of hands, precision vision, spatial understanding, and lack of training data. Dr. Li emphasizes avoiding over-promising on the timeline for general-purpose robots.

Dr. Li's Broader AI Perspectives

  • AI as Civilizational: Having dedicated her career to AI, Dr. Li now sees it not just as a personal quest, but as a "civilizational" technology, adding a profound sense of responsibility to her work as a technologist and educator.
  • Breathtaking Speed of Development: The rapid pace of AI advancements is exciting but also creates anxiety about staying current, even for seasoned experts. Continuous learning and curiosity are paramount.
  • Call for Nuanced Dialogue: Dr. Li expresses deep concern about the polarized rhetoric surrounding AI, oscillating between "total tech utopian" and "doomsday" scenarios.
      • Responsible Discourse: Technology is a double-edged sword, and the agency to guide it for good lies with every individual – entrepreneurs, leaders, engineers, and citizens.
      • Optimism with Responsibility: She advocates for a more nuanced, benevolent, and optimistic conversation focused on using AI responsibly, rather than optimizing for clickbait.
  • Defining Success for AI: Success for AI, like for electricity in the past, means an improved civilization where schools are lit, homes are warm, machines empower life, life expectancy lengthens, and more children learn. It's about empowering individuals to pursue happiness, prosperity, and dignity.

Notable Quotes

"The ability to understand, to reason, to interact with and to, to navigate the real 3D, 4D physical world is the foundation, as foundational as language intelligence. And the linchpin technology is spatial intelligence." — Dr. Fei-Fei Li "I think as marble becomes better, we're still in the early days, we're going to see, more and more horizontal use use cases." — Dr. Fei-Fei Li "The speed of development of AI this past few years is just breathtaking... this technology is just moving us at a breathtaking, speed. So that's that gives me a lot of excitement, but also keeps me very grounded about how little I know." — Dr. Fei-Fei Li "Technology is a double edged sword, but the agency is within all of us every entrepreneur, every product manager, every business leader, every software engineer, every citizen. We still have to have that agency to know where to lead this technology." — Dr. Fei-Fei Li
It’s been a pleasure to see the progress that’s been made within World Labs in the past year. So let’s start by talking about what you’re doing and why it’s so important. Right now I wake up every day and think about just one thing and one thing only, which is spatial intelligence. That’s the company that I co-founded two years ago with a group of young technologists. It’s called World Labs. So why is spatial intelligence so important? And why do I say that it’s the next frontier of AI? If you take an evolutionary view of the development of intelligence, which starts more than half a billion years ago, the first thing that kicked off an evolutionary arms race of the development of the nervous system is perception, not language. Language compared to perception is a very new form of intelligence. If you’re generous and think about it, language is probably half a million years old, probably not even that. But one and a half billion years ago, animals started to see light and touch the environment. So tactile and visual sensing, and instinct and perception as well. Instinct is a very interesting word. To me, instinct is a fluffy word. I’m not rejecting the word instinct, but physical touch and seeing kicked off the development of the nervous system. That set off the evolutionary arms race of animals becoming more and more active, interacting with their environment and becoming more intelligent. All this is a long way of saying that the ability to understand, to reason, to interact with and to navigate the real 3D, 4D physical world is as foundational as language intelligence. The linchpin technology is spatial intelligence. That’s why I believe this is the next generation or next frontier of AI. This is what World Labs has been building towards. So talk to us about Marble. What is Marble? You launched that recently. Marble is the name for our first generation of our spatial intelligence model. We tend to call it casually a world model. But the truth is that there are so many definitions of world model, it almost doesn’t matter if you call it a world model or not. It’s a spatial intelligence model, a frontier model. What Marble does is that it takes multi-modal inputs, whether it’s just a sentence, a picture, a video, a few pictures, or a simple 3D input, it doesn’t matter. It’s multimodal. It then turns that prompt into a fully navigable, interactable world that is 3D, which is permanently consistent and very different from the video models you see these days. It has the geometric structure to support simulating robotic actions or coding up a game and all that. Marble is a frontier model. We released it two months ago. It’s still a first-generation model. We’re very excited to start this journey. Right now, it’s still the state-of-the-art 3D generative world model. There’s a school of thought that says that if you just go with language as the model, we will not be able to get to AGI. You need to have an augmentation of physicality. You, of course, believe that. But talk to us about that dimension. What’s the big unlock that happens over time? The obvious use case is robotics. But even beyond that, what are the things that we’ll be able to do in five years with world models that we don’t even think about today? You don’t even have to wait for five years. We’re already engaging with users and customers to play with our early-stage world model. We are seeing users develop games with it. We’re seeing VFX users and customers use it to work in commercial virtual production settings. We are engaging with our robotics and simulation partners from big companies like Nvidia, as well as startups and academic labs, where they’re using Marble as training environments for robots. We’re seeing architects and designers use this for interior design and other kinds of design. We’re also getting surprise use cases; for example, clinical researchers have come to us and expressed a lot of enthusiasm. It turned out a lot of psychiatric and mental health research, as well as intervention, requires immersive environments that are personalized to particular situations. For example, OCD patients, some people are triggered by very specific situations. Personally, I’m triggered by dirty laundry, but I’m sure these people are triggered in different ways. These researchers are studying that it’s very hard for them to get their hands on these environments. With Marble, every time you prompt, within minutes you get many different kinds of environments that you can use. This is actually a surprise use. We’ve also seen people with well-being and fitness training to personalize yoga environments or other environments. So as Marble becomes better, and we’re still in the early days, we’re going to see more and more horizontal use cases. You’ve been in the AI industry for a very long time. That’s a nice way to talk about my age. No, no, no, I mean, you’re one of those people that hasn’t just looked at this for three years; you’ve dedicated your life to AI. What surprised you the most as you built this company and as you were starting to think about building the spatial intelligence side? That’s a great question. I know, Gita, you’re the same. We talk about, even in our age, the most important thing is learning, and there’s hunger for learning. Yes, I’ve been in this field for many years. I would say that I came to AI because of my very personal curiosity. I just love to ask that audacious question: what is intelligence and how to make machines intelligent? But it was impersonal. Nobody cared about AI. The world didn’t know how to spell AI, and it was fun. The past ten years have completely changed. AI is not just personal for me; AI is civilizational. That added a layer of responsibility for me in terms of how I can contribute not only technologically and as an educator, but also to the healthy development of AI. What surprised me the most as an entrepreneur... Couple of things. One is the speed of development of AI this past few years is breathtaking. I know everyone, whether we pretend or not, deep in their heart feels that anxiety of there’s just too much to read – too many blogs, too many news, too many model releases. That sense of anxiety speaks of our time, that this technology is just moving us at a breathtaking speed. That gives me a lot of excitement, but also keeps me very grounded about how little I know. This famous saying, "I don't know anything" – someone like me even feels that. I want you to at least hear this from me and recognize we all feel that. Don’t give up your learning and continue to be curious. The other thing that surprised me, and frankly continues to worry me, is the polarized rhetoric of AI. Too many cyberspace cycles are still spent discussing AI and its implications at the extreme ends, whether it’s total tech utopian, as if technology is never a double-edged sword, or it’s total doomsday, as if humans are facing an existential crisis every minute. That’s just not a responsible way of talking about a technology that is so profoundly important to our civilization. Technology is a double-edged sword, but the agency is within all of us – every entrepreneur, every product manager, every business leader, every software engineer, every citizen. We still have to have that agency to know where to lead this technology. I really hope that going into 2026 and forward, we don’t stay at the two extreme ends of talking about AI. Let’s be more nuanced. Let’s be benevolent. Let’s have the optimism of using technology for good, but the sense of responsibility of using it responsibly. Let’s have that conversation and not just optimize for clickbait. So what does success look like in your mind for AI in the next few years? I really like your framing of this kind of polarized view that we have: all jobs are going to be lost and we’re just going to be staring at the ocean, or this thing is completely useless and not going to do anything. G2, if we roll time back 150 or 100 years, I don’t remember the exact years. If a version of two of us were on stage, talking about what success looked like for electricity, I hope that there was a version where our schools are lit, our homes are warm. It would empower machines to industrialize our life. It will in turn lengthen our life expectancy. More children will be learning when they hit school age. That’s the meaning of technology and that’s the meaning of AI. That’s a timeless value. Success looks like when civilization is better, and civilization is made by every single individual pursuing happiness, prosperity, and with a sense of dignity. That is what success looks like for AI and for every piece of technology. It’s fantastic. So I’m going to go down to tactics for a second as well. Again, are our large world models as computationally intensive as language models? More or less. That’s a great question. First of all, there are different kinds of large world models. We are very committed to creating world models that have 3D, 4D explicit representation to empower robotics, gaming, entertainment, and design and all that. And there are other models that are called world models that are more video generation models right now in the field. Our models are not as large as the bigger language models; GPT-5, I believe, is trained around 10 to the 26 flops. Our Marble model is still a few orders of magnitude smaller. Is that just because you don’t have enough data to go out and feed these models? I think it’s actually both. Data is one of the scaling factors that starts with data and model parameters. Data plays a role. It’s also because this field is early. Our language models started—the Transformer paper was published in 2016—so it’s almost ten years, right? World models are a much newer field. We are still in the early stages; I wouldn’t say the earliest, but I think in the past two years, we’ve de-risked some of the science. We’re still in the relatively early days of figuring out the model architecture, so it’s relatively small. But having said that, given the progress our lab has made in the past couple of years, and looking at our field, I think the next couple of years are going to be very exciting. We’re going to see ourselves riding the curve of the scaling law in large world models. We’ve talked about this before, but this thing that was fascinating to me was when you highlighted to me, when we were first starting to talk, the fact that language models are trained on free, publicly available data on the internet. It’s easy to go out and get large amounts of data. Physical data is hard to do. So synthetic data gets to be a big deal. But you also have to collect that data in a much slower fashion. Walk us through the constraints that brings, and is the speed of advancement of the world models, because of that scarcity of data, going to be similar to what we’ve been used to with language models? Is it going to be slower, and then how is that going to manifest itself even in physical robotics where are you going to have general-purpose robots that can do everything for you, or is it going to be special-purpose robots just because of the data that we have available? That’s a loaded question, G2. So let’s talk about data and let’s talk about generalized robots. They are related, but there’s a little bit of nuance. For example, at World Labs, what is our data strategy? A lot of people ask, and obviously, I can only tell you so much, but one thing is very clear: we take a hybrid data strategy approach. I’m so envious of my language friends, because at the end of the day, the input data, the alpha data, is fully observable, more or less one modality – just text. And texts are super clean, right? I know that there are words that are not good words, but by and large, the lexicons carry very clean meanings and semantics, whereas the world of pixels and voxels is just so much messier. In order to push the boundary of the technology to creating 3D, 4D worlds, we don’t have large amounts of 3D, 4D data to train our model. So what we have to do is take the hybrid approach of layering multi-modality data. We do have texts and images and videos that are more or less at the internet scale. But we also need to have simulated data. We also need to have what we call real-world captured data. You might be wondering what those data are. One of the biggest examples is to look at self-driving cars, whether we look at Tesla or Waymo or other self-driving cars. These companies spent years, decades, collecting real-world data, as well as simulated data. So creating our models will take that kind of approach. Is it going to be slow? That’s a really interesting question because there are some confounding factors. For example, way more people are joining this journey. The compute is much more powerful. The chips and the ecosystem are much more mature. We work with data vendors that were not around even three years ago. Synthetic data is really taking effect pretty well and is actually adding to our efforts. What we are making will also add to the world of simulation. So there is a flywheel. Our model will also be used for simulation data for robotics. Just a couple of sentences on generalized robots. I have run the Robotic Learning Lab at Stanford for the past decade and beyond. I’m super excited by robots. I do think that, as a scientist, it’s important to recognize this is a very hard problem. Just because the North Star is clear doesn’t mean the journey is short. In fact, it’s a long journey. Think about 2006, my colleague Sebastian Thrun and I led the Stanford team to create the first self-driving car that could run 138 miles in the desert of Nevada. That was when we declared we were going to have self-driving cars. Twenty years later, last year was the first year of Waymo running on the streets of San Francisco and some larger cities. We more or less have gotten to a major milestone, but that’s a very long journey. The difference between cars and robots: a car is a square-ish robot that moves on two-dimensional surfaces, and the only thing the car needs to do is not touch things – don’t touch pedestrians, don’t touch the curbs. Think about a robot. It’s a three-dimensional thing, whether it’s humanoid or not, working with a three-dimensional world. The goal of a generalized robot is to touch other things in a way that is not breaking them. That is a much higher-dimensional problem, and we are making progress. The simulation of the hand has been really hard. Dexterity of hands, the precision of vision, the understanding of space, the lack of training data – all this is challenging. This is why I started World Labs, because I know this is a very important problem. We want to work on this. But I also, personally, as a scientist, don’t like to overpromise. So, Feifei, for the people from the enterprise side of the house, how should they be thinking about world models, physical AI, and all your work that you’re thinking about? The last thing I’ll say, because I know we’re running out of time, is we wouldn’t be able to have a Cisco AI summit without Feifei anymore because it would feel incomplete. So we’re going to keep inviting you. Thank you. I’m waiting for my swag. Just kidding. I still think like a grad student, always looking for free food as well. For the enterprise, especially for world models and spatial intelligence, we’re very much enterprise-facing, and in my opinion, it’s a business-focused area. World Labs is very open to talk to enterprise partners. It’s a horizontal technology. We talk about robotics, simulation, and immersive interactive entertainment experiences. But we haven’t talked more about healthcare. We haven’t talked about educational products. We haven’t talked about field services. We haven’t talked about financial services. We haven’t talked about agricultural manufacturing. Inspection, warehouse, city urban planning, and all this – there’s just a lot we can do with spatial intelligence. I do think this is the next frontier and invite all of you to work with us or work by yourselves on this topic. Thank you.

Cloud & AI | Jeetu Patel & Matt Garman, CEO, AWS

Cisco(417.0K) ·21:25 · 2026-02-04
AWS CEO Matt Garman emphasizes that successful AI adoption hinges on clear success metrics, robust security frameworks, and scalable infrastructure, detailing AWS's "AI-first cloud" vision, custom silicon, and sovereign cloud solutions designed to enable rapid and secure enterprise deployment.
TL;DR AWS CEO Matt Garman emphasizes that successful AI adoption hinges on clear success metrics, robust security frameworks, and scalable infrastructure, detailing AWS's "AI-first cloud" vision, custom silicon, and sovereign cloud solutions designed to enable rapid and secure...

TL;DR

AWS CEO Matt Garman emphasizes that successful AI adoption hinges on clear success metrics, robust security frameworks, and scalable infrastructure, detailing AWS's "AI-first cloud" vision, custom silicon, and sovereign cloud solutions designed to enable rapid and secure enterprise deployment.

Defining Success and Overcoming Adoption Barriers

  • Gap in AI Deployment: Many companies struggle to move beyond AI proofs-of-concept (PoCs) to production due to a lack of clearly defined success criteria from the outset.
      • Example: One hospital leader saw no financial savings from AI-driven ambient listening, while another successfully used it to reduce doctor and nurse attrition (from a 30% risk) by improving work-life balance.
  • Measurement Challenges:
      • Strong Metrics: Companies typically have good metrics for AI in customer service and code generation (e.g., lines submitted).
      • Fuzzy Metrics: General workforce productivity gains from AI often lack clear, measurable metrics.
  • Security and Operational Hurdles:
      • Agent Risks: Concerns about AI agents acting outside intended parameters, agent sprawl, and identity management.
      • Scaling PoCs: Difficulty scaling AI PoCs (e.g., from a single GPU instance) to global production environments, leading to underutilized resources and operational complexities.

AWS's "AI-First Cloud" Strategy and Economics

  • Ubiquitous AI Integration:
      • Vision: AWS believes AI inference will become a fundamental component of *every* application, dissolving the distinction between "AI applications" and non-AI ones.
      • Core Principle: This necessitates native integration of AI into all aspects of AWS infrastructure, including storage, VPC, security, permissions, and model testing.
  • Platform and Ecosystem Approach:
      • AWS Bedrock: A platform designed to offer extensive model choice, capabilities, and the robust security expected by customers within their VPCs.
      • Partner Ecosystem: AWS acknowledges it cannot build every component and actively partners with other providers to ensure a comprehensive ecosystem for customer success.
  • Custom Silicon (Trainium Chips):
      • Strategic Investment: Over a decade of investment in custom silicon like Trainium aims to offer differentiated performance and choice.
      • Economic Impact: While potentially improving AWS margins versus solely relying on third-party GPUs, the primary goal is often to provide better price-performance for customers, fostering a flywheel effect where lower costs enable more customer innovation.

Addressing Infrastructure and Supply Chain Realities

  • Physical Infrastructure Constraints:
      • Scaling Disparity: While software development accelerates, physical infrastructure build-out (e.g., pouring concrete, securing permits, power access) remains time-consuming and doesn't scale at the same pace.
  • Space Data Centers:
      • Theoretical Benefits: Offers appeal with infinite power and easy cooling.
      • Current Impracticality: Significant barriers include the immense weight of servers, lack of permanent space structures, limited rocket availability, and "massive", uneconomical payload costs for transport.
      • Bottleneck: The primary constraint is the cost and availability of transport into space.
  • "Full Stack" Control and Utilities:
      • Evolving Role: AWS's journey into chip manufacturing indicates a willingness to integrate vertically when necessary.
      • Utility Gap: Traditional utility industries, often with fixed/regulated pricing and 3-5% annual growth, cannot scale to meet the exponential demand of hyperscalers.
      • AWS Involvement: AWS may need to directly fund or develop "behind-the-meter" power solutions to bridge this gap, even if not directly operating power stations.
  • Chip Cycle and Capacity Planning:
      • Compression: Chip cycle times are compressing (18-24 months), though constrained by manufacturing processes (e.g., TSMC).
      • Demand & Longevity: High demand often means older chips (like A100s) remain fully utilized. Some use cases (e.g., HPC requiring higher precision) still depend on older generations.
      • Long-Term Planning: AWS manages complex capacity planning across multiple time horizons, from 20-30 year data center amortization to 5-6 year server lifecycles, and manages supply chain risks across all levels of investment.

Transforming Software Development with AI

  • AI-Driven Coding Acceleration:
      • Significant Gains: Teams adopting an "AI-driven coding" mandate from the start see 10x to 100x improvements.
      • Benefits: AI systems gain deep context, understand interdependencies, auto-document code, and facilitate comprehensive automated testing.
  • Current Limitations and Future Outlook:
      • Complex Legacy Systems: AI struggles to provide the same speed-up for highly integrated, large-codebase legacy systems (e.g., S3), especially in lower-level stack areas like memory management.
      • Ongoing Development: AWS expects to address legacy code challenges within 6-9 months by developing novel ways for AI systems to comprehend existing codebases.

Navigating Sovereign AI and Safe Deployment

  • Sovereign AI Infrastructure:
      • Customer Concern: European companies, for instance, express trust in AWS but concern over potential U.S. government intervention ("What if the U.S. government decides to turn me off?").
      • Prioritizing Resilience: For critical sectors like banking and telecommunications, national resilience and control often outweigh pure technological advantage.
      • AWS Solution (EU Sovereign Cloud): Launched as a fully separate subsidiary within the EU, beholden to EU laws, with an independent governing board. All data, including metadata and logins, remains within EU regions, and the system has been tested for disconnection from the broader AWS backbone. This model was co-designed with regulatory bodies like BSI Germany.
  • Advice for CIOs and CSOs (Accelerating Adoption):
      • "Guardrails Analogy": Just as handrails enable running across a narrow board over a canyon, robust security and operational guardrails allow teams to adopt AI rapidly and safely.
      • Mitigate Fear: Address concerns that unleashing AI agents could lead to production outages or security incidents.
      • Empowerment through Safety: Provide teams with "safe places to run fast" in production settings through AWS building blocks like Agent Core, which offer necessary controls and guardrails.

Notable Quotes

"I think one of the challenges that many companies had is that when they started doing a bunch of proof of concepts, with AI, they didn't actually have good success criteria defined at the beginning." – Matt Garman, CEO, AWS "we view that AI and inference in particular, is going to be a critical part of every single application in the world. And so there's not going to be AI applications and non-it applications. There's going to be applications. And they all are going to have inference integrated into those capabilities." – Matt Garman, CEO, AWS "what if the U.S government decides to turn me off and it's, you know, it's a question that many people have" – Matt Garman, CEO, AWS (on customer concerns regarding sovereign AI) "the more you can find, give your teams safe places to run fast. In a production setting, I think you'll benefit" – Matt Garman, CEO, AWS
What's the biggest gap you see between companies that are experimenting and companies that are actually starting to find successful deployments in production? What are you seeing is a big characteristic differential? I think one of the challenges that many companies had is that when they started doing proof of concepts with AI, they didn't actually have good success criteria defined at the beginning. It was just a learning journey. I'm sure many of you went on this where you had asked your teams to do hundreds of different experiments and roll them out, and you didn't really have a goal of what you were going to accomplish. Knowing what that is and helping define that is one of the first steps of really understanding which of those things to move to production. I have a fun example. I was at the JP Morgan Health Conference a couple weeks ago here in San Francisco. One of the big things that people were excited about was ambient listening, where you can have AI that would listen to the doctors and would automatically put the doctor notes into Epic and automatically send the information off to the insurance companies. People were excited about it. I met with one hospital leader who said, "I did all this stuff, and it's great. The doctors have more time at night to have dinner with their families. I haven't saved a single dollar. What am I getting out of this?" Literally, I haven't fired a doctor. Two hours later, I met with another hospital administrator who saw the exact same story. All these things are happening. He said, "I was really worried about attrition. There was risk that 30% of our nurses and doctors were going to attrite in the next 12 months, and now they have more free time with their families. And that attrition number has gone down. So I'm excited. I'm rolling it out across the hospital." Having that metric as to what the real value you want out of that, I think, is a huge thing that blocks companies because they initially say, "I'm not saving any money with this thing." And that may be true, but it may also not be the goal. What percentage of companies do you think have good, solid metrics that you feel are of the right level? It's often by the area they're rolling it out. When people are rolling it out for customer service, they usually have good metrics. When people are starting to roll it out with code as an example, which we heard you talking about earlier, there are lots of metrics like lines submitted and things like that. For the rest of the workforce, where it's a general productivity metric, people don't really have a good sense there. We'll get there, but I do think that that's part of it. The other thing that blocks people from rolling out, and this is a more fundamental piece, particularly with agents, is that as you get into generic workflows, people are worried about security. They're worried that the agents are going to go off and do things. They're worried about the sprawl of agents, they're worried about Agent Identity, and those things. They're worried about how you scale. They worry about, "I did a proof of concept, so I bought one EC2 instance with GPUs on it and I tested it. Now I want to roll it out around the world, but that GPU is relatively underutilized. How do I scale this? How do I think about that?" So I think just that general productizing of a proof of concept that people haven't really got their hands around how to do from a security, operational, and scaling point of view. You folks have been talking a lot about this notion of AI-first cloud. What does that actually mean? Talk to us a little bit about how AWS is thinking about it. We view that AI and inference, in particular, is going to be a critical part of every application in the world. So there aren't going to be AI applications and non-AI applications. There are going to be applications, and they all are going to have inference integrated into those capabilities. It's going to change every business and every industry. From our perspective, that means it has to be integrated into every piece of the AWS infrastructure. You have to be natively thinking about how your agents talk to your storage, how it's going to work inside of your VPC and your security settings, how you're going to think about permissions, how you're going to think about trade-offs and testing one model versus the other once it actually gets to production. We're building a platform called Bedrock. The idea is that we want a real platform that's going to have model choice, capabilities, and all the security that our customers expect inside a VPC to roll that out. We supplement that with the observation that all of our biggest partners in the world operate inside and on top of AWS, too. We open that up and make sure that every partner and every part of the AWS ecosystem can be a big part of what customers are building, because we don't think we're going to build every part of that. We know customers need that whole ecosystem to be successful in what they build. But you are building one of the high-margin products in that stack, which is the Trainium chips, which have seen a tremendous amount of success. Congratulations on that. Do you see the economics of AWS changing where the margin structure gets even better? If every application has inference and you're going to have inference capability on the stack that you have built out, does that improve your margins over time? It improves our margins versus only using Nvidia GPUs. They're always going to be a big part of that, but a big part of that margin goes to Jensen and the team. We love their products, and they execute awesomely. But when you have 70-80% gross margins, there's room in there for somebody to take less margin, have a higher performing part, and offer a choice to customers. We think we can offer better price performance for customers, choice for customers, so they can pick between the various options out there and have the richest set of capabilities that are unique to AWS that they can't get anywhere else. That's part of why we've invested in silicon for the last ten plus years now, and it's such a big part of what we deliver: differentiated performance all the way down to the silicon layer, which, together with our partners, offers a rich set of capabilities that people can build on. Trainium for us is a huge part of that, whether it ultimately allows us to increase margin or keep margins where they are and lower prices for customers. My guess is it's probably more of lowering prices, which we try to do to grow faster. We find that every time we lower prices, we generally pass them on to customers, and it gives customers more budget to build new workloads, which generally leads to a good flywheel for us. That's the Jevons paradox in full swing. Let's talk about constraints on infrastructure. We've talked about this. One of the challenges hyperscalers have is that it's not just about capital being allocated; it is literally physically time-consuming to build out the shell, get the permits, have the power, and make sure the entire stack gets built out. Do space data centers change the equation? Do you think space data centers will actually take away some of those constraints because solar is going to be 30% more efficient in space? Do you see that happening, or do you feel like we're still pretty far away from that? It all depends on your timeline. You're right. A big chunk of this is hard. Building some of these things are difficult. It turns out that people are excited that they can build software in a fraction of the time, but pouring concrete takes the same amount of time. Building buildings takes the same amount of time. We haven't yet built AI agents that can do that. Robots will eventually. But for space data centers, I think there are compelling ideas about a data center in space: infinite amount of power that's always available, easy cooling. I don't know if you've seen a rack of servers recently; they're heavy. Humanity has yet to ever build a permanent structure in space, on the moon, or anywhere like that. Maybe. Elon says he's going to launch a million satellites, but there are not enough rockets to launch a million satellites yet. So we're far from that. I think there will probably have to be improvements in the efficiency and the cost. If you think about the cost of getting a payload into space today, it's massive. It's not economical today. I think there is an interesting dynamic depending on where that cost curve goes. They're probably going to find cheaper rocket fuel. There are many people investing in making space transport much more economical, but that's a ways off. That does become a huge bottleneck in space: just the transport. It is absolutely the bottleneck today—the cost and availability of getting things into space. Let's think of latency concerns as well. Let's stick with Earth data centers for a second. Do you think that the definition of full stack for hyperscalers changes? Do you start getting more into the power business and the construction business because it feels like if you control the full stack, you would be able to accelerate this faster? If you ask me 20 years ago, AWS would be making chips, and I would have told you no, but here we are. The answer will be yes, almost for sure, because if you look at the economics of those industries, they don't scale with the level that we need them to scale. Utilities, as an example, for almost the history of time, have fixed and regulated pricing, which is a limiter to them. They get fixed return on their investment, so the whole industry is constructed to grow at 3-5% a year. It's hard to break them out of that, because why would they take a risk when they get low returns? Whether we own the power or we fund the power, I don't really want to operate power stations or things like that. That's definitely something I would like to not do. The whole system is much more efficient if it can be connected to the grid. There are probably some steps between here and there where you're going to have to do behind-the-meter power and other things like that, because we're going to have to fund that ramp-up while the world catches up. So, staying on that topic, we rely on people like Tariq to do it too. Let's say that you continue to make sure that you're building out the chips. What's the average cycle time of chips now that every 18 months you're getting a new version of the chip out? 18 or 24? It depends, but that's compressing. Somewhere between 18 to 24 months. Sometimes you're also limited on your process generations and things like that. You're also limited on how fast TSMC improves process generations. You're also limited on how fast you can actually get these systems into production. From the time you print your first chip to when you're really at scale for these large AI systems, it actually takes 9 to 12 months from first chip out to really, manufacturing is quite hard. But in general, you would agree that the cycle time has compressed. How do you do capacity planning at the scale of AWS? Does that equation change a little bit? You don't want a bunch of chips that you bought to become obsolete in 18 months, but you have to make sure that there's a long tail on how different workloads start using them. How does capacity planning work? There are a couple of things that play in our favor. It's a challenge, and it's part of the challenge that we take on so customers don't have to. One is because there is so much more demand than supply, there typically still is demand for the older chips. We are completely sold out and have never retired an A100 server, for example. That's because there's still demand for it. There are some structural reasons why every next generation. Part of where the industry today is getting so much gain on the AI side is reducing the floating point accuracy. I was talking to some customers recently, and they were saying, "I can't move to Blackwells; I have to use Haswell because I'm doing HPC-style calculations, and the precision is not enough for me." So they're in a hard place where the AMD new processors can still enable that. A lot of the world and some of the old applications rely on that level of precision. There are use cases where smaller models still run great on A100s and things like that. For some of these large labs, they're also willing to take five-year commitments for some of this capacity sometimes, too, which also mitigates some of our risk and pushes it on the customers potentially. We do a lot of planning here. We have to think about many time horizons that we have to plan for. For example, last year, we added just under four gigawatts of new capacity in AWS for the data centers. Data centers have a 20-30 year amortization timeline. The power requires long-term commitments, etc. So you have to think about data center planning for a long time because you're going to have this asset for 20 or 30 years. You think about servers, which you have for 5 or 6 years, networking gear, maybe six to ten years, different components. You have to think about all of those. We spend a ton of time thinking about supply chain and how we think about risk levels at all those levels of investment. Let's switch to AI coding. You were saying that you're seeing great results from that internally. Talk to us about what you're seeing working well and not working so well. We see that the thing that is giving massive acceleration, sometimes 10x or even 100x improvement, is when teams start from the beginning thinking about AI-driven coding. In fact, we have teams now where they have almost a mandate that they write no line of code. All they do is prompt the models or the systems. There are a couple of benefits you get from that. One is the AI systems build context around what they're building. They also know how all those things work together. They've also documented the code that's written, so you will start to get a real flywheel where it can then test all the code because it knows the documentation and the things it needs to test, etc., and has all that coverage. I will say where we're not there yet, and it's a "yet" because probably within the next 6 to 9 months we'll solve this problem, is that we have not yet seen that speed-up for complicated integrated legacy systems. They're not necessarily legacy in the sense of mainframe, but even a large distributed system that has a large codebase, say something like S3, which has been around for a little bit of time. We've reimagined it many times before, but it's not zero. We're not starting from scratch. Giving all that context to the models so that you can get the same speed-up, we still see some speed-up, but not quite the 10x to 50x speed-up. But I think we're building and thinking about some novel ways in which we can get coding systems to understand legacy code, and I think that's one of the areas that can help people start to get massive speed-up, which turns out most of the code out there is legacy code. We are seeing that the further down in the stack you go, the harder it gets. Memory management is much harder to do. Management and correctness of objects is a little bit different than an end-user application, where you say, "build me a website," which the agents are quite good at doing now. So when you start thinking about sovereign AI infrastructure and how countries are going nationalistic, they each have their own infrastructure. Do architectures like Outpost get even more interesting? I think this is an interesting topic. This was at Davos two weeks ago. Nearly every conversation I had with a European company was started with, "Look, we trust you. I don't know if I can trust your country." We try to walk them through why that's not the trade-off they should think about. They also realize that they can't tie their own hands behind their back and not compete with the world's best technology. So these companies are realistic, but they're having this struggle. I literally get the question of, "What if the U.S. government decides to turn me off?" It's a question many people have, and we assure them that's very unlikely to happen. It's a concern they have. Resilience almost trumps real intelligence in some ways. When you think about banking systems, telcos, or other areas like that, from a country perspective, the countries feel like they now need that lever, or they're at risk of us being a lever against them. So we're thinking about different mechanisms to solve this around the world. Just two weeks ago, we launched the EU Sovereign Cloud. For the EU, this is going to be fantastic, and your partners are launch partners. The idea behind the EU Sovereign Cloud is that it's a fully separate subsidiary that is incorporated inside the EU, beholden to EU laws, and has an independent governing board. We make sure that every bit of data, including metadata and account logins, all of that lives inside the EU region. We have even tested disconnecting that whole region from the AWS backbone to make sure that it works. We co-designed this with the BSI from Germany and other EU countries to make sure that they like this model. That works for the EU because it's such a large economy in and of itself. We are exploring different things like that to help companies around the world grapple with this, where there are obvious trade-offs, but they are hard questions, and we hopefully that's where we can help. Can you provide the CIO and the CSO audience some advice on what they need to do to accelerate adoption internally? I have an analogy I like to use: if you have a giant canyon with a board across it, you walk very slow across that board. You may crawl across it. But if you put handrails and guardrails up, you can run across it. It's weird because it's the same board. Part of what slows people down is that they're worried. We had examples internally where somebody was writing some code and asked the agent to do something, and the agent was about to delete some infrastructure because it thought that was the fastest way to do it. No, no, that will actually cause production issues if you do that. We have other controls, that wasn't the case, but it highlights that many companies are worried that if they just unleash agents into their enterprise, bad things will happen: production will go down, security issues will happen. What I would say is that we're trying to build building blocks for our customers so they can go fast. Things like Agent Core allow you to have some of these guardrails that let you go fast. The more you can give your teams safe places to run fast in a production setting, I think you'll benefit. Thank you. Thanks so much.

Ozempic Won't Solve America's Obesity Problem

a16z(245.0K) ·40:36 ·1.5K views · 2026-02-04
America is grappling with a severe chronic disease crisis, largely stemming from an environment and food system that systematically promotes unhealth, rather than individual failures, requiring urgent systemic changes in food policy, chemical regulation, and healthcare incentives for prevention.
TL;DR America is grappling with a severe chronic disease crisis, largely stemming from an environment and food system that systematically promotes unhealth, rather than individual failures, requiring urgent systemic changes in food policy, chemical regulation, and healthcare...

TL;DR

America is grappling with a severe chronic disease crisis, largely stemming from an environment and food system that systematically promotes unhealth, rather than individual failures, requiring urgent systemic changes in food policy, chemical regulation, and healthcare incentives for prevention. Justin Mares, CEO of Truemed, proposes re-engineering healthcare by allowing tax-free HSA/FSA spending on preventative lifestyle interventions like gym memberships and healthy food.

America's Chronic Health Crisis: Systemic Roots

  • Worsening Health Outcomes: US healthcare outcomes are dire and declining, with obesity and heart disease at record levels, primarily because the environment systematically outputs unhealthy people.
      • The average child spends less time outdoors than a maximum-security prisoner.
      • 70% of children's diets are ultra-processed foods.
  • The 1970s Shift: A significant decline in health began in the 1970s, driven by shareholder pressure on large food companies to optimize for earnings per share.
      • Ingredient Degradation: Companies replaced real ingredients (strawberries, sugar) with cheaper, artificial versions (strawberry flavoring, high-fructose corn syrup).
      • Ultra-Processed Foods: Today, the majority of diets consist of addictive, nutrient-depleted, ultra-processed foods containing novel chemical compounds never encountered in human evolution.
  • The Subsidy Problem: US crop subsidies for corn, soy, and wheat, totaling nearly $100 billion over the last decade, make these ingredients artificially cheap.
      • Pervasive Unhealthy Ingredients: This leads big food companies to use them in nearly everything, often replacing healthier alternatives (e.g., soybean oil instead of olive oil).
      • Inflammatory Impact: Soybean oil, a highly processed and inflammatory ingredient, now accounts for almost 20% of the average American's caloric intake.
      • Uniquely American: This extensive federal intervention in food subsidies is largely unique to the US, unlike Europe which favors local agriculture and stricter chemical regulations.

Beyond Pharmaceuticals: Prevention and Lifestyle as Healthcare

  • Ozempic is Not a Universal Solve: While GLP-1s could jumpstart health for the nearly 80% of Americans who are overweight or obese, they don't address fundamental food issues.
      • Nutrient Deficiencies: Relying solely on Ozempic without improving diet can lead to protein and micronutrient deficiencies.
      • Historical Precedent: No single intervention has ever solved all species-wide problems.
  • Truemed's Approach: Truemed allows qualified individuals to spend tax-free HSA/FSA dollars on lifestyle interventions that treat, reverse, or prevent disease.
      • Mechanism: Patients receive a "Letter of Medical Necessity" from a telemedicine provider, enabling them to use pre-tax funds for items like gym memberships (Peloton, Lifetime Fitness), sleep aids (Eight Sleep), healthy foods, and supplements.
      • Incentivizing Prevention: This aims to redirect hundreds of billions of dollars towards prevention, financially incentivizing people to invest in their long-term health, unlike the current system that pays millions to manage chronic disease post-onset.
  • Improved Food Guidelines: Recent updates to US food guidelines are a positive step, emphasizing whole foods, more vegetables, and well-sourced proteins, moving away from past recommendations that allowed high sugar for young children.

Policy Levers and Systemic Reform

  • National Security Crisis: The chronic disease crisis should be treated as a national security issue; if an adversary caused such widespread illness, the nation would mobilize immediately.
  • Key Policy Recommendations:
      • Fix Crop Subsidies: Eliminate subsidies for corn, soy, and wheat that make unhealthy ingredients artificially cheap.
      • Invest in Prevention: Mandate healthcare system investment in prevention, potentially through "health coaches" similar to Singapore's model.
      • Stricter Chemical Regulation: Adopt a European-style regulatory approach requiring rigorous safety testing for novel chemical compounds before market introduction, rather than the US "Generally Recognized As Safe" (GRAS) system.
          • The US has 60,000-80,000 chemical compounds not allowed in the EU.
          • Lobbying by companies like Monsanto (e.g., for glyphosate) hinders effective regulation.
      • Reform Food & Health Guidelines: Combat corporate influence on food guidelines, clean up school and military lunch programs (e.g., ending soda contracts in schools).
      • Streamline Drug Approval: Accelerate approval for innovative therapies targeting chronic diseases.
  • Consumer Choice vs. Public Health: While consumer choice is important, government should not subsidize unhealthy options (like high-fructose corn syrup) or allow corporations to unduly influence nutrition guidelines, especially concerning children's health.

Holistic Health: Mental Wellbeing and Emerging Therapies

  • Metabolic Psychiatry: Mental health is deeply tied to biological health.
      • Functional Medicine Approach: Addressing inflammation, gut health, sleep, diet, and exercise can be more effective for depression than traditional talk therapy.
      • Metabolic Roots: Many conditions categorized as mental health issues (epilepsy, depression, schizophrenia, bipolar disorder) have metabolic causes and can respond to dietary interventions like ketogenic diets.
  • Psychedelics for Mental Health: Psychedelics are powerful, underinvested interventions for mental health.
      • Efficacy: Ketamine therapy shows superior efficacy to SSRIs for treatment-resistant depression and PTSD, especially for veterans.
      • Therapeutic Potential: Should be viewed as vital mental health therapies with few side effects when doctor-supervised, moving beyond outdated stigmas.
  • Peptides: Disrupting Pharma: Peptides represent a class of compounds with significant potential to disrupt the pharmaceutical industry.
      • Human Enhancement: Unlike many traditional pharmaceuticals focused on disease management, peptides offer benefits like increased energy, improved sex drive, reduced inflammation, and better gut health.
      • Accessibility: They are generally not patentable and can be acquired affordably, making them a different category of health intervention.
  • Nutrition Science Reform: Current nutrition science is often compromised by industry funding.
      • N=1 Experimentation: Individuals should experiment with diets (e.g., Ray Peat's metabolism-boosting approach vs. caloric restriction for longevity) and use lab tests and wearables to track personal biomarkers and well-being.

Rethinking Health: Environment and Community

  • The Zoo Animal Theory: Human health is a direct reflection of our environment.
      • Mismatch: Just as zoo animals develop diseases not seen in the wild due to unnatural habitats, humans in modern environments develop chronic diseases.
      • Environmental Shaping: Focus should shift from individual discipline to shaping environments that are naturally health-promoting.
  • Community-Level Interventions: Improving health outcomes necessitates thinking beyond the individual to the collective environment.
      • Innovative Models: Exploring ideas like health-oriented cities, towns, or group health insurance for communities committed to lifestyle interventions could have a much larger impact than individual optimization.

I think that this wholesale swapping of taking the worst ingredients in their most highly processed form and replacing them with an ingredient that humans traditionally ate is one of the root causes of chronic disease. This is how you've heard of the forever chemicals and stuff like this got into our food system and basically are all over the planet now, because 3M invented them something like 60 years ago, and basically they just started using them.
