So I think there's, my idea was that I think there's a ton of room for a grocery store that is a more active participant in your health, telling you to eat certain things, tracking your macros, all these sorts of things that I think would solve a lot of problems for people. So, let's talk more about if we fully identified the health crisis as one of our main national priorities. What else would we do? Maybe we could start looking at it in the light of what were some of the pillars or main principles and maybe talk about where they are so far. I think that you have seen across all of healthcare, across all of food, corporate capture of our food guidelines, of our health guidelines, of all these sorts of things. The previous FDA commissioner's stated goal with running the FDA, their number one goal, was to combat misinformation. The current one is to make Americans healthy. That means GRAS reform, so cleaning up how we regulate chemicals. That means fixing school lunches, fixing military lunches, basically what we feed kids. 80% of schools have contracts with soda companies. These soda companies literally pay a school district to put these vending machines all over the schools. It helps fund schools, but it also comes at the cost of getting kids sick. So I think cleaning up school lunches, cleaning up military lunches, cleaning up the food guidelines. The second thing is improving and streamlining the way that we look at regulating drugs. Make it faster for companies to lean in and launch innovative therapies that can make a dent on the chronic disease crisis. And just trying to bring common sense back into the way that we think about health, the way that we think about food, and try and make a dent in the chronic disease crisis are some of these things. Are some of these ingredients that we're discussing, or foods, or soda so addicting or so dangerous or so appeasing that they should be outlawed? How do we think about that? No, I actually don't think that many of these things should be outlawed. I do actually think that consumer choice is a very important thing. But specifically with kids, we have guidelines, and we already don't think that kids should have free choice. We don't let them drink. So I think that there is a place for soda in this country. I just also think that if you want to drink soda all the time, we probably shouldn't be subsidizing it by subsidizing high-fructose corn syrup. We probably should not have Coca-Cola spending $140 million to influence our nutrition guidelines, as they have over the last 15 years. It's things like that where a lot of times the big food companies and people will fall back on this idea of consumer choice. I think it's very important, but we also are currently grappling with an incredibly important chronic disease crisis. If we don't take effort to uniquely address this thing, I think that the US is in an incredibly tough spot 10, 20, 30 years from now. I want to ask you about psychedelics. Is that in this discourse at all? Does that move outcomes? Is that not really? Not really. I think that if you just look at the data, psychedelics are an incredibly powerful intervention. Ketamine therapy, ketamine-assisted therapy, works better than SSRIs in many cases, especially for treating things that are very intense like treatment-resistant depression, PTSD, things like this. I think that for many of our veterans and others that suffer from PTSD and intense depression, psychedelics should be part of a menu of treatments that we are open to giving people. I think that we probably vastly underinvest in them. I think that there's a ton of really promising research around their efficacy and things like this. I think it's an open question of how ready the average American is to think about these things not as a group of people dropping acid in Golden Gate Park in the 60s, but as a vital mental health therapy for people that are really, really struggling. I'm very supportive of, again, just looking at psychedelics as another therapy that can address the many mental health and depression issues that we have in this country. If you look at it just as that, through that lens, a therapy that has few to no side effects, especially when done with doctor oversight and things like this, I think they should be part of how we treat and address many of the mental health crises that we see today. Are there any other really big levers that you would focus on if one of your main goals was to target the mental health crisis? I think that we are hugely underestimating the impact of biological health and how that is tied to mental health. There are some really interesting studies out there around taking a functional medicine approach where you come in and say you're depressed, and rather than doing talk therapy and things like this that statistics show don't work very well for many people, we focused just on reducing your inflammation and fixing your gut. There have been studies that show just by focusing and taking a functional medicine approach, focusing on sleep, focusing on gut health, focusing on lowering your inflammation through diet and exercise, that combination of therapies works better than just talk therapy and things like that. So I think that we massively underestimate the degree to which we are human beings, biological organisms, and our mental health is very tightly coupled to our physical health. So I think that we should fund much more research along these lines of what's called metabolic psychiatry, which is basically this idea that many diseases that we put in the mental health bucket, things like epilepsy, depression, schizophrenia, many of these things have causes that are actually metabolic in root. For example, one of the best therapies for someone that is schizophrenic or epileptic, or even in many cases bipolar, if you put them on a ketogenic diet, these things will in many cases just resolve. That is not a thing that our current mental health world really underwrites. It reminds me of a conversation I once had with Daniel Gross when he was saying, "Hey, anytime someone is feeling depressed, it would behoove them, before attributing it to some psychological defect or making some deep psychoanalysis about it, first just saying, 'Am I sleeping well? Am I eating well? Am I exercising?'" Totally. "Have I been outside recently?" Yes. Totally. I want to shift lastly to another topic that you've just been really curious about for a while, which is consciousness. What has driven your hunch or curiosity there, and where has your thinking drawn you? I think that I have just been interested in this idea of what does it mean to be healthy? What does it mean to live a life that people are excited about, have energy with, and are just... what does it mean to feel great in your body and your mind and everything? And I think that consciousness is this root layer of what is your experience of being alive, of life. And frankly, we don't really have a great answer to this right now. A lot of our theories and philosophies of what is consciousness are not great. They're not predictive. You ask the average person, you ask even philosophers that have thought a lot about this, and there's nothing near consensus. So I don't—it is an area that I'm very interested in. I think we're going to learn a lot about in the next decade. I hope to have friends and play somewhat of a role in exploring and learning more about what consciousness is, how to change it, how to improve it, all these sorts of things. But yeah, I would put it very much in the personal pet interest bucket. Not like I'm doing anything around it right now. Well, you're obviously focused on building True Med, we'll close there. But if you could scale yourself infinitely, for people listening who are also obsessed with this problem, is there something else you think is underexplored, whether it's a research area or a company idea or some sort of policy thing that not enough people are looking into that we haven't yet discussed that you want more people to spend? An idea I've been thinking about recently is, and we mentioned at the beginning, that the health of an organism is the health of your environment. In my view, what that means is that if we started thinking about not improving just Eric's health, but improving the health of all of your friends and community and people around you that exist in the same environment, intervening and improving your environment could have a much larger impact on health than just optimizing your own health. So I think that there are potentially interesting models of improving one's environment, whether that's building a health-oriented city or town or suburb development or something like that. Whether that's group health insurance for people that are deciding to live differently and have different interventions and invest in root cause lifestyle interventions. I think there's a lot of interesting things that could come about when you start thinking about health through the lens of a community or tens, hundreds of people, not just through the lens of me as an individual. So, if I had copies of myself, I would explore companies in that direction. I love that. Let's close on True Med and go deeper into it in the sense that the company's done phenomenally. We're very lucky to be a part of it. Say more for people who are not, in terms of the actual product and the direction in terms of where it's going. Sure. True Med, basically what it is, is for people that qualify, we allow them to spend tax-free HSA and FSA dollars on things that treat, reverse, and prevent disease that fall within the lifestyle intervention category. We're partners with Eight Sleep, we're partnered with Peloton, we're partnered with Momentous, Lifetime Fitness. All of these brands that, for someone that is working to treat, reverse, or prevent heart disease or obesity or things like this, these lifestyle interventions are healthcare. So we've built a payment integration that allows people to spend their tax-free HSA dollars on these interventions and are scaling that up. I think that where this can go over the next decade is every American that is at risk or has some sort of chronic condition should be incentivized, and tax-free money is a great incentive, to spend their own money, their dollars, on things that treat, reverse, or prevent disease. There are no mechanisms in today's healthcare system for someone to actually spend, for someone to get a gym membership for cheaper than the list price, or to get a gym membership covered by their employer, by an insurance provider. So that's kind of the way that I think about what we're building is building infrastructure that allows people and incentivizes them to invest in lifestyle interventions to stave off and prevent different chronic conditions. And I actually have to ask, what are your thoughts on peptides and any predictions as it relates to that? Oh man, I think peptides are going to be so disruptive to our current healthcare system. I think that if you look at peptides, there needs to be way more research. I'm not totally yet a peptide believer. But there needs to be way more research, but from early signs and even just N-of-1 people taking these things and using them, this seems like a class of compounds that are very effective. They are not patentable. You can basically buy these for fairly cheap. I would put them in the bucket of human enhancement companies. Our current healthcare system and most of our pharmaceuticals are kind of like, "Don't die," "Oh, here's a statin, don't die, take this pill." I think that these peptides are in a different class where many of them give you more energy, improve your sex drive, lower your inflammation, improve your gut health. They're just a different category of compound. I think that as many people start to experiment with them and try them and things like this, you're going to see really interesting positive healthcare outcomes from a class of compounds that no one has thought about for a long period of time. I think it's going to be exceptionally disruptive to pharma and to a bunch of other players in the healthcare system. And I'm also curious for you or just collectively, are we learning more about optimizing our nutrition? You got into the Ray Peat diet, or you were writing about it. What are your thoughts there more broadly? I think nutrition science is probably among the worst of the sciences in the US right now. Most of nutrition science is funded by a big food company. They outfund the NIH, 11 to 1 on nutrition science, at least historically. That's hopefully changing. I think that there are all of these different diet tribes out there. The Peaters, the Ray Peat crowd, I think, is one that's particularly interesting that's focused on ramping up your metabolism. But I think that for many people, you should just try and experiment with the diet where you feel good. We now have lab tests that you can get via Function or something to show how this diet impacts your energy, your biomarkers, things like this. You have wearables that can actually give you feedback around how is your sleep, how's your HRV, how's your resting heart rate, since switching your diet or since doing things differently. I think that that combination of getting insight into how these things are actually making you feel and how they're impacting your biomarkers means that people should be way more open to experimenting and seeing, "Hey, I'll try a Peat diet for 90 days and just see how I feel." So I'm very bullish on people doing N-of-1 dietary exploration and experimentation. And I think that the Peat community is probably the most interesting one out there for me right now. And why is that? Because there are two schools of thought, basically. There's the Bryan Johnson school of thought, which is, "We've done all the research. We know that you need to eat a plant-based diet. We know that you need to do caloric restriction all in the name of longevity." That is the current dogma in many ways. The Peaters are the exact opposite. Bryan Johnson would say sugar is bad. You should not eat carbohydrates, all this. The Peaters say you should eat as much sugar as you possibly can, mostly in the form of honey and fruit. You should try and hugely avoid seed oils, hugely avoid things that are going to impair your metabolism. But if Bryan Johnson says, "I want to eat less and ramp down my metabolism, it means I can live longer," the Peaters say, "Ramp it up as much as you can." The more energy you have, the longer you are able to use that energy over a long period of time. So it is such a contrarian view to 100 years of nutrition dogma that says sugar is bad, should be avoided, that I think it's a very interesting idea, and a lot of the people in the Peat community have seen phenomenal results. I've started drinking orange juice and eating fruit in the morning, and I feel great, and my biomarkers have gotten even better from, I've been doing probably a decade plus of mostly low-carb, paleo. So it's just a very interesting, different lens that is worth playing with. No, essentially, I'll have to check out the Peaters. Bryan Johnson's obviously been so interesting on so many topics. One question I was curious, if I get a girlfriend, should I make a launch video? Justin, the company is True Med. Thank you so much for coming on the podcast. Thanks for having me. Lucky to be involved.

Executive Briefing: The Future of European Banking & UniCredit's Transformation

TL;DR: Andrea Orcel, CEO of UniCredit, emphasizes that European banks must rapidly achieve scale and undergo deep digital transformation, driven by a client-centric approach, significant AI adoption, and a culture of empowerment and grit, to thrive in a volatile environment and deliver outstanding shareholder returns.

1. Challenges and Evolution of European Banking

  • Biggest Challenges:
      • Scale: Essential for innovation and investment in technology.
      • Transformation: Crucial as legacy banks and fintechs converge, requiring banks to match client experience and efficiency while leveraging their existing client base and product complexity.
  • Competition and Market Structure:
      • Current State: Local authorities often block market shares exceeding 25% to ensure competition, but this can hinder banks from offering clients what they need.
      • Optimal Structure: Andrea Orcel advocates for a "midpoint" – enough competition to push banks to be better, but sufficient scale to meet client demands.
      • Need for Stronger Banks: Europe requires robust financial services ("gasoline") to finance its industry transformation and drive growth, but current regulatory frameworks are often restrictive.
  • Evolving Business Model:
      • Financial Targets: The industry is becoming more appealing but demands higher efficiency. Targets have shifted from 50% Cost-Income Ratio (CIR) and 12-13% Return on Equity (ROE) a decade ago, to aspiring for 40% CIR and ROE in the high teens (18%+) today.
      • Rethinking Everything: Achieving these targets requires a complete overhaul of organization, processes, ways of working, and technology.

2. UniCredit's Strategic M&A Approach

  • M&A as a Strategic Tool: Andrea Orcel views M&A as a disciplined tool to accelerate strategy, not an end in itself, emphasizing careful execution and alignment with the company's vision.
  • Commerzbank Stake (2024):
      • Initial Rationale: The 5% stake purchased from the German government strategically aligned with strengthening UniCredit's position in Germany and Poland, targeting SMEs and affluent clients.
      • Shifting Landscape: Due to changes in government alignment and market dynamics, UniCredit opted to convert derivatives into a 30% financial stake, deciding against an immediate full bid.
      • Current Outcome: The stake is financially successful, generating approximately €800 million annually post-tax, with future potential to create a truly pan-European bank across Germany, Italy, Austria, and CEE.
  • Banco BPM Bid:
      • Sequential Strategy: The bid was launched once a full bid for Commerzbank was deemed not in the foreseeable future, making it a manageable "one-boxer" situation.
      • Italian Consolidation: Participating in Italian market consolidation, a key home market, was deemed essential for shareholders.
      • Financial Discipline: The terms were financially aligned, offering an expected 15% Return on Investment, strengthening UniCredit's Italian business.

3. Leveraging Technology for Client-Centric Transformation

  • Revolutionizing Client Experience:
      • The "Netflix Test": Banks must provide the same seamless, intuitive experience as leading digital platforms, or risk obsolescence within five years.
      • Client at the Center: UniCredit "flipped the organization" to be client-centric, designing processes from the client's perspective.
      • Accelerated Services: Reduced consumer loan approval from 7 days to 13 minutes (or instant for pre-approved clients); aiming for mortgage approvals within a week.
      • Personalized Offerings: Utilizing data to proactively offer relevant products, e.g., travel insurance for a client booking a trip.
  • Impact of Artificial Intelligence (AI):
      • Cost Reduction: AI is set to massively reduce costs and is now a critical focus for European banks.
      • Process Transformation: AI's true power lies in transforming workflows, not just easing employee tasks.
      • Credit Analysis Example: A pilot AI engine reduced the preparation of a complex corporate credit file from 6 weeks for an experienced officer to 14 minutes with 98% accuracy.
      • Reskilling Imperative: With 80-90% of roles in areas like file preparation potentially disintermediated, UniCredit has a reskilling unit (650-700 employees since late 2022) to transition staff to new, often client-facing, roles.
  • Stablecoins and Blockchain:
      • Strategic Priority: Blockchain is seen as a key technology for modernizing financial markets by eliminating intermediaries.
      • Euro-Backed Stablecoin: UniCredit joined the Kalis consortium to launch a euro-backed stablecoin by Q3 2024, countering the dominance of USD-backed stablecoins which could lead to Europe's financial disintermediation on blockchain platforms.

4. Leadership, Culture, and Grit at UniCredit

  • Navigating Volatility: The current era of volatile interest rates, geopolitics, and rapid technological change demands a new management style focused on flexibility and continuous adaptation.
      • Empowered Teams: Leaders must set a clear direction, but empower sub-teams to constantly adjust plans to reach shared goals.
      • Cultural Shift: This requires immense trust, transparent communication, and a culture where failure within defined metrics is viewed as a learning opportunity, not a punishable offense.
  • Importance of Grit: Andrea Orcel's leadership is rooted in "grit" – determination, resilience, and the ability to learn from setbacks and pick oneself up. He believes "the impossible is possible" through consistent effort.
  • Rebuilding UniCredit's Culture:
      • Inherited DNA: UniCredit had a "winning DNA" in its 69,000 employees but had experienced a period of retrenchment. Orcel focused on re-empowering this existing drive rather than imposing a completely new culture.
      • Core Values: Through extensive interviews with employees, three core values were identified: Integrity, Ownership, and Care.
      • Empowerment Model: Shifted from a top-down command-and-control structure to a highly empowered, bottom-up organization, guided by shared vision, strategy, and KPIs. This ensures decisions are made with common principles and values, fostering confidence in outcomes.
  • Leadership Principles:
      • "Start by making your own bed": Do small things well before aiming for big accomplishments, maintaining humility and attention to detail.
      • Listen and Engage: Leaders must spend time with their teams, listen to their ideas (often found "in the trenches"), and be humble enough to accept challenges as opportunities for growth.
      • Drive for Excellence: Inspire teams to push beyond perceived limits, fostering self-improvement and aiming for the "possible" rather than just the "medium."
  • Capital Allocation Discipline: UniCredit prioritizes deploying capital to areas yielding the best returns and actively works to improve performance in lower-return businesses. This drives "quality growth" – profitable expansion that defends margins and leverages unique competitive advantages.

Notable Quotes

"Scale and transformation. I think they combine scale because without scale you don't have enough innovation. You don't have enough ability to invest in technology and in all the things that you should invest to then transform." — Andrea Orcel, CEO of UniCredit "If you have a session on Netflix or on some other of those platform that we're so accustomed to be on, and then for any reason you need to switch off that and move on your bank. Do you have the same experience? Because if you don't you will not survive in five years." — Andrea Orcel, CEO of UniCredit "AI changes that accelerating things. So, number one, what AI does and this is what everybody's focused on is it reduces cost massively." — Andrea Orcel, CEO of UniCredit "I believe that the impossible is possible... if it's impossible be more creative and you will make it possible." — Andrea Orcel, CEO of UniCredit
Welcome to In Good Company. Today, I'm in particularly good company because I'm here with Andrea Orcel, who is also called the Ronaldo of banking. Andrea has truly left his mark on the European banking industry. He's now at the helm of UniCredit, and so many exciting things are going on in his business and in his life. So, a warm welcome. Thank you very much. I'm very happy to be here. What is the biggest challenge that the European banking industry is facing just now? Scale and transformation. I think they combine. Without scale, you don't have enough innovation, nor the ability to invest in technology and all the things you should invest in to then transform. And transformation, because we tended to look at legacy banks on one side and fintech on the other. We observed them but didn't fully consider their impact. But if you take the next five to seven years, they are going to converge. Therefore, you need to ask yourself, can I offer the same level of client journey, the same level of client experience, with the same level of efficiency, and beat them on what they don't have? That means primary clients, which we start with, and secondly, the ability to deal with multiple products and complexity. There's been a focus on having a lot of competition in each country, right? Ideally, the competition authorities have wanted to have a lot of local competition in each country. Is that changing now? Well, I think local authorities have wanted local competition, and the European Union competition authorities are looking at that. By and large, if you get close to 25% market share in any area, you get blocked. That is to prevent anyone from reducing that level of competition. I don't think that is changing. Actually, you have some markets, Germany being one of them, where the market shares are much lower than everywhere else. And it's like everything in life, it's a balance. Too much market share and too much size do not support competition and good service. Too little market share and too little size prevent you from offering clients what they want. So what is the best for clients, what is the best market structure? I think a midpoint. For me, it's a midpoint where you have enough competition. Depending on the market and the client segment, it changes, but I do agree by and large that once you move over 20% market share in any place, you need to start asking yourself if there is enough competition, because competition pushes you to be better. Lack of competition pushes you to sit still. So, is there now too little competition in Switzerland, for instance, after the merger of UBS and Credit Suisse? Some people could argue that, and it depends again on which market segments. If you take the entire industry from a retail market standpoint, for individuals, I think they're okay, given all the banks that they have. When you start moving on to small and mid-caps and large caps—the corporate sector—that level of competition declines because of how strong UBS is with Credit Suisse facilities. Do we need stronger banks in Europe to make Europe more competitive? Look, the parallel I always use is, if industry is the engine, financial services, banks, and capital markets are the gasoline. So now Europe faces two challenges. One, it is trying to transform itself because we have never been faced with such a challenge in terms of the structure of our industry and the leadership of our products. I mean, if you travel to Spain or Italy today, in Spain, it's an extreme: most car dealerships from European producers are empty, and most car dealerships from Chinese producers are full. For us as Europeans, this is a shock because we had the leadership in that. There are other industries where Germany, Italy, or France had the leadership that we don't have anymore. We need to transform that. In addition, if you look at Europe over the last 15 years, we've always been lagging the growth of the US and China, and we need that growth. So, to do those two things, you need new policies, and then you need to finance them. I think some countries feel they can finance them as they have done so far, through government budgets. But government budgets are not enough. They need the leverage of banks and capital markets and our investors to be able to do that transformation and financing. And I do think that, at the moment, the environment, the regulation, the framework in which we operate does not allow us to do that. Andrea, not many people work with what they studied at university. At university, you wrote your thesis on hostile takeovers. Yes, I did. And in September 2024, you acquired a big stake in Commerzbank. Yes. What was the thinking behind that? I've been working all this time in the same area. Maybe I'm not as creative as people think. I am one of those very lucky individuals who identified early what they love to do, which is takeovers. I would say, at the beginning, for sure, I used to say that if I wanted to be a bean counter, I would have gone into management, and now I am in management. But at university, I was reading so much about hostile takeovers and the attacks on Nabisco and all these things that I wanted to do the same thing. I convinced a professor, who was awesome, to let me do a thesis on something that in Italy was not even understood. Then I got myself through doing exactly what I wanted. I never regretted it. But in life you evolve, and then your job evolves, and that's where I am now. On Commerzbank, there are a lot of questions about that. Usually, M&A is viewed as an end in itself. I think after 35 years of M&A, it can be a fantastic tool to strengthen a company and catapult it further, but it can also be a doom or a bad thing if you don't do it in a disciplined fashion. M&A is a tool for your strategy. Your strategy decides what it is. You have a vision, a plan, a strategy, and you're doing it organically. M&A can accelerate it if it fits and if it's done at the right terms. How does Commerzbank fit into all this? How can I put it? The night before announcing the famous 5% stake that we bought from the German government, it was something that totally fit into our strategy. Germany—we believe in Germany. Poland. We want to strengthen that area from a client standpoint. SMEs—this is our bread and butter across Europe. Affluent clients—bread and butter across Europe. And we felt we had implicit support from the government and implicit support from the then leadership of Commerzbank. But that changed in the morning. We woke up to a very different world. We were discussing before this call about volatility and change. Every morning you wake up and find a different reality. We found a different reality. At that point, the choice for us—and this is one characteristic of M&A—was: one, not go forward and therefore reverse course. We felt at the time that would be an admission that we had done something incorrect, and we didn't think we had. Two, stay as is and bob around. Or three, get to the maximum we could get without launching a bid and then sit and see how the situation would map. We chose the third course. That's exactly what we did. Between the end of 2024 and the end of 2025, in my opinion and in the opinion of my board, it has been a success. Today, that stake will generate close to 800 million euros a year post-tax, growing, assuming they make their plan. And perhaps in an undefined period of time, there will be the possibility to convert that into a combination that would, in my opinion, make us the most truly pan-European bank in Europe because east of "Tin" would have market share everywhere, in leadership across Germany, Italy, Austria, and VE. But why did the Germans change their mind? I usually have learned in life to focus on what I control and not to speculate on what others do. I would say the following, though: one of the things that have changed, and many things have changed in Europe, is that we now almost always deal with coalitions. Coalitions are not uniform. This happens in Germany, it happens in Italy, it happens in Spain—I can name almost every country where I am active. They're coalitions. I would say that the part of the government we were talking to at the time was much more liberal and market-friendly. The rest of the coalition was less so. And maybe within the government, they were less aligned than one would have thought. When everything came to bear, and there was a lot of noise about it, they took a majority position that was against the position one side had taken vis-à-vis. And then quite soon afterwards, you launched a bid for Banco BPM. Yes, we did. So you're a boxer. Does it make sense to box against two people at the same time? For us, there were two things or the following consideration. The first consideration is that by the time BPM emerged, we were absolutely convinced that while we would convert our derivatives and everything else into a 30% stake, we would not launch a bid in Commerzbank in the foreseeable future. So while there was all the financial planning of getting to 30% and converting the stakes, there was no integration, nothing industrial. We would just sit on a financial participation. So it wasn't boxing with two; it was boxing with one. And then we said, if we launch BPM now, by the time we close the transaction and then integrate it, we will be able to go on to the second. Because in Italy, differently from Germany, we felt – obviously our evaluation did not materialize – we would close the transaction in nine months, integrate it in eleven. And this is one of the advantages of UniCredit: we are active in 13 markets. So 13 legal entities, 13 boards, and all related structures. The Italian leadership team would run it; the other 12 markets would not even notice. And sequentially, if the conditions were then right, we would be able to box the second boxer, as you call it, in another market, with another team, with another framework, and that was possible. So, one, it was that. Two, frankly, for our shareholders, we could not not at least try to participate in Italian consolidation, which is one of our two main markets, if it was going on. We couldn't just say, "Well, I'm busy there, so I haven't done that, and the market has consolidated away from me." At least we needed to try, and therefore we tried. And these were the reasons. At the time, we had waited for three and a half years. The terms, the financial terms, were aligned. So we could do a transaction which, at the time, even if we reviewed it up for a premium, we could bring in and tell our shareholders, "Strategically, it is because you always wanted me to do it, and term-wise, it could return on investment 15%, which at the time was about two points above my hurdle rate to adjust for risks, and it would strengthen our business in Italy." So that's why we brought it in. When you look into the future, do we need banks in their current forms? I think, as we started with this, banks will morph, moving into new forms. The business model is changing, the way we do business is changing, translated into financial terms. Maybe 10 years ago, you saw commercial banks, legacy banks, with a 50% cost-income ratio. They were solid, they were good, with 12-13% return on equity. They were solid, they were good. Today, the same terms would be 40%. In my opinion, you need a 40% cost-income ratio. And the return on equity, which was about 12%, I think today you need to have an ambition to be in the high teens, so 18% and above. So the industry, if you want to look at it positively, is becoming more appealing, but it requires rethinking how you do things. If you're not sitting back and continuing to operate on the business model you did 50, 30, or 20 years ago, you need to rethink the organization, the process, the way of working, the technology, the AI, and change, and change, and change. So when you then take UniCredit and you think about UniCredit 10 years from now, what does it look like? What is it? Well, if you look at it from a client standpoint—and for a second, let me consider individuals and families—I always say to our people: if you have a session on Netflix or some other platform that we're accustomed to, and then for any reason you need to switch off that and move to your bank, do you have the same experience? Because if you don't, you will not survive in five years. So the client experience needs to evolve to what we now consider the requirement, the bar, the expectation in many other facets of our life, and that requires a complete rethink of the way we operate, especially on the client journey. Tell us how you're going to change it. I think it's block by block. Obviously, banks are regulated, so I can't just change it; it needs to make sense, and the regulator needs to approve it. But for instance, in my opinion, you start – and everybody says they do that, but they don't – the client needs to be at the center. You need to flip the organization. That's what we did. The client needs to really be at the center of everything you do. So when you think about the way you onboard a client, when you think about how you grant a loan to a client, you need to start by thinking how the client will react to that. I'll give you a very concrete example: consumer finance. You go into a shop, you want to buy something at UniCredit. If you were not pre-approved, and we didn't pre-approve many people, it would take seven days. Is that an effective way to support a client? It's not. Today, it takes 13 minutes. If you're not approved beforehand, and most of our clients are pre-approved anyway, so it doesn't take 13 minutes; they know their limit, they can spend, and they can go. To do that, it means my process, which was 23 steps, needs to become two. My credit models need to be online all the time to assess the risk of that particular client segment, and so on. My product needs to be targeted to what they want, and I need to be in a position to react that quickly, because otherwise I can do it fast, but everything will blow up at the back. To do that, the entire thinking of how we do consumer finance has changed top to bottom. It has taken us three years. And the same will apply to mortgages. For example, as a client, you don't need an answer in seven minutes, but you do want an answer in a week. If you are really keen to buy a house, you want to know if you have financing or not in a reasonable period of time to move forward. And I can combine that. Let me give you another example. I give your consumer loan in seven minutes, and I know from your spending that you're buying a trip to Egypt. Do I offer you flight insurance, travel insurance in general? Do I offer you discounts at my clients who are all supermarket chains, or whatever, to get boots or the right casual wear? Can I do that? Because if I can do that, then I have financed your dream, and I am suggesting other things to complete the experience. How will AI change this further? AI changes that by accelerating things. What AI does, and what everyone is focused on, is massively reducing costs. But are you seeing that already, or is it still to come? I think, honestly, certainly for Europe—I'm not in the US—most banks, with very, very few exceptions if any, if you asked that question at the end of 2024, they would tell you something about AI, but the reality is it was a curiosity. If you ask them today, or at the end of 2025, they all talk about it, they all know it's a big, critical item, but now the question is how do we harness it? The first reaction you see in many places is, and I read it all the time, "I gave Copilot to every one of my employees, I sent everybody to a course, I did this." But that AI, while it will make the life of your employee easier, is that changing the way you work and is that transforming and extracting synergies? Oh, that's what I wanted to say. So now some banks, and some are ahead of us, but we're trying to catch up quickly. If you look at a credit file, it's very simple. You're a big company; you want a loan from us. I need to gather information from your accounts, from the internet, from a number of sources, create a credit file with all that data, reclassify it into my spreadsheets, and then apply my metrics and tell you if you fit or if you don't. I'm simplifying, but that's it. Usually, it takes six weeks for one experienced credit officer to do that from scratch to the end, if all the data is available. We did a pilot together with one of our partners. We developed an AI engine to do the same. It took us one week to develop the engine, and they could do it—on one file that had taken us six weeks to prepare—in 14 minutes with 98% accuracy. And this is the first trial; it will become 100% very, very quickly. So what does that tell you? It tells you that if I take the credit process of a bank, 80%, if not 90%, of the people in the large caps prepare files. So what are you going to do? Because now you are eliminating 90% of the people. The way of working changes. You need to manage the social impact. In many countries where I am, I cannot do that if I don't reskill them. So it's progressive. So what do you do? For example, since the end of 2022, we've started a reskilling unit. We've reskilled about 650-700 people who are not leaving, but who are good professionals whose areas are getting disintermediated. So what do you reskill them to do? To move to other things where we need more of them. In my opinion, banks have curtailed too much of the human touch for the client. So I may want everything online, but if I'm taking a mortgage of 250k, I want to see a person in front of me. So do you think banks generally will not reduce costs as much as people think? I think if you don't, in five or six years, you won't be here. Do you get people to change as quickly as you thought they would change? I have been pleasantly surprised at UniCredit. Okay. But it's not simply getting on a podium, doing a presentation that some very intelligent consultant has prepared for you, pontificating for an hour, and then stepping off expecting everyone to do it. It doesn't work that way. Firstly, you need to co-opt them into realizing that this is going to happen with them or without them. So the choice is, if we do it at the beginning, we have time to reskill you, we have time to change, and we can manage our pace. If you wait to hit the wall, you'll be out of a job because this is happening one way or another. It is happening. Once you have that conviction, then how do you manage, and over how long, to manage the balance between being fast enough but, on the other hand, not doing things that trade unions and honestly society will not allow you to do? Can that be done? I think it can be done. We have done it again and again at UniCredit, and I find that over time, if you provide alternatives and if you treat people correctly, the outcome is more positive than people expect. Another technology change we are seeing is, of course, stablecoins, blockchain, and so on. How is that going to fit into your business? Well, if you had asked me three years ago, I would have said I don't really know. Today, I think it is a priority. If you look at the modernization of markets – whether it's bonds, equity, contracts for mortgages, or anything else – it's just a change in technology, eliminating intermediaries, blockchain. That's what it is. You need to be at the forefront of it. And the other thing is, if you're on blockchain, you need a means of payment. It's either a cryptocurrency, which we only get involved in to the extent that our clients want to do it, because we think it's too speculative for a commercial bank like us. So we support it, but we're not using it. Stablecoin is fundamentally more stable. That's why we joined a consortium of banks in Europe, Calis, to launch a stablecoin based on euros, because that's another threat for Europe at the moment. If you look at stablecoins, they are predominantly US dollar-backed. That would mean that if you go on blockchain and you need a means of payment, you're risking to be completely disintermediated by the US dollar. And the Americans love stablecoins because what is backing them is the dollar and US Treasuries. And when will we have a European stablecoin? We are aiming to launch a first stablecoin, meaning that it's working, by the third quarter of this year. Are there any other trends in financial markets which make you nervous or worried? No, I do think maybe one thing, which we were discussing at the beginning. If you come from having lived in the pre-COVID era, especially in Europe, you'd be talking about stable and low interest rates, a very low cost of risk – defaults that are very low because interest rates are so low. You'd be talking about very limited to no inflation. You could look at the next three years, and very easily, you'd be within one standard deviation in terms of projection. You'd budget, break down the budget into pieces, have processes, tell everybody what to do, and track that. Today, it's diametrically opposite. I remember we launched UniCredit Unlocked in December 2021. Our stock price reacted very positively to a very detailed plan. It had taken us nine months to do it. A month later, Russia invaded Ukraine, and the entire plan went in the bin. Since then, it's been the same. We have volatile interest rates, volatile geopolitics, and technology and AI are changing the way we work. All the assumptions we make, we wake up the next morning and they are changing because we see new things that we didn't expect. So now, in my opinion, to run a company and to compete, you need to understand the general direction of travel and have such a deep understanding across your organization, top to bottom, of your vision, strategy, and plan. This direction of travel allows these sub-teams – be it country or subsets in countries, retail, corporate, etc. – to constantly adapt and rejig the original plan to achieve the same direction, but while adapting to a changing environment. The style of management is diametrically opposite because you're constantly looking at how they have adjusted and seeing if it's going in the right direction, rather than monitoring if they are applying what you thought they would apply at the beginning. That adjustment is absolutely critical. Unless the volatility we see around us goes down, and I don't think it will, this is a completely different way of running things. If you don't do that, the repercussions are very difficult. But to do that, the level of empowerment, the level of trust, the culture that gels together your individuals, the understanding of where we're going, why we're going, and what levers they can use, and the comfort of your institution that if they fail within the metrics you gave them, it's okay – they just need to flag it, regroup, and start rather than facing penalties – is massive. This is a cultural shift that for banks, which are very sclerotic, is very difficult to do. How important is grit in your life? Very important. It is probably at the essence of what I have always believed. Not everybody is equal; some people are born more gifted than others, and we see that every day. But by and large, in a majority of situations, people at a certain level, maybe not the most talented in the class, learn from failure and learn how to deal with it. They learn how to learn from failure, how to pick themselves back up, and how to go further. We share a favorite book, 'Grit' by Angela Duckworth. We actually had Angela Duckworth on the podcast, so you listened to it. Where did grit start for you? You didn't have a particularly hard upbringing, right? You were a middle-class kind of guy, right? Well, first of all, I went through the French Lycée, and still today the French Lycée is very Cartesian, and the style of teaching – I don't know today – but in my times, it was very hard. You needed to deserve the place you had at that chair, at that desk, and therefore it taught you to regroup and do better, because otherwise you wouldn't survive that kind of style of teaching. But in general, it suits me. Why? Because I believe that the impossible is possible. Somebody told me that the impossible is impossible until it's done, or, if it's impossible, be more creative and you will make it possible. I think human beings are here to improve themselves, and grit is what tells me that I may start behind, but through determination, resilience, consistency, and the ability not to take a challenge or setback as a disaster. That does not mean you don't react badly; you do. But then at some point you say, "Right, it happened. Why did it happen?" Pull yourself out, start again. You evolve and you become a much better individual in whatever you set your mind to do. And for me, going through this life and thinking, if I'm determined, if I really want it, if I work at it, I will be able to get there with a lot of effort. Then it's a journey that is worth going through. And that's, for me, at the base of a lot of things that I believe. You speak most of the European languages. Not German, but I do Portuguese. My wife is Portuguese. At least I understand that you would tell me that I speak a derivative of Spanish, but I do speak Spanish. I do speak French, Italian, English, and a little bit of Portuguese. What does this kind of pan-European background do to your thinking? It's actually quite interesting, and my wife and my daughter always point that out. I still count in French. If there is a mathematical problem or I need to correlate things and I speak aloud to hear myself on the logic, it's always French. I do presentations in English because I've been here for 35 years, and therefore all my professional life has been in English. I enjoy life and enjoy communicating in Spanish when I am on holidays. So they have marked different aspects of my life and they shape what I am altogether. What kind of temperament or psyche do you have? Where is that from? You can probably call it determined, ambitious, even dysfunctional. For me, it's that. For example, a lot of people kept on asking me about work-life balance, and I think that statement assumes that work is bad and you need something else. Totally. And if I am doing the job I chose with the people I like, and I am on a journey that is exciting, it's almost like it's not work. I'm actually enjoying the journey with the people I'm doing it. So I will naturally tilt towards more work, and I do think that also these things move in life. If you had known me when I was 25, yes, I was working a lot. Today, I'm still working a lot, but because I have a daughter who is 15, my priority has moved. So this work-life balance for me is choices. But what is a lot? What is it to work a lot? Well, I think when I was 25, I still went through the boot camp of investment banks in the US where if you weren't doing three all-nighters a week, not taking any holidays in your analyst years, and weren't working six, if not seven, days a week, you weren't considered 'through the boot camp' at the end. I don't think that's necessarily the right thing to do. Do you work your people really hard? I think I work my people really hard. I don't believe it's that; I think because I work really hard, I drag them. If you ask many of them, when they need time – and many times I ask them to take time – I am the first person who will never pick up the phone to call them or to ask for anything. But I do think that if we are on a journey together and we're trying to get there, and they see that I am all in, they choose to be all in. Now you could say, "Yeah, but by being all in, you're dragging them." I understand that. So, to an extent, it is my responsibility to see if some others are going too far. And I stop it. But I don't demand people to be at a certain time in the office or until another time. For me, there is a task to be done. We're all doing it together. You're doing this part, I'm doing that part. Let's get it done. Is it important for you to be liked, to an extent? I think, especially when you get into more responsible or leadership positions, it is very important to keep your feet on the ground and remind yourself every day that you're no different; you're just having a different role. But at the same time, you need to be fair to people because you wield power that they don't have, and you need to really think – which I force myself to do – "How would I feel if that was done to me?" And not dismiss it. So we talked about M&A. M&A takes a completely different meaning in that context. When you are 25 years old and you're doing a spreadsheet, it's numbers. When you are 62 and you're evaluating what you're going to do with people, it's a completely different matter. But I do think that unfortunately, life is not fair and unfortunately life is tough, and if you're leading an organization, there are decisions you need to make in the collective interest. Not taking those decisions because not everybody thinks that they are the right decision is wrong. And I do believe, and I have seen it again and again, that even the people who disagree with you prefer that, having listened to everybody, you then say, "Right, I've listened to everyone this time. This is it, and we're going in that direction." So, it's not important for you to be liked. No, not in the way you define it. Then I would love to be liked in many cases. You challenge the establishment in many ways, right? I don't know if you can say that. I would say that for me, the other important thing – and it happened to me already once – is that when I'm through with this job, I want to be in a position to look back and not have any regrets. That means asking: have I done everything I could, and what I thought was right at the time, to do the best by my organization, and did I leave a better organization as a result? Well, I think if we look at your results so far, when you became CEO five years ago, the share price was eight and today is 70, and you paid seven in dividends. So, ten times the value. Yes. So that's totally outstanding, right? Yes. But I would tell you one thing: if you look at the team – the 69,000 people at UniCredit every day – they live and breathe that. It's almost like this gave them back their pride. When they talk to clients who say, "Thank you, because I'm financing my holidays with the dividends that I'm getting from UniCredit," they're riveted to have that kind of interaction with a client. So there is an element that is not just performance; it is what that performance means in terms of achievement on both sides. Where did you learn this? Because you've been working at places like Goldman Sachs, BCG, Merrill Lynch, UBS. So tell us, where did you learn to make a bank go up ten times? I think it comes down to being very focused, if not obsessive, on what excellence looks like and how to get there, number one. Number two, being ready to listen to people who know a lot more than you do. When I arrived at UniCredit, I had worked as an adviser to commercial banks for 35, 40 years, but I'm not a commercial banker; I'm an investment banker. Most, if not all, of the good ideas that transformed UniCredit came from listening to people in the branches and in the trenches in general. So I think if you want to achieve excellence and you have the humility to listen to people, and then over time you can bring that together into what works, what doesn't work, what needs to be pulled together, and this is the strategy we're going to pursue, you will find that the process is hard, but the outcome is easier. The other very important thing is that because you went through that process, and because the ideas are by and large coming from people who feel, "This is my idea, and I'm seeing it in action," they're embracing it 150% and want to take it on the other side. So I do think these are generic qualities you need. The rest is, I tend to be a little bit obsessive. I think about banks all the time. My wife would say that on the first trip we took to Mexico, before we even went to see Chichen Itza, I took her to see a branch because they were the most modern at the time, to see how they act, how they function, because I like the industrial makeup of how it goes from the client all the way back. So, it's a number of things, but ultimately you need to love what you do. You need to have that aspiration to get it done. When you make decisions, how much is instinct or pattern recognition, and how much is analysis? I think it's both. The first thing that helps is having witnessed trading for a large period of my life. We used to say that outstanding traders are right 55% of their time – not 100%, not 90%, not 80%, but 55%. But they're very good at cutting their losses on that 45%, understanding what was wrong. They cut their losses with discipline and start again. That has always defined my view that speed is important. So yes, you need to gather as many elements as possible, but there is a point where you need to make the decision, and you will never have 100% of all the facts. Is there an element of gut feeling? Yes, by definition there is when you are making decisions on thorough but incomplete information at speed. By definition, there is an element of gut feeling, an element of, I would say, discipline and humility in saying, "I know this may not work, so let me observe it and be ready to acknowledge if I made a mistake. I'll cut it off, start again, cut my losses." It's not a decision that the boss made so it cannot be wrong, because then you dive into a situation that is not good. And I think if you do that again and again by iteration, you get to the right parameter at the right speed. When you compare how you spend your day now compared to, let's say, three and five years ago, how has it changed? I would love to tell you that it has changed. The element that has changed is that I know the people I work with a lot better. When I arrived, by definition, I was relatively detached because I was not emotionally invested; I arrived to do a job. Today, I am emotionally invested. What are the implications of that? The implication of that is both positive and negative. There are certain decisions I need to force myself to take because I know the consequences they have on people whose faces I know. I know what they do, and I know how they're going to react. Before, it was easier. At the same time, it feeds energy because it feeds in you, and at least for me, I can't let them down. We need to get that done. We can get that done. What does it take to get that done? So it gives you an energy and a determination that otherwise you don't have. You are known for spending more time on capital allocation than other CEOs. Tell me about how you think about capital allocation. I would say that I'm known for a lot of things. Most of them are not true. I would say that if you asked my team, I spend most of my time on the industrial side of the business. How do the processes work? How does the technology work? How does the way of working work? Can we change the organization to make it better? Why that product rather than that solution, and so on? So I go a lot into the details. One thing is to ask people who know more than you—the classic subject matter experts on the branch, risk, or whatever. Another thing is to be oblivious and delegate everything, assuming everything is okay. So I try to understand everything that occurs. It is true, however, that one element that banks, and this comes from my advisory career, have always underestimated until recently, is that we have operational costs, and we have the cost of equity, which is also a cost. So not factoring in that my cost of capital is 9, 10, 11, or 12%, or when I started, 17%, is wrong. Therefore, in the decisions that we made, we prioritized the deployment of capital, which is a scarce commodity. It doesn't belong to us; we're entrusted by shareholders with it, so we deploy it into the places where we could extract the best return. It started with that. Then it became about making places with lower returns perform better so I could deploy capital there as well. Therefore, capital allocation became a mantra for driving a business that grows profitably. Everybody talks about growth or return. If you look at the plan we have now, that is no news. What we're saying is we want to be at the optimal intersection of growth and distribution. To do that, you need to maximize profitability, and therefore, you need to seek what we call 'quality growth.' This is growth that defends margins and ventures into areas where competitors don't go for various reasons: perhaps it's too complicated, they lack the infrastructure or knowledge, or it's too painful to create value. From a financial standpoint, we look at our cost-income ratio in that area – how efficient we are – and secondly, how much capital we are using and whether that capital is being rewarded above its cost. We look at that and we rank the businesses. But then we go back on those businesses and we say, how do we run them? Can we change? It's like building a car. Every process is a step-by-step procedure. Can we change that and automate it? People tend to automate; however, first you need to redesign the process, then you can automate and use AI. How have you redesigned the corporate culture? This is a good question because I believe that culture is the lynchpin of everything. With the right culture, you're eventually going to win. So when you came in the first day and you sat down and talked to people, what part of the corporate culture did you think, "Wow, I think we need to change this?" I would say this: changing a corporate culture completely is very difficult and takes an extremely long period of time. Eventually, how long does it take? Dramatically changing it takes more than a decade, because inevitably, to change it, you need to replace people who are not aligned with that culture. If you have 80,000 people and most of them are not, it will take a very long time. So I don't think you can impose your culture. The way I look at it is you better go into a place where your principles, values, and culture match, maybe not 100%, but to a large extent. Otherwise, you're going to have a very difficult time. What I found in UniCredit—I knew them from before—was that they always struck me as living and breathing UniCredit; they adore it. As we say in Italian, they 'wear the UniCredit t-shirt' whether they are at work or not. For them, it's an element of pride to be part of that team. Not many institutions are like that, especially coming from investment banking. I have people who have been there 20, 25, 30 years, and they love having been there for all that time. Secondly, they used to be the team to beat in the early 2000s, and they were accustomed to that. They were fast, innovative, and aggressive. Perhaps with the benefit of hindsight, it's always easy to say they weren't constrained in a way that prevented that aggression and speed from creating problems. But the DNA was a winning one. And I felt, looking from the outside, that despite the retrenchment they had to suffer – and to a certain extent, what I would call almost humiliation, going from the top to the bottom – they still had that spirit within them. It was just a question of putting them back in a position to achieve. So, rather than changing the culture, what I did when I arrived was spend nine months, with a small team, interviewing directly and indirectly about 20,000 to 30,000 of our 80,000 people. We tried to determine what principles and values made them tick. Integrity, for example, came up strongly. Many people—well, an organization that doesn't talk about integrity is unlikely to succeed. Secondly, ownership, for a commercial bank, is quite significant. And thirdly, care. Coming from investment banking, I didn't expect 'care' to be there. It was the most voted tenet they wanted to have. You mean, because investment banks don't care? Well, because 'care' means caring about your colleagues, your clients, and many things that have to do with the organization. Investment banks are more individualistic than a commercial bank like this one was. On those pillars, we started by asking: 'Do we all agree with this vision? Do we all agree with this strategy? Do we all agree with this plan?' Yes. 'Do you feel passionately about it?' Yes. Then we empowered them. We had a very top-down command-and-control structure; now we are probably one of the most empowered bottom-up organizations. But to do that, they need to believe in the vision, the strategy, and the plan. They need to be structured around KPIs that they believe in and can understand, and share a common culture. Fundamentally, I talked a lot about the subject matter experts in the branches, in risk, and so on; they are much better at doing their jobs than I am. However, I need to understand that when they make a decision, they will have in mind the same concerns, principles, and values that I have. Because they may be very good, but if they go in the wrong direction, how do you control all of that? Culture, then, is what makes you confident that 95-98% of the time, you're going to have the right outcome. Is there anybody from outside the world of banking who has inspired you? Any other discipline? Outside the world of banking. Well, I go back a long way to my childhood, to a Roman general called Scipio, who did the impossible. At 26, he was leading the Roman legions against Hannibal in Spain. Then he went and took back – I think it's called Carthage in English – and beat Hannibal with his elephants. It was all about strategy, empowerment, taking the best of individual prowess, and doing the impossible. More recently, that's how you beat the other elephants out there. So you had a unique insight into these people. Now, if you were to just list your leadership principles, the most important ones that you adhere to. A leadership principle I like a lot is a quote from Admiral McRaven, who said, "If you want to conquer the world or change the world, start by making your own bed." He added that he wouldn't mind being late for an important meeting if it meant he needed to finish making his bed. What he's saying is, first, do small things well before you have an ambition to do big things well; this applies to both small and big decisions. Secondly, it brings you back to what I said at the beginning: keeping both feet on the ground, humility, and so on. This translates to any large organization you lead: you need to listen, be part of them, and spend a lot of time with the people you lead. Understand them. Show that you're not an outsider, but one of them. Show that you value them. Explain. Listen to their questions. Get them to guide you on some questions you may have, and be humble enough to accept a challenge not as an attack on you, but as something that will make you stronger. Then, I think these things lead to realizing, as we said before, that your job is just one part of the puzzle: bringing it all together into something that your people share, identify with, and feel passionate about, and then driving them towards excellence. At the beginning, the impossibility is, "This person is asking too much of me." But then it becomes, "Wow, many times what I thought was impossible, I achieved." That gets you to re-rate yourself in a way that makes you almost walk on air. Even if you fail, you're usually ahead of people who just aim for mediocrity, not the impossible. Where does your drive come from? Who do you want to show? I think to a large extent, myself. Contrary to what people believe, I don't like public recognition. I like to think to myself, "I set an objective, it was very hard, and I got there. And I got there, and now I'm going to set another one, and I will get there." For me, it's self-satisfaction to a very large extent. Your mother worked for the UN, you're right. Her work was in Rome. Has it been important for you to make her proud or your father? Very, very important. From a young age, I was exposed to diversity in Rome, both because of school and because Rome is the only city in the world where you have embassies and consulates for the Italian state, the UN, and the Vatican. So it's full of public servants from every part of the world, and they tend to gravitate to international schools, of which the French Lycée was one. So, diversity of views, diversity of religion, diversity of belief, all while remaining good friends. That shaped me. I grew up in a generation that thought about openness, attracting people who were different by demonstrating that there was more we could do together. So I'm not coping very well with the world of fracture, separation, and distances that we have today. How do you relax? How do I relax? Today, I spend time with my wife and daughter, or with my dog, or I do sports because it clears my mind. We talked before about boxing, and while you're doing it, your mind needs to focus on your adversary, or you're going to get punched very quickly. You need to focus on what you're doing, and that forces me to completely let go of everything else around me. That, for me, is a luxury I don't get to enjoy very often. So, physical exercise or endeavors where I need to focus. I love skiing outdoors with my family, both on water or on snow. That's what I do. I don't do much more. What do you read? I read from time to time. The last book I recently reread, after having read it a long time ago... There is a book by a Spanish author actually called Poster Gillio, who wrote this book about the cursed relegence, which is CP invasion of Africa. Obviously, it's a story as well as historical facts. It's very good from an historical standpoint, but it's a story and it's good to get my mind away from everything else and get absorbed in something completely different. What is your advice to young people? That's always very difficult, advice to young people. I would be generic. I would say number one, take the time to choose the journey, the job, the trajectory that you love, that motivates you, that gets you there. I remember that being in investment banking, which was a choice, as you remember, that I did because I loved it. People were asking me the first question, "When will I make my first million dollars?" And my first answer was if that is why you chose it, it's very, very hard to get to that point because you need to go through boot camp. There is no money that is going to compensate for that if you don't like it. So number one, make sure it is not what your friends like or what your parents like. Make sure the choice is your choice and you really like what you do. The second thing is choose to do it in an organization and with people where you identify. When we're all young, we think that a bank is a bank, a company is a company. Banks and companies are very, very different. I worked at Goldman Sachs. I worked at Merrill Lynch. Arguably, many, many people would tell you Goldman Sachs is a materially better company than Merrill Lynch was. One went almost bankrupt, the other one didn't. But for my character, Merrill Lynch was better because I thrived in that environment and at Goldman I didn't. That does not mean Goldman is not an exceptional firm and my best friends are still there. Fantastic. But where you do it is very important because that kind of company will attract people with whom you have something in common or not. If you have those two things, then the rest is going to take care of themselves. The other thing is to have an idea of the direction where you want to go, but don't think it's going to happen the way you think it's going to happen because careers are not straight lines; they're jigsaws. Actually, failures are probably as important, if not much more important because of what we were saying about grit, than the wins. You are satisfied with a win, but you don't learn anything from wins. From failures, you learn a lot about yourself and about what to do better. So if you have those things, then I would offer a more general recommendation: I hope that the generation coming into the ranks today doesn't take no for an answer and does what today seems impossible, which is getting the world to go back to a center from which we are currently moving away. I think as a generation, if we don't do something, we are leaving our children in a situation that is not as good as the one we found when we were their age. Well Andrea, it's very clear that you know where you're going, that you don't take no for an answer, that you love what you do and you do it with people. It's been great. Thank you very much.
